10 min
The Three Components of a Proposition
Author outlines the three components and copula debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of any proposition and analyze the differing views on the copula in simple vs compound propositions.

Core Idea
Every proposition has a subject, predicate, and linking relation—but scholars disagreed on the copula's role.

In this passage, the author breaks down the proposition into its essential parts. While all agree on three components, a key debate arises: do simple existential propositions like 'Zayd exists' contain an explicit copula? Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurately analyzing propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
The author states that every proposition, whether simple or compound, is completed by three things: subject, predicate, and a complete reporting relation. Underline these in the Arabic.
The relation is described as 'tamma ikhbariyya' (complete reporting relation).
2
Understand the two types of propositions
Simple existential (haliyya basita) like 'Zayd exists' vs compound attributive (haliyya murakkaba) like 'Zayd is a writer'. Note the difference: the simple predicates existence directly, the compound predicates an attribute.
3
Analyze the three opinions on the copula
The passage lists three scholarly views: (1) simple lacks a copula, compound has it; (2) simple's predicate implies the copula; (3) compound contains an extra relational existence. Read each opinion carefully.
The author critiques the third view as verbose and asserts both types need the linking relation equally.
4
Apply to Arabic examples
Take 'Zayd exists' and 'Zayd is a writer'. For each, determine which of the three opinions would best describe its structure. Use the author's own examples.
Remember that in Arabic, the copula is often omitted in nominal sentences, but logically it is still present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْقَضِيَّةُ)، سواء كانت هَلِيَّات بسيطة أو مركبة (إِنَّمَا تَتِمُّ بِأُمُورٍ ثَلَاثَةِ): أولها الموضوع وثانيها المحمول وثَالِتُهَا نِسْبَةٌ [تَامَّةٌ إِخْبَارِيَّةٌ حَايَةٌ) ، لا أزيد ولا أنقص.
واعلم أنهم اختلفوا، فذهب بعضهم إلى أن الهليات البسيطة غير مشتملة على الرابط ، بخلاف المركبة ؛ فإن العجم لا يذكرون الرابط في ترجمة زید موجود، ويقولون زيد هست، بخلاف المركبة وذهب بعضهم إلى أن
·
محمول الهليات البسيطة متضمنة للرابط ، بخلاف المركبة، واستدل بأن مفاد زید موجود وجود زید، بخلاف زيد كاتب ؛ إذ مفاده وجود الكتابة لزيد، فالوجود بنفسه متضمن للربط .
وذهب بعضهم إلى أن الهليات المركبة مشتملة على الوجود والعدم الرابطيين، سوى النسبة التامة ؛ فإن معنى زيد قائم زيد وجد على صفة القيام، بخلاف البسيطة ؛ إذ ليس معنى زيد موجود زيد يوجد الوجود، واختاره صاحب الأفق المبين، وبينه بعبارات مطنبة غير محصلة المعنى، والضرورة شاهدة بأن البسيطة والمركبة متساويتان في درجة الحكاية في الاحتياج إلى النسبة الرابطة ، وعدم الفاقة إلى أمر زائد عليها.
،
وههنا فائدة جليلة يجب الاطلاع عليها قالوا: إن الهليات البسيطة والمركبة مفترقتان في درجة المحكي عنه باشتمال الثانية على الوجود الرابطي دون الأولى، وفسروه بوجود الشيء في نفسه، على أنه في محل لكون ذلك الشيء من الحقائق الناعتية، ويوصف به تارة موضوعه، فيسمى العروض، كما يقال البياض موجود للجسم وعارض ،له وتارة يوصف به متعلق موضوعه، ويقال له الاتصاف، كما يقال الجسم موجود له البياض ومتصف به، وقالوا وجود الأعراض في أنفسها هو وجودها لمحالها، سوى العرض الذي هو الوجود، فمصداق زيد كاتب وجود الكتابة لزيد، ومصداق زید موجود نفس وجود زيد، ومثل هذا قالوا في العدم.
ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ لأنهم إن أرادوا أن الوجود الرابطي موجود في
Then the proposition, whether it is a simple or compound existential (haliyya), is only completed by three things: first the subject, second the predicate, and third a complete reporting relation, no more and no less. And know that they differed: some went to that simple existential propositions do not contain the copula, unlike compound ones; for the non-Arabs do not mention the copula in translating 'Zayd exists' and say 'Zayd hast', unlike the compound. And some went to that the predicate of simple existential propositions implies the copula, unlike the compound, and they argued that the meaning of 'Zayd exists' is the existence of Zayd, unlike 'Zayd is a writer' for its meaning is the existence of writing for Zayd, so existence itself implies the linkage. And some went to that compound existential propositions contain the relational existence and non-existence, besides the complete relation; for the meaning of 'Zayd is standing' is Zayd has come to be in the attribute of standing, unlike the simple; for the meaning of 'Zayd exists' is not 'Zayd comes to exist'. And the author of al-Ufuq al-Mubin chose this view and explained it with prolix expressions that do not achieve meaning, and necessity witnesses that simple and compound are equal in the degree of reporting in needing the linking relation, and lack of need for anything beyond it. And here is a valuable benefit that must be learned: they said that simple and compound existential propositions differ in the degree of the object reported, by the second including the relational existence while the first does not. And they explained it as the existence of the thing in itself, insofar as it is a locus for that thing being among attributive realities, and it is described sometimes as its subject, and it is called 'inherence' ('urud), as it is said 'whiteness exists for the body and is an accident to it', and sometimes it is described as what pertains to its subject, and it is called 'characterization' (ittisaf), as it is said 'the body has whiteness existing for it and is characterized by it'. And they said the existence of accidents in themselves is their existence for their loci, except for the accident that is existence. So the referent of 'Zayd is a writer' is the existence of writing for Zayd, and the referent of 'Zayd exists' is Zayd's own existence. And they said similar about non-existence. And it is not hidden what is in it; because if they meant that the relational existence exists in...
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the proposition (qadiyya). The author first states that every proposition, whether simple existential (haliyya basita, like 'Zayd exists') or compound attributive (haliyya murakkaba, like 'Zayd is a writer'), consists of three components: subject, predicate, and a complete reporting relation (nisba tamma ikhbariyya). He then discusses three opinions regarding the presence of the copula (rabita) in simple vs compound propositions: (1) Some say simple propositions lack a copula, citing that Persian speakers say 'Zayd hast' without an explicit linking word, unlike compound ones. (2) Others say the predicate of a simple proposition itself implies the copula, because 'Zayd exists' means 'the existence of Zayd', which inherently connects the subject to existence, unlike 'Zayd is a writer' where the copula is separate. (3) Yet others claim that compound propositions contain a relational existence (wujud rabiti) and non-existence beyond the complete relation—e.g., 'Zayd is standing' means Zayd has come to be in the state of standing, while 'Zayd exists' does not mean Zayd comes to exist. The author criticizes the third view as unnecessarily verbose and asserts that both simple and compound are equal in needing the linking relation. Finally, he presents a 'valuable benefit' about the difference in the object reported (mahki 'anhu): in compound propositions, the object includes the relational existence, while in simple ones it does not. He illustrates with the examples of 'Zayd is a writer' (referring to the existence of writing for Zayd) and 'Zayd exists' (referring to Zayd's own existence), and distinguishes between 'inherence' ('urud) and 'characterization' (ittisaf). All examples stem from Arabic language usage, a core Islamic science.

Key Terms
هَلِيَّة بسيطة
هَلِيَّة مركبة
الرابط
الوجود الرابطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three components of a proposition?
A) The subject
B) The predicate
C) The truth value
D) A complete reporting relation

Key Takeaway
All propositions share the same three-part structure; debate focuses on how the copula manifests in simple vs compound forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the proposition 'Zakat is obligatory' (al-zakat wajiba). Identify its subject, predicate, and the linking relation. Then determine which scholarly opinion on the copula best applies to it.
?
Take the simple proposition 'Allah exists' (Allah mawjud). Using the second opinion, explain why the predicate itself implies the copula. Contrast with 'Allah is merciful' (Allah rahim).

10 min
The Object Reported: Simple vs Compound Propositions
Author explains the difference in object reported and introduces 'urud and ittisaaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the object reported (mahki 'anhu) in simple vs compound propositions and apply the concepts of 'urud (inherence) and ittisaaf (characterization).

Core Idea
Compound propositions report relational existence, while simple ones report the thing's own existence.

The author presents a 'valuable benefit' that clarifies a deeper difference between simple and compound propositions: what they describe in reality. This leads to the distinction between inherence of an accident and characterization of a subject—key ideas for understanding how language maps onto ontology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the 'valuable benefit'
In the Arabic, find the sentence starting with 'Wa hahuna fa'ida jalila'. This is the core revelation of the passage.
2
Understand the difference in object reported
The simple existential (like 'Zayd exists') reports Zayd's own existence. The compound (like 'Zayd is a writer') reports the existence of writing for Zayd—this is the relational existence.
Relational existence is the existence of an attribute in the subject.
3
Distinguish 'urud and ittisaaf
'Urud (inherence) describes the attribute's side: existence of whiteness for the body. Ittisaaf (characterization) describes the subject's side: the body characterized by whiteness. Note how both refer to the same reality from different angles.
The passage uses 'body' and 'whiteness' as examples.
4
Apply to new examples
Take 'The sky is blue'. Following the pattern, state the relational existence (existence of blueness for the sky) and the two perspectives: 'urud (blueness exists for the sky) and ittisaaf (the sky is characterized by blueness).
This exercise connects logical analysis to everyday statements.

The Debate on Relational Existence in Propositions
Author critiques views on relational existence in simple vs compound propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions regarding whether simple propositions include the relational link (al-wujud al-rabati) and explain the difference between them.

Core Idea
The relational link is present in both simple and compound propositions.

In this passage, the author examines a key debate in Islamic logic: do simple existential propositions (like 'Zayd exists') contain a relational copula, or is the copula only present in compound attributive propositions? Some logicians argue that the predicate of a simple proposition itself implies the link, while others maintain that only compound propositions include an explicit relational existence. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping how propositions function in logical proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting views
Locate in the Arabic where the author presents two different scholarly positions: one saying simple propositions include the relational link (al-rabiṭ) within the predicate, and another saying only compound propositions include it. The first view is introduced with 'wa dhahaba baʿḍuhum ilā anna...' and the second with 'wa dhahaba baʿḍuhum ilā anna...'.
2
Understand the argument for the first view
The first group argues that the meaning of 'Zayd exists' is simply 'the existence of Zayd', so existence itself functions as the link. Contrast this with 'Zayd is a writer', whose meaning is 'the existence of writing for Zayd', where the link is separate. See how they claim the predicate inherently contains the copula.
3
Understand the argument for the second view
The second group claims that compound propositions (e.g., 'Zayd is standing') include both the relational existence and a complete relation (al-nisba al-tāmma). They interpret 'Zayd is standing' as 'Zayd existed upon the description of standing'. The author notes that the advocate of this view, the author of al-Ufuq al-Mubīn, explained it with 'elaborate phrases that do not yield the meaning'.
4
Recognize the author's initial critique
The author asserts that 'necessity witnesses that the simple and compound are equal in the degree of narration in needing the relational link'. This is his starting point: both require the link equally. Note the phrase 'al-ḍarūra shāhidatun bi-anna al-basīṭa wa al-murakkaba mutasāwiyatānī fī darajati al-ḥikāya fī al-iḥtiyāj ilā al-nisba al-rābiṭa'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتملة على الرابط ، بخلاف المركبة ؛ فإن العجم لا يذكرون الرابط في ترجمة زید موجود، ويقولون زيد هست، بخلاف المركبة وذهب بعضهم إلى أن محمول الهليات البسيطة متضمنة للرابط ، بخلاف المركبة، واستدل بأن مفاد زید موجود وجود زید، بخلاف زيد كاتب ؛ إذ مفاده وجود الكتابة لزيد، فالوجود بنفسه متضمن للربط . وذهب بعضهم إلى أن الهليات المركبة مشتملة على الوجود والعدم الرابطيين، سوى النسبة التامة ؛ فإن معنى زيد قائم زيد وجد على صفة القيام، بخلاف البسيطة ؛ إذ ليس معنى زيد موجود زيد يوجد الوجود، واختاره صاحب الأفق المبين، وبينه بعبارات مطنبة غير محصلة المعنى، والضرورة شاهدة بأن البسيطة والمركبة متساويتان في درجة الحكاية في الاحتياج إلى النسبة الرابطة ، وعدم الفاقة إلى أمر زائد عليها. وههنا فائدة جليلة يجب الاطلاع عليها قالوا: إن الهليات البسيطة والمركبة مفترقتان في درجة المحكي عنه باشتمال الثانية على الوجود الرابطي دون الأولى، وفسروه بوجود الشيء في نفسه، على أنه في محل لكون ذلك الشيء من الحقائق الناعتية، ويوصف به تارة موضوعه، فيسمى العروض، كما يقال البياض موجود للجسم وعارض له وتارة يوصف به متعلق موضوعه، ويقال له الاتصاف، كما يقال الجسم موجود له البياض ومتصف به، وقالوا وجود الأعراض في أنفسها هو وجودها لمحالها، سوى العرض الذي هو الوجود، فمصداق زيد كاتب وجود الكتابة لزيد، ومصداق زید موجود نفس وجود زيد، ومثل هذا قالوا في العدم. ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ لأنهم إن أرادوا أن الوجود الرابطي موجود في المركبة في درجة المحكي عنه فظاهر أنه من الانتزاعيات التي لا وجود لها في الأعيان، وإن أرادوا أن المحكي عنه صالح لانتزاع الوجود الرابطي دون البسيط، فإن أرادوا الوجود الخارجي الرابطي فمسلم أن البسيط لا يشتمل عليه، لكن المركبات التي مبادئ محمولاتها انتزاعية كذلك. وإن أرادوا نفس الوجود الرابطي الواقعي فظاهر أنه كما أن المركبة صالحة كذلك البسيط ، وكما أن وجود سائر الانتزاعيات وجوداتها في أنفسها هو بعينها وجوداتها لمحالها كذلك وجود الوجود ؛ فإن الوجود من الكليات المتكررة الأنواع. وبالجملة الفرق بين الهليات المركبة والبسيطة، وإن كان في درجة المحكي عنه عسير ، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Including the relational link, unlike the compound for the non-Arabs do not mention the relational link in the translation of 'Zayd exists', and they say 'Zayd hast', unlike the compound. And some went to that the predicate of simple haliyyat includes the relational link, unlike the compound, and they argued that the meaning of 'Zayd exists' is the existence of Zayd, unlike 'Zayd is a writer' for its meaning is the existence of writing for Zayd, so existence itself includes the linking. And some went to that the compound haliyyat include the existential and non-existential relational links, besides the complete relation for the meaning of 'Zayd is standing' is Zayd existed upon the description of standing, unlike the simple for the meaning of 'Zayd exists' is not Zayd's existing of existence, and the author of al-Ufuq al-Mubin chose this and explained it with elaborate phrases that do not yield the meaning, and necessity witnesses that the simple and compound are equal in the degree of narration in needing the relational link, and not needing anything extra beyond it. And here is a valuable benefit that must be examined. They said: The simple and compound haliyyat differ in the degree of the narrated object by the second including the relational existence without the first, and they interpreted it as the existence of the thing in itself, considering it is in a place because that thing is from the attributive realities, and it is sometimes described by its subject, called 'arud (accidence), as is said 'whiteness exists for the body and is accidental to it', and sometimes described by the related of its subject, called 'ittisaf (description), as is said 'the body exists for it whiteness and is described by it'. And they said the existence of accidents in themselves is their existence for their loci, except for the accident which is existence, so the referent of 'Zayd is a writer' is the existence of writing for Zayd, and the referent of 'Zayd exists' is the very existence of Zayd, and similar they said about non-existence. And what is in it is not hidden, because if they meant that the relational existence exists in the compound in the degree of the narrated object, then it is apparent that it is from the abstracted entities that have no existence in external objects, and if they meant that the narrated object is suitable for abstraction of the relational existence without the simple, then if they meant the external relational existence then it is conceded that the simple does not include it, but the compounds whose predicates' principles are abstracted are likewise. And if they meant the actual relational existence itself, then it is apparent that just as the compound is suitable, so is the simple, and just as the existence of other abstracted entities in themselves are precisely their existence for their loci, so is the existence of existence, for existence is among the repeated kinds of universals. In summary, the difference between the compound and simple haliyyat, even if in the degree of the narrated object, is difficult. This, and God knows best the correct.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage examines the nature of simple and compound propositions (haliyyat) and the role of the relational existence (al-wujud al-rabati) as the copula. The author begins by noting that non-Arabic speakers omit the copula in translating 'Zayd exists' (saying 'Zayd hast'), but include it in compound sentences. Some scholars argue that the predicate of a simple proposition (like 'Zayd exists') itself contains the relational link, because the meaning is simply the existence of Zayd. Others claim that compound propositions include the relational existence (or non-existence) beyond the complete relation, as in 'Zayd is standing' meaning Zayd existed on the description of standing. The author critiques these views, asserting that both simple and compound propositions are equal in requiring a relational link and that the attempts to differentiate them in the 'degree of the narrated object' (i.e., what the proposition is about) are problematic. He explains that the relational existence is an abstracted entity (i'tibari) with no external reality, and that both simple and compound propositions can refer to it. Ultimately, the distinction between them in the degree of narration is difficult. The passage uses examples from logic (e.g., 'Zayd exists' vs 'Zayd is a writer') to illustrate the debate about the copula.

الهليات المركبة
الوجود الرابطي
النسبة التامة

The Author's Critique of Distinguishing Simple and Compound Propositions
Author critiques the attempt to differentiate simple and compound haliyyat in the degree of narrated object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that simple and compound propositions are equal in needing a relational link and explain his critique of the attempt to differentiate them in the 'degree of the narrated object'.

Core Idea
The relational existence is an abstracted entity; thus both proposition types refer to it equally.

After presenting the two views on relational existence, the author delves into a deeper analysis. He critiques the idea that simple and compound propositions differ in the 'degree of the narrated object' (the level at which the proposition describes reality). He argues that the relational existence (al-wujud al-rabati) is a mere abstraction (i'tibari), not an external entity, and therefore both proposition types can be said to include it or be suitable for its abstraction. This lesson unpacks his nuanced refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position being critiqued
The author refers to a group (قالوا) who claim that simple and compound haliyyat differ in the degree of the narrated object (darajat al-mahki 'anhu): compound propositions include relational existence, while simple ones do not. They interpret relational existence as 'the existence of the thing in itself' and discuss attributes like 'arud (accidence) and 'ittisaf (description). Locate this in the Arabic.
2
Understand their distinction using examples
The group gives examples: 'Zayd is a writer' refers to 'the existence of writing for Zayd', so relational existence is part of the narrated object. 'Zayd exists' refers to 'the very existence of Zayd', so no extra relational existence. They also apply this to non-existence. The author finds this problematic.
3
Follow the author's first critique
The author argues: if they mean that relational existence actually exists in the compound in the narrated object, then it is merely an abstracted entity (min al-i'tibarayat) with no external reality. So claiming it is present in compound but not simple is misleading because abstracted entities are not real.
4
Follow the author's second critique
If they mean that the narrated object is suitable for abstraction of relational existence (i.e., we can conceive of it) in compound but not simple, then if they mean external relational existence, it is true that simple lacks it, but many compound predicates are also abstracted (e.g., 'whiteness exists for the body' is abstracted). If they mean the actual relational existence itself (as a concept), then both simple and compound are equally suitable for abstraction.
5
Understand the final analogy
The author draws an analogy: just as the existence of other abstracted entities in themselves is exactly their existence for their loci (e.g., whiteness exists in itself as it exists in the body), so too the 'existence of existence' is the same. Existence is a kind of universal that repeats. Therefore, the simple proposition's narrated object ('existence of Zayd') also allows abstraction of relational existence. The author concludes that the difference between compound and simple in the degree of narrated object is difficult (عسير).

مشتملة على الرابط ، بخلاف المركبة ؛ فإن العجم لا يذكرون الرابط في ترجمة زید موجود، ويقولون زيد هست، بخلاف المركبة وذهب بعضهم إلى أن محمول الهليات البسيطة متضمنة للرابط ، بخلاف المركبة، واستدل بأن مفاد زید موجود وجود زید، بخلاف زيد كاتب ؛ إذ مفاده وجود الكتابة لزيد، فالوجود بنفسه متضمن للربط . وذهب بعضهم إلى أن الهليات المركبة مشتملة على الوجود والعدم الرابطيين، سوى النسبة التامة ؛ فإن معنى زيد قائم زيد وجد على صفة القيام، بخلاف البسيطة ؛ إذ ليس معنى زيد موجود زيد يوجد الوجود، واختاره صاحب الأفق المبين، وبينه بعبارات مطنبة غير محصلة المعنى، والضرورة شاهدة بأن البسيطة والمركبة متساويتان في درجة الحكاية في الاحتياج إلى النسبة الرابطة ، وعدم الفاقة إلى أمر زائد عليها. وههنا فائدة جليلة يجب الاطلاع عليها قالوا: إن الهليات البسيطة والمركبة مفترقتان في درجة المحكي عنه باشتمال الثانية على الوجود الرابطي دون الأولى، وفسروه بوجود الشيء في نفسه، على أنه في محل لكون ذلك الشيء من الحقائق الناعتية، ويوصف به تارة موضوعه، فيسمى العروض، كما يقال البياض موجود للجسم وعارض له وتارة يوصف به متعلق موضوعه، ويقال له الاتصاف، كما يقال الجسم موجود له البياض ومتصف به، وقالوا وجود الأعراض في أنفسها هو وجودها لمحالها، سوى العرض الذي هو الوجود، فمصداق زيد كاتب وجود الكتابة لزيد، ومصداق زید موجود نفس وجود زيد، ومثل هذا قالوا في العدم. ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ لأنهم إن أرادوا أن الوجود الرابطي موجود في المركبة في درجة المحكي عنه فظاهر أنه من الانتزاعيات التي لا وجود لها في الأعيان، وإن أرادوا أن المحكي عنه صالح لانتزاع الوجود الرابطي دون البسيط، فإن أرادوا الوجود الخارجي الرابطي فمسلم أن البسيط لا يشتمل عليه، لكن المركبات التي مبادئ محمولاتها انتزاعية كذلك. وإن أرادوا نفس الوجود الرابطي الواقعي فظاهر أنه كما أن المركبة صالحة كذلك البسيط ، وكما أن وجود سائر الانتزاعيات وجوداتها في أنفسها هو بعينها وجوداتها لمحالها كذلك وجود الوجود ؛ فإن الوجود من الكليات المتكررة الأنواع. وبالجملة الفرق بين الهليات المركبة والبسيطة، وإن كان في درجة المحكي عنه عسير ، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.

Opinion: Simple Assent to the Weighted Side
Author proves opinion is simple assent and refutes the Imam's composite view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why opinion (zann) is a simple assent to the weighted side alone and refute the alternative composite view using the tripartite proposition analysis.

Core Idea
Opinion is not composite; it is a simple assent to the stronger side only.

In this passage, the commentator argues that opinion (zann) is a simple act of assent directed only at the weighted side of a probability, not a combination of weighted and unweighted. He refutes the view attributed to Imam al-Razi that opinion is the sum of both. By understanding the structure of a proposition and the impossibility of a single relation being both weighted and unweighted, you will see why the composite view leads to absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parts of a proposition
The author starts from the fact that a proposition has three parts: subject, predicate, and the relation (nisba) linking them. This is the foundation for the argument.
2
Understand the two competing definitions of opinion
The author claims opinion is simple assent to the weighted side only. The competing view (attributed to Imam al-Razi) says it is a composite of weighted and unweighted sides.
The weighted side is the stronger, the unweighted the weaker.
3
Follow the argument against the composite view
If opinion included both sides, each side would require its own relation (since one relation cannot be both weighted and unweighted). This would add a fourth part to the proposition, contradicting the three-part structure.
The author systematically rejects the logical possibilities: both relations inside, both outside, or one in one out.
4
Consider the objection from later scholars
Later scholars who adopt a four-part proposition might object that this would make five parts. The author replies that they can assign the weighted side to the complete relation and the unweighted to the restrictive relation, avoiding redundancy.
This shows the argument is adaptable to different frameworks.
5
Extend the ruling to wahm and shakk
The author emphasizes that imagination (wahm) and doubt (shakk) are also simple, non-composite qualities. This generalizes the principle: all epistemic qualities lack composition.
This reinforces the simplicity of assent across different cognitive states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ هَهُنَا، أَيْ مِنْ أَجْلِ أَنَّ الْقَضِيَّةَ تَتِمُّ بِأُمُورٍ ثَلَاثَةٍ، يَسْتَبِينُ أَنَّ الظَّنَّ إِذْعَانُ بَسِيطٌ، وَهُوَ الطَّرَفُ الرَّاجِحُ فَقَطْ، لَا كَمَا زَعَمَ الْإِمَامُ أَنَّهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ مَجْمُوعِ الرَّاجِحِ وَالْمَرْجُوحِ، وَإِلَّا لَصَارَ أَجْزَاءُ الْقَضِيَّةِ هُنَاكَ أَرْبَعَةً؛ لِأَنَّ الرَّاجِحَ وَالْمَرْجُوحَ لَا بُدَّ لَهُمَا مِنْ نِسْبَتَيْنِ؛ ضَرُورَةَ اسْتِحَالَةِ كَوْنِ النِّسْبَةِ الْوَاحِدَةِ رَاجِحَةً وَمَرْجُوحَةً، فَإِمَّا كِلْتَاهُمَا دَاخِلَتَانِ، فَثَبَتَ الْمُلَازَمَةُ، أَوْ خَارِجَتَانِ، أَوْ أَحَدُهُمَا دَاخِلَةٌ وَالْأُخْرَى خَارِجَةٌ، فَيَلْزَمُ تَعَلُّقُ الْإِذْعَانِ خَارِجَ الْقَضِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ خِلَافُ الْمُقَرَّرِ. وَلَا يَلْزَمُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ كَوْنُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ خَمْسَةً؛ لِأَنَّ لَهُمْ أَنْ يَقُولُوا الطَّرَفُ الرَّاجِحُ يَتَعَلَّقُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ التَّامَّةِ وَالْمَرْجُوحُ بِالتَّقْيِيدِيَّةِ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى تَجْوِيزِهِمْ تَعَلُّقَ الْوَهْمِ بِهَا. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ هَذَا الْحُكْمَ غَيْرُ مُخْتَصٍّ بِالظَّنِّ فَقَطْ، بَلِ الْوَهْمُ وَالشَّكُّ كَذَلِكَ؛ فَإِنَّ الْكَيْفِيَّاتِ لَا تَرْكِيبَ فِيهَا أَصْلًا. هَذَا، وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِالصَّوَابِ.
From here, i.e. because the proposition is complete with three things, it becomes clear that opinion is a simple assent, which is only the weighted side, not as the Imam claimed that it is an expression of the composite of the weighted and the unweighted. Otherwise the parts of the proposition there would become four; because the weighted and the unweighted must have two relations, due to the impossibility of a single relation being both weighted and unweighted. So either both are included, then the entailment is established, or both are excluded, or one is included and the other excluded, then the attachment of assent would be outside the proposition, contrary to what is established. And it does not follow, according to the doctrine of the later scholars, that the parts of the proposition become five; because they can say that the weighted side is attached to the complete relation and the unweighted to the restrictive relation, based on their permitting the attachment of imagination to it. Know that this ruling is not specific to opinion only, but rather imagination and doubt are likewise; for the qualities have no composition at all. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn statement that the proposition is complete with three parts (subject, predicate, relation). The commentator argues that opinion (zann) is a simple assent to one side (the weighted side) only, not a composite of weighted and unweighted. If it were composite, the proposition would have four parts: subject, predicate, and two relations (one for each side). But it is impossible for one relation to be both weighted and unweighted. The author refutes the Imam's view (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) that opinion is the sum of both sides. He considers four scenarios: both relations inside, both outside, or one inside one outside. The only consistent possibility is both inside, leading to four parts, which contradicts the three-part view. Then he anticipates an objection: on the later scholars' view (who adopt a four-part proposition), this would make five parts. He answers that later scholars can claim the weighted side relates to the complete relation and the unweighted to the restrictive relation, so no redundancy. Finally, he extends the ruling: imagination (wahm) and doubt (shakk) also lack composition; they are simple qualities. This is a purely epistemological argument about the nature of assent.

Key Terms
الظن (al-zann): A preponderant belief (rajih) with possibility of the opposite; here defined as simple assent to the weighted side alone. [Note: In earlier logic, zann is a type of tasdiq; later muta'akhkhirun debated its composition.]
الإذعان (al-idh'an): Assent or acceptance; here used as a simple mental act. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
الراجح (al-rajih): The stronger side in a probable judgment, the object of opinion.
المرجوح (al-marjuh): The weaker side, not assented to in opinion.
النسبة (al-nisba): The relation or copula linking subject and predicate in a proposition. [Note: In the later tradition, this is split into restrictive and complete relations.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why would opinion being composite lead to four parts in the proposition?
A) Because the weighted and unweighted sides require two distinct relations, making a total of four components.
B) Because the proposition always has four parts according to all scholars.
C) Because the Imam said so.
D) Because opinion is a type of doubt.

Key Takeaway
Opinion is a simple assent to the weighted side alone, not a composite; this follows from the tripartite structure of the proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a real fiqh scenario: a mujtahid considers two hadiths regarding a ruling. He finds one stronger. Explain why his resulting opinion (zann) is a simple assent to that stronger evidence only, not a combination of both.
?
Consider the definition of 'doubt' (shakk). Using the author's reasoning, argue that doubt is also simple: it is an equal inclination, not a composite of two equal beliefs.

Critique of the Four-Part Proposition
Author critiques later logicians' view on proposition parts and doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the restrictive relation cannot be the object of doubt and reconstruct the author's argument against the four-part proposition.

Core Idea
Doubt requires a report of occurrence; the restrictive relation is not a report.

Later logicians (muta'akhkhirun) introduced a fourth part to the proposition: the 'restrictive relation' (al-nisba al-taqyidiyya), claiming that doubt attaches to it. The author finds this surprising and argues that hesitation (taraddud) necessarily concerns a report (hikaya) about whether something occurs. In this lesson, we will unpack the author's reasoning and see why he defends the earlier three-part view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the later logicians' claim
The later scholars claimed the proposition is complete with four things: subject, predicate, restrictive relation, and complete relation. They said doubt is attached to the restrictive relation, which they call the 'in-between relation' (nisba bayna bayna).
The restrictive relation is the connection between subject and predicate considered without asserting actual occurrence.
2
Understand the nature of doubt
The author defines doubt (or hesitation, taraddud) as 'deeming possible the correspondence of the report (hikaya) to what it reports about' (tajwiz mutabaqat al-hikaya lima hiya hikaya 'anhu). This means doubt is about the truth of a report concerning occurrence or non-occurrence.
Doubt is not doubt about a mere relation; it is doubt about whether the report matches reality.
3
Examine the author's critique
The author asks: how can hesitation be attached to the restrictive relation, which is not a report? The restrictive relation, even considered as occurring or not, is not a report (hikaya). Therefore, it cannot be the object of doubt. The actual object of both certainty and doubt is occurrence (wuqu') or non-occurrence (la-wuqu').
The difference between certainty and doubt is only in the mode of perception, not in the object.
4
Grasp the resolution about the whole
The passage raises an objection: if in doubt the three known things (subject, predicate, relation) are present, then the whole proposition should be realized, but according to the well-known view it is not. The resolution is that in doubt, the proposition is an 'accidental whole' (kull bi al-'arad), i.e., its parts are present but not as a unified judgment.
This preserves the earlier view that the proposition has three parts and that doubt concerns occurrence.

وَالْمُتَأَخِّرُونَ زَعَمُوا أَنَّ الْقَضِيَّةَ تَتِمُّ بِأُمُورٍ أَرْبَعَةٍ، ثَالِثُهَا التَّقْيِيدِيَّةُ، وَرَابِعُهَا التَّامَّةُ، وَقَالُوا: إِنَّ الشَّكَّ مُتَعَلَّقٌ بِالنِّسْبَةِ التَّقْيِيدِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ مَوْرِدُ الْحُكْمِ، وَيُسَمُّونَهَا النِّسْبَةَ بَيْنَ بَيْنَ، وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ بِمَعْنَى الْوُقُوعِ، أَيِ النِّسْبَةُ التَّامَّةُ الْإِيجَابِيَّةُ، وَاللَّاوُقُوعِ، أَيِ السَّلْبِيَّةُ، فَلَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ إِلَّا التَّصْدِيقُ. قَالَ بَعْضُ أَجِلَّةِ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ لَعَلَّ ذَلِكَ مَنْشَأُ قَوْلِهِمْ بِتَرْبِيعِ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّهُمْ لَمَّا رَأَوُا الشَّكَّ وَالْإِذْعَانَ مُتَعَلِّقَيْنِ بِقَضِيَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَكَانَ رَأْيُهُمْ أَنَّ الِاخْتِلَافَ بَيْنَهُمَا بِالْمُتَعَلِّقِ فَقَطْ قَالُوا بِتَرْبِيعِ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ. أَعْجَبَنِي قَوْلُهُمْ، أَمَا فَهِمُوا أَنَّ التَّرَدُّدَ لَا يَتَقَوَّمُ حَقِيقَةً مَا لَمْ يَتَعَلَّقْ بِالْوُقُوعِ الَّذِي هُوَ حِكَايَةٌ؟ فَإِنَّ الشَّيْءَ مَا لَمْ يَصِرْ حِكَايَةً، كَيْفَ يَتَقَوَّمُ بِهِ التَّرَدُّدُ؟ فَإِنَّهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ تَجْوِيزِ مُطَابَقَةِ الْحِكَايَةِ لِمَا هِيَ حِكَايَةٌ عَنْهُ، وَالنِّسْبَةُ التَّقْيِيدِيَّةُ وَإِنِ اعْتُبِرَتْ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهَا وَاقِعَةٌ أَوْ لَيْسَتْ بِوَاقِعَةٍ، بِأَنْ يَكُونَ الْوُقُوعُ خَارِجًا لِعَدَمِ كَوْنِهَا حِكَايَةً غَيْرَ صَالِحَةٍ لِتَعَلُّقِ التَّرَدُّدِ، وَأَمَّا مَجْمُوعُهُمَا فَلَا دَخْلَ لِلتَّقْيِيدِيَّةِ فِيهِ، بَلِ الْوُقُوعُ كَافٍ، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى عَلَى مَنْ لَهُ أَدْنَى فَهْمٍ. فَالْمُدْرَكُ فِي الصُّورَتَيْنِ وَاحِدٌ، وَهُوَ الْوُقُوعُ وَاللَّاوُقُوعُ، وَالتَّفَاوُتُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الْإِدْرَاكِ بِأَنَّهُ إِذْعَانِيٌّ أَوْ تَرَدُّدِيٌّ، أَيْ إِدْرَاكٌ مُتَحَقِّقٌ فِي ضِمْنِهِ. فَقَوْلُ الْقُدَمَاءِ هُوَ الْحَقُّ. وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ الثَّلَاثَةَ الَّتِي هِيَ جَمِيعُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مُتَحَقِّقَةٌ فِي صُورَةِ الشَّكِّ وَالْوَهْمِ وَالتَّخَيُّلِ، مَعَ أَنَّهَا غَيْرُ مُتَحَقِّقَةٍ عَلَى مَا هُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ، فَيَلْزَمُ انْفِكَاكُ الْكُلِّ عَنْ جَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهِ الَّذِي هُوَ الْكُلُّ. قِيلَ فِي حَلِّهِ: إِنَّ الْقَضِيَّةَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى تِلْكَ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ كُلٌّ بِالْعَرَضِ.
The later scholars claimed that the proposition is complete with four things, the third of which is the restrictive relation, and the fourth the complete relation, and they said: doubt is attached to the restrictive relation, and it is the locus of judgment, and they call it the in-between relation. As for judgment in the sense of occurrence, i.e. the complete affirmative relation, and non-occurrence, i.e. the negative, only assent is attached to it. Some eminent later scholars said: perhaps that is the origin of their statement with the four parts of the proposition; for when they saw doubt and assent attached to a single proposition, and their view was that the difference between them is only in the object of attachment, they said with the four parts of the proposition. I am amazed at their statement: did they not understand that hesitation is not constituted as a reality unless it is attached to occurrence which is a report? For something, as long as it has not become a report, how can hesitation be constituted by it? For it is an expression of deeming possible the correspondence of the report to what it is a report about. The restrictive relation, even if considered from the aspect that it is occurring or not occurring, such that occurrence is external due to its not being a report, is not suitable for the attachment of hesitation. As for their composite, the restrictive has no part in it; rather occurrence suffices, as is not hidden from anyone who has the least understanding. So the perceived in the two cases is one: it is occurrence and non-occurrence, and the difference is only in the perception, whether it is assenting or hesitating, i.e. a perception realized within it. So the statement of the early scholars is the truth. And here there is a doubt: that the three known things which are all the parts of the proposition are realized in the case of doubt, imagination, and illusion, while they are not realized according to what is well-known, so it follows that the whole is separated from all its parts which are the whole. It was said in its resolution: the proposition, in relation to those known things, is a whole accidentally.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the later logicians' (muta'akhkhirun) doctrine that the proposition has four parts: subject, predicate, restrictive relation, and complete relation. They claim that doubt pertains to the restrictive relation (the 'in-between' relation), while certainty (tasdiq) pertains to the complete relation (occurrence or non-occurrence). The author finds this surprising and criticizes it. He argues that hesitation (taraddud) essentially involves a report (hikaya) about occurrence—i.e., one doubts whether a certain state of affairs is the case. The restrictive relation, on its own, is not a report; it is merely a connection considered as occurring or not, but it does not assert anything. Thus, it cannot be the object of doubt. The actual object of both certainty and doubt is the same: occurrence (wuqu') or non-occurrence (la-wuqu'). The difference lies only in the mode of perception: in certainty it is firm assent (idh'ani), in doubt it is hesitant (taraddudi). Therefore, the earlier scholars (qudama) who held that the proposition has three parts and that doubt is about the same occurrence are correct. The passage then raises an objection (shakk): if doubt involves the same three components (subject, predicate, relation), then the whole proposition should be realized in doubt, but according to the well-known view it is not, leading to a contradiction (the whole separated from its parts). The answer is that in doubt, the proposition is a whole only accidentally (kull bi al-'arad), meaning its parts are present but not as a unified judgment. This resolution preserves the earlier view.

النسبة التامة

D) Because the complete relation is the only one that exists.

Key Takeaway
The object of doubt is the same as the object of certainty: occurrence. The later logicians' four-part view overcomplicates the proposition and mislocates the object of doubt.

Later Logicians' Four-Part Proposition and Critique
Author critiques later logicians' four-part proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the later logicians' four-part proposition is invalid and identify the correct earlier view.

Core Idea
Doubt attaches to occurrence, not to a relative relation that is not a report.

The later logicians proposed that a proposition has four parts, adding a 'relative relation' (nisba taqyidiyya) as the locus of doubt. In this passage, the author dismantles their reasoning, arguing that hesitation concerns whether a report matches reality, and the relative relation is not a report. Understanding this clarifies the structure of propositions and the nature of assent and doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the later logicians' view
Find the claim that the proposition is completed by four things, with the third being the relative relation (nisba taqyidiyya) and the fourth the complete relation. They say doubt attaches to the relative relation.
2
Locate the author's objection
The author says hesitation is not constituted unless it attaches to occurrence (wudu'), which is a report (hikaya). The relative relation is not a report, so it cannot be the object of doubt.
3
Understand the author's alternative
The earlier view: the perceived in both conviction and hesitation is the occurrence/non-occurrence; the difference is only in the mode of perception (conviction vs. hesitation).
4
Evaluate the argument's strength
The critique hinges on the nature of doubt: it requires a report that can conform or not. Since the relative relation is not a report, the later view collapses.
This shows how Islamic logicians critique doctrines on epistemological grounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُتَأَخَّرُونَ زَعَمُوا أَنَّ القضية تتم بأمور أربعة، ثالثها التقييدية، ورابعها التامة ، وقالوا: إن الشَّكَ مُتَعَلَّقُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ التَّقَيُّدِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ مَوْرِدُ الْحُكْمِ، وَيُسَمُّونَهَا النِّسْبَةَ بَيْنَ بَيْنَ، وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ بِمَعْنَى الْوُقُوعِ)، أي النسبة التامة الإيجابية، (وَاللَّاوُقُوعِ)، أي السلبية (فَلَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ إِلَّا التَّصْدِيقُ)، قال بعض أجلة المتأخرين لعل ذلك منشأ قولهم بتربيع أجزاء القضية ؛ فإنهم لما رأوا الشك والإذعان متعلقين بقضية واحدة وكان رأيهم أن الاختلاف بينهما بالمتعلق فحسب قالوا بتربيع أجزاء القضية. (أَعْجَبَنِي قَوْلُهُمْ، أَمَا فَهِمُوا أَنَّ التَّرَدُّدَ لاَ يَتَقَوَّمُ حَقِيقَةٌ مَا لَمْ يَتَعَلَّقُ بِالْوُقُوع) الذي هو حكاية؛ فإن الشيء ما لم يصر حكاية، كيف يتقوم به التردد ؛ فإنه عبارة عن تجويز مطابقة الحكاية لما هي حكاية عنه، والنسبة التقييدية وإن اعتبرت من حيث إنها واقعة أو ليست بواقعة، بأن يكون الوقوع خارجا لعدم كونها حكاية غير صالحة لتعلق التردد ، وأما مجموعهما فلا دخل للتقييدية فيه، بل الوقوع كاف ، كما لا يخفى على من له أدنى فهم، هذا ، والله أعلم بالصواب. (فَالْمُدْرَكُ في الصُّورَتَيْنِ وَاحِدٌ)، وهو الوقوع واللاوقوع، (وَالتَّفَاوُتُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الْإِدْرَاكِ بِأَنَّهُ إِذْعَانِيُّ أَوْ تَرَدُّدِيٌّ، أي إدراك متحقق في ضمنه، (فَقَوْلُ الْقُدَمَاءِ هُوَ الْحَقُّ).
And the later logicians claimed that the proposition is completed by four things, the third of which is the relative relation, and the fourth the complete relation. They said: doubt is attached to the relative relation, which is the locus of judgment, and they call it the relation between-between. As for judgment in the sense of occurrence—that is, the affirmative complete relation—and non-occurrence—that is, the negative—only assent attaches to it. Some great later scholars said that perhaps this is the origin of their statement about the fourfold division of the parts of the proposition; for when they saw that doubt and conviction are both attached to a single proposition and they held that the difference between them is only in respect of the object, they said with the fourfold division of the parts of the proposition. (What astonishes me is their statement: did they not understand that hesitation is not constituted as a reality unless it attaches to occurrence) which is a report? For if something is not a report, how can hesitation be constituted by it? For it is an expression for deeming the conformity of the report to what it reports about. And the relative relation, even if considered in terms of its being occurring or not occurring—such that the occurrence is external to it because it is not a report—is not suitable for the attachment of hesitation. And as for their combination, the relative relation has no role in it; rather, the occurrence suffices. This is as clear as can be to anyone with the least understanding. This, and God knows best what is correct. (So the perceived in the two forms is one), namely occurrence and non-occurrence, (and the difference is only in the perception, in that it is either a perception of conviction or a perception of hesitation, i.e., a perception in which there is, within it, a state of being certain or hesitant). (So the statement of the earlier scholars is the truth).
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage critiques the later logicians' view that the proposition (qadiyya) has four parts, introducing a relative relation (nisba taqyidiyya) intermediate between subject and predicate. The author argues that doubt (shakk) must attach to the occurrence (wudu') itself, not to the relative relation, because hesitation concerns whether a report matches reality. The relative relation is not a report, so it cannot be the object of doubt. Therefore, the earlier view, that the perceived is the occurrence/non-occurrence and the difference is only in the mode of perception (conviction vs. hesitation), is correct.

The Dialectical Critique of the Accidental Whole Solution
Dialectical exchange on accidental whole and proposition unity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the dialectical exchange in this passage and explain why the accidental whole solution fails to account for the immediate unity of the proposition.

Core Idea
The accidental whole solution for propositions collapses because it requires an external perception of occurrence.

In this passage, the commentator engages with a doubt about the three parts of a proposition being present in doubt and imagination. The solution offered is that the proposition is an accidental whole relative to those parts. However, the commentator shows that this introduces an extra factor—perception of occurrence—which is not part of the proposition itself. Follow the back-and-forth to see how the solution is dismantled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt (shakk)
The doubt states that the three known things (parts of a proposition) are present in doubt/imagination, yet the proposition itself is not realised according to the famous view. This would mean the whole is separate from its parts.
2
Understand the proposed solution (qila)
The solution claims the proposition is an accidental whole relative to the three parts, so no entailment of the whole's realisation. It depends on a medium (wasta) either in subsistence or inherence.
Example of writer and rational animal illustrates accidental whole.
3
Examine the commentator's objection (aqulu)
If it is accidental whole, another thing must be considered—perception of occurrence (idrak al-wudu')—which is external to the proposition. This shows the solution fails to keep the proposition self-contained.
4
Analyze the counter-objection and response
A conceiver suggests the condition could be a mere qualification, like pieces of wood arranged as a bed. The commentator rejects this because it would make the proposition something externally imposed; the known things would not be the proposition themselves.
5
Evaluate the outcome
The accidental whole solution is shown to be problematic. The passage ends with a further objection, leaving the debate unresolved, but the critique stands: the proposition's unity must be essential, not accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ الثَّلاثَةَ الَّتِي هِيَ جَمِيعُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مُتَحَقِّقَةٌ فِي صُورَةِ الشَّكِّ) والوهم والتخيل، (مَعَ أَنَّهَا غَيْرُ مُتَحَقِّقَةٍ عَلَى مَا هُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ)، فيلزم انفكاك الكل عن جميع أجزائه الذي هو الكل . (قيل في حَلِّهِ: إِنَّ الْقَضِيَّةَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى تِلْكَ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ كُلُّ بِالْعَرَضِ)، أي بواسطة الغير واسطة في الثبوت، بأن يجعل الواسطة تلك المعلومات كُلاً، أو واسطة في العروض، بأن يكون الكل تلك الواسطة بالذات، وهو المنعقد . العقد وإنما ينسب الكلية إلى القضية لاتحادها معه بالعرض، (فَلاَ يَلْزَمُ تَحَقُّقُه، أي تحقق الكل الذي هو القضية، إما لعدم تحقق الواسطة الإيقاع، وإما لجواز انفكاك الكل بالعرض عن أجزائه العرضية، (كَالْكَاتِب بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ ، وهذا التمثيل يؤيد التوجيه الثاني. (أَقُولُ : [إِذَا كَانَ كُلاً بِالْعَرَضِ فَيَجِبُ أَنْ يُعْتَبَرَ) في انعقاد القضية (أَمْرُ آخَرُ بَعْدَ الْوُقُوعِ). أما على التوجيه الأول فلأنه لا بد من أخذ الواسطة، وأما على الثاني فلأن الكل بالعرض يكون لازما للكل بالذات، فانفكاكه عن المعلومات يدل على انفكاك الكل بالذات، فلا بد من اعتبار أمر آخر، (وَلَيْسَ) ذلك الأمر (إِلَّا إِدْرَاكُهُ (۱) ، أي إدراك الوقوع واللاوقوع، (وَذَلِكَ خَارِجٌ إِجْمَاعًا)، ولما كان لمتوهم أن يقول : إنه يجوز أن يكون شرطا خارجا أجاب بقوله: (وَأَخْذُ الْوُقُوع بِشَرْطِ الْإِيقَاعِ نَصْحِيحُ لِمَجْمُولِيَّةِ الذَّاتِي (٢) ؛ إذ المعلومات عين القضية، وقد جعلها الإيقاع قضية ، (وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ). واعترض عليه بأنه يجوز أن يكون قيدا للمعلومات، خارجا عن مفهوم القضية، كالسرير ؛ فإنه عبارة عن قطعات الخشب المعروضة للهيئة كذلك يكون القضية عبارة عن المعلومات المعروضة، ولا . مجعولية ذاتية. وأيضا يمكن أن يكون مراده بالكل بالعرض الكلي العرضي.
And here is a doubt: that the three known things which are all the parts of the proposition are realized in the form of doubt, imagination, and fancy, even though they are not realized according to the famous view. So it follows that the whole is separate from all its parts, which is the whole. (It was said in its solution: that the proposition relative to those known things is an accidental whole), i.e., by means of another, either as a medium in subsistence—by making the medium those known things as a whole—or as a medium in inherence—by making the whole that medium essentially—and that is the 'contract'? The contract? And the whole is only attributed to the proposition because of its accidental unity with it. (Thus its realization is not necessary, i.e., the realization of the whole which is the proposition, either because the medium of the occurrence is not realized, or because it is permissible for the accidental whole to be separate from its accidental parts, like the writer relative to the rational animal). This example supports the second interpretation. (I say: if it is an accidental whole, then another thing must be considered in the constitution of the proposition after the occurrence). According to the first interpretation, because the medium must be taken; according to the second, because the accidental whole is a necessary concomitant of the essential whole, so its separation from the known things indicates the separation of the essential whole, so another thing must be considered. (And) that thing (is nothing but its perception), i.e., the perception of occurrence and non-occurrence, (and that is external by consensus). And since a conceiver might say that it is permissible that it be an external condition, he answered with his statement: (taking occurrence with the condition of the making-occur is not valid for essential made-ness?; for the known things are identical to the proposition, and the making-occur has made them a proposition, and that is impossible). And an objection was raised against it: that it is permissible that it be a qualification of the known things, external to the concept of the proposition, like the bed; for it is an expression for the pieces of wood arranged for the configuration. Likewise, the proposition would be an expression for the known things arranged. And there is no essential made-ness. Also, it is possible that by 'accidental whole' he meant the universal accidental whole.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. First, a doubt (shakk) is raised: if the proposition consists of three parts (subject, predicate, and judgment), these parts are present in doubt and imagination, yet according to the famous view the proposition itself is not realized in those cases. This would entail the whole being separate from its parts. The solution (hall) offered is that the proposition is an accidental whole (kull bi al-'arad) relative to those three parts, meaning its realization depends on an external medium (wasta), either in subsistence or in inherence. The example of 'writer' relative to 'rational animal' illustrates: a writer is an accidental whole relative to the essential whole (human), and can be separate. The author (the commentator) then objects (aqulu) that if it is accidental whole, then an additional factor is required—perception of occurrence (idrak al-wudu')—which is not part of the proposition itself. The attempted evasion that occurrence could be a condition is rejected because the three known things are identical to the proposition, so making them a proposition by a condition is impossible. Finally, a further objection is raised: the condition could be a mere qualification, like pieces of wood arranged as a bed; the proposition could be the three known things arranged, without essential composition. Also, 'accidental whole' might mean universal accidental whole (kulli 'aradi). The passage ends mid-argument.

Key Terms
كل بالعرض
الواسطة
إيقاع
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example is given in the solution (qila) to illustrate the accidental whole?
A) The writer relative to the rational animal
B) The bed relative to the pieces of wood
C) The proposition relative to its three parts
D) The occurrence relative to doubt

Key Takeaway
The proposition's unity is essential, not accidental; treating it as accidental introduces an external perception that is not part of its meaning, undermining the solution.

The Definition of Proposition and Its Independence from Assent
Author defends the definition of proposition as subject-predicate composite regardless of assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical definition of a proposition (qadhiyya) from the speaker's psychological state of assent, using the arguments from this passage.

Core Idea
A proposition is defined by its truth-apt form, not by the speaker's conviction.

What makes a statement a proposition? Is it the speaker's belief or the statement's structure? This passage clarifies that a proposition (qadhiyya) is defined solely by its subject-predicate form and its capacity to be true or false, irrespective of whether the speaker actually assents. The author refutes an objection that would tie the proposition to the moment of judgment, showing that the logical definition stands regardless of psychological states.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the word 'قضية' (qadhiyya) in the Arabic. The passage is defending its definition against an objection.
The term is central to logic and appears frequently in Islamic sciences.
2
Extract the definition given
The author states that a proposition conveys a meaning that admits truth (صدق) and falsehood (كذب). It is composed of subject and predicate. Note that assent (إذعان) is not part of the definition.
The definition is operational: if it can be evaluated as true or false, it is a proposition.
3
Understand the objection
The passage mentions an objection: someone might argue that a proposition awaits its attainment after communication, meaning it depends on the occurrence of the judgment. The author rejects this.
The objection ties the proposition to the speaker's psychological act.
4
Follow the refutation
The author counters by quoting the matn: 'Communication is prior to occurrence.' He argues that considering the attachment of occurrence (إيقاع) as a condition or a part has no role in attaining the reality of the proposition. The proposition is defined by its form, not by whether it corresponds to reality.
Even in doubt, the utterance is still a proposition; hesitation is about correspondence, not about the proposition itself.
5
Apply to an example
Take the statement 'Zaid is standing.' According to this definition, it is a proposition whether or not the speaker believes it, because it can be true or false. The doubt is only about its correspondence to Zaid's actual state.
This separates logical form from epistemic states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية كلي عرضي لتلك المعلومات، فلا بأس بالانفكاك، وحينئذ لا عليه شيء. يرد هذا، وإن كان بعيدا لكنه مصلح لكلامه، نعم، كاف في دفعه ما أشار إليه بقوله: (وَالْإِفَادَةُ) للسكوت (مُقَدَّمٌ عَلَى الْإِيقَاعِ، وَالْقَضِيَّةُ لَيْسَتْ مُنْتَظِرَةَ التَّحْصِيلِ بَعْدَهَا) ، أي الإفادة ، (فَاعْتِبَارُ تَعَلُّقِ الْإِيقَاعِ بِالْوُقُوعِ) شرطا أو شطرا مِمَّا لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ في تَحْصِيلِ هَذِهِ الْحَقِيقَةِ)، أي حقيقة القضية، (فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدُ هُوَ قَائِمٌ قَضِيَّةٌ عَلَى كُلِّ تَقْدِيرِ) ، سواء كان مذعنا أو لا ; (فَإِنَّهُ يُقِيدُ مَعْنَى مُحْتَمِلًا لِلصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ، فَفِي صورة الشَّكْ إِنَّمَا التَّرَدُّدُ في مُطَابَقَةِ الْحِكَايَةِ للمحكي عنه، لَا فِي أَصْلِ الْحِكَايَةِ وَاحْتِمَالِهَا لَهُمَا)، أي للصدق والكذب.
The proposition is a universal accidental to those known things, so there is no harm in separation, and then there is nothing upon it. This is refuted, even if it is far-fetched, but it fixes his speech. Yes, sufficient in repelling it is what he indicated by his saying: 'The communication is prior to the occurrence, and the proposition does not await its attainment after that', i.e. the communication. 'So consideration of the attachment of the occurrence to the occurrence as a condition or a part has no role in attaining this reality', i.e. the reality of the proposition. 'So the truth is that our saying 'Zaid is standing' is a proposition in every case', whether he is convinced or not; 'for it conveys a meaning that admits truth and falsehood. So in the case of doubt, the hesitation is only in the correspondence of the narration to the narrated, not in the essence of the narration and its admitting both', i.e. truth and falsehood.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the definition of proposition (qadhiyya) against an objection. The author begins by stating that the proposition is a universal accidental to the known things (the subject and predicate), so separation (of the parts) is fine. Then he presents an objection and refutes it. He quotes the earlier matn: 'Communication is prior to occurrence, and the proposition does not await its attainment after that.' That is, the communication (the utterance) is prior to the actual event of judging. Therefore, the proposition is defined by its structure (subject-predicate) that can be true or false, regardless of whether the speaker assents or not. So 'Zaid is standing' is a proposition even if one is in doubt; the doubt is about the correspondence to reality, not about the essence of the propositional form. This emphasizes that the logical definition of proposition is a statement that can be evaluated as true or false, irrespective of the speaker's conviction.

Key Terms

إذعان
صدق
كذب

Key Takeaway
A proposition is a subject-predicate statement capable of truth or falsehood, independent of the speaker's conviction.

Assented vs. Imaginary Propositions in Logic
Author distinguishes propositions used in sciences from imaginary in poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between propositions used in the sciences (with assent) and imaginary propositions used in poetry, and explain the role of each in the five arts.

Core Idea
Poetry uses imaginary propositions; science requires assent.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key distinction within the logical classification of propositions. Not all propositions are treated equally in the Islamic sciences. While the sciences demand propositions that command assent, poetry operates on imagination. Understanding this division helps you see how logic serves different arts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the Arabic passage and locate the two types of propositions mentioned: those with 'idh'an' (assent) and those described as 'mukhayyal' (imaginary).
The passage explicitly contrasts these two.
2
Determine the role of assented propositions
The author states that propositions 'considered in the sciences' are those to which assent is attached. Why? Because doubt does not lead to perfection in knowledge.
Assent implies belief in truth, which is necessary for scientific reasoning.
3
Locate the domain of imaginary propositions
The passage says imaginary propositions have consideration and are required in 'the art of poetry' (sina'at al-shi'r). This is one of the five arts of logic.
Poetry aims at evoking emotions, not establishing truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نَعَمْ، الْقَضَايَا الْمُعْتَبَرَةُ فِي الْعُلُوم هِيَ الَّتِي تَعَلَّقَ بِهَا الْإِذْعَانُ، إِذْ لَا كَمَالَ في تَحْصِيلِ الشَّكٌ، هذا في المشكوك ، أما المخيل فله اعتبار، ويجب في صناعة الشعر من هذا الفن،
Yes, the propositions that are considered in the sciences are those to which assent is attached, for there is no perfection in attaining doubt. This concerns the doubtful [proposition]; as for the imaginary, it has consideration and is required in the art of poetry from this craft.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage clarifies a point about the types of propositions. The author states that in the sciences, only propositions that involve assent (i.e., belief in their truth) are used, because doubt does not lead to perfection in knowledge. However, the imaginary proposition (al-mukhayyal) has its own role, specifically in the art of poetry (sina'at al-shi'r), which is one of the five arts of logic. Poetry deals with imaginative statements that evoke emotions, not with truth. This distinguishes the scientific use of propositions from the poetic use.

Key Terms
إذعان (idh'an): Assent or conviction in the truth of a proposition. [Note: Standard.]
مخيل (mukhayyal): An imaginary proposition that evokes a mental image or emotion, not necessarily true or false in a scientific sense. [Note: In logic, it is specifically the domain of poetry.]

Key Takeaway
Propositions in logic are divided into assented (for sciences) and imaginary (for poetry), each serving a distinct purpose within the five arts.

Three Components of a Categorical Proposition and the Copula
Author explains the three parts of a categorical proposition and the copula in Arabic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components (subject, predicate, copula) in any categorical proposition from Islamic texts, and distinguish between explicit and implicit copula forms.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition is defined by its three-part structure: subject, predicate, and the relation that binds them.

In this lesson, we explore the internal anatomy of a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). The classical logicians insisted on three essential parts: the subject (mawdu'), the predicate (mahmul), and a relation (nisba) of affirmation or negation. The verbal expression of this relation is called the copula (rabita). In Arabic, the copula can be omitted and indicated by case endings, leading to a distinction between bipartite and tripartite propositions. Understanding these components is crucial for analyzing statements in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
In a given proposition, separate the entity being talked about (al-mawdu') from what is being attributed to it (al-mahmul). Example: in 'al-‘adlu wajibun' (justice is obligatory), 'adl is subject, wajib is predicate.
2
Identify the relation (nisba)
Determine whether the predicate is affirmed or negated of the subject. In 'al-‘adlu wajibun', the relation is affirmation (ijab). In 'al-zulmu laysa bi-mustahabb' (injustice is not recommended), the relation is negation (salab).
3
Find the copula (rabita)
Look for an explicit word indicating the relation, such as 'huwa' (e.g., 'al-‘adlu huwa wajibun'), or recognize its absence where case endings imply it. In 'al-‘adlu wajibun', the nominative case on both subject and predicate signals the affirmative relation.
The copula may be expressed by particles or implicitly by the syntactic structure (hay'a tarkibiyya).
4
Classify as bipartite or tripartite
If the copula is omitted, it is bipartite (thuna'iyya); if mentioned, it is tripartite (thulathiyya). Understand that both forms are valid in Arabic logic, but the copula is always conceptually present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا وإن كان مما لم يقرع سمعك من المنطقيين، وإن صرح الفاضل التفتازاني بكونه جملة خبرية في اصطلاح المعاني . قال الشيخ فأما الذي يجب بحسب الأمر في نفسه، فهو أن القضية الحملية تتم بأمور ثلاثة ; فإنها تتم بمعنى الموضوع والمحمول ونسبة بينهما، وليس اجتماع المعاني في الذهن هو كونها موضوعة ومحمولة، بل يحتاج إلى أن الذهن يعتقد مع ذلك النسبة التي بين المعنيين بإيجاب أو سلب، لَكِنَّهُ هُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ . ثُمَّ إِذَا كَانَتِ الْأَجْزَاءُ ثَلَاثَةٌ فَحَقُهَا أَنْ يُدَلَّ عَلَيْهَا بِثَلَاثِ عِبَارَاتٍ : فَالدَّالُ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ يُسَمَّى رابطة، وفي لغة العرب ربما حذفت الرابطة بعَلَامَاتِ إِعْرَابِيَّة دَالَةِ عَلَيْها دلالة التزامية، هذا غير ظاهر في غير الزمانية، بل اللغات، ولا الظاهر أن الهيئة التركيبية موضوعة للنسبة الساذجة، فتسمى القضية ثنائية إذا حذفت الرابطة، فإن ذكرت الرابطة فتسمى ثلاثية. والمذكور وإن كان اداة لكونه دالا على نسبة غير مستقلة، لكنه ربما كان في قالب الان
This, even if it is not something that has reached your ears from the logicians, and indeed the excellent Taftazani explicitly declared it to be an informative sentence in the terminology of meanings. The shaykh said: As for what is necessary according to the matter itself, it is that the categorical proposition is completed by three things; for it is completed by the meaning of the subject, the predicate, and a relation between them. The mere combination of the meanings in the mind is not its being subject and predicate, but the mind also needs to believe the relation between the two meanings by affirmation or negation; that is the verification. Then, since the parts are three, it is only right that they be signified by three expressions: the signifier of the relation is called the copula. In the language of the Arabs, the copula may be omitted by case endings that indicate it by implicational signification; this is not apparent in non-temporal [languages], but in languages, it is apparent that the syntactic structure is posited for the bare relation. So the proposition is called bipartite if the copula is omitted, and if the copula is mentioned, it is called tripartite. The mentioned [copula] is a tool because it signifies a relation that is not independent, but it may be in the form of 'in'?
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the structure of a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). It begins by noting that this view may not be commonly held by logicians, but al-Taftazani affirmed it as an informative sentence. Then it states that a categorical proposition consists of three things: the subject, the predicate, and the relation (nisba) between them. The relation, or copula (rabita) as it is called in logic, is essential. In Arabic, this copula can be omitted and indicated by case endings (i'rab) that imply the relation. If the copula is omitted, the proposition is called bipartite; if it is expressed (e.g., by 'huwa'), it is called tripartite. The copula is a tool because it signifies a non-independent relation. This discussion touches on the debate between logicians and grammarians about whether Arabic has an explicit copula.

Key Terms
قضية حملية
رابطة
نسبة
هيئة تركيبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two: subject and predicate
B) Three: subject, predicate, and copula (relation)
C) Four: subject, predicate, copula, and modality
D) One: the proposition itself

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition always consists of three elements: subject, predicate, and the relation (copula), which may be explicit or implicit in Arabic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Critique of the Logical Copula 'Huwa'
Author critiques logicians' use of 'huwa' as copula, defending Arabic grammar consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Arabic grammarians' objection to the logical copula 'huwa' and evaluate the strength of the counterarguments.

Core Idea
Logic cannot redefine Arabic grammar by fiat; consensus of grammarians is authoritative.

The author challenges the logicians' use of 'huwa' as a copula, asserting it contradicts the consensus of Arabic grammarians. This lesson explores the tension between logical terminology and native grammatical tradition, and why a single grammarian's opinion cannot override consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author states that counting 'huwa' as a copula contradicts the consensus of Arabic grammarians (ahl al-'Arabiyya). Recognize that the claim rests on the authority of ijma' in language.
2
Examine the logicians' defense
The passage mentions two justifications: homonymy (shared between referent and relation) and inclusion (indication by implication). Why does the author reject these? Because the people of language (ahl al-lugha) deny them.
The author implies these justifications are contrived and not accepted by native speakers.
3
Assess the authority argument
The author dismisses relying on al-Radi alone without proof. Reflect on the principle that a single scholar's view, even a renowned grammarian, cannot override a consensus (ijma') unless supported by strong evidence.
This mirrors usul al-fiqh principles where ijma' is a primary source.
4
Explore the alternative
The author suggests that derived forms from incomplete verbs (like kana) might be acceptable as non-temporal copulas. Consider why these are less objectionable: they are explicit verbs that carry temporal meaning, unlike 'huwa' which is a pronoun.
The passage is cut off, so the alternative is only hinted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the logicians' practice of treating the word 'huwa' (he/it) as a copula (rabita) in Arabic propositions. The author firmly states that this practice contradicts the consensus (ijma') of Arabic grammarians (ahl al-'Arabiyya), who are the authoritative experts in this field. He then refutes two possible justifications for this usage: that 'huwa' is homonymous between the referent and the relation, or that it indicates the relation by inclusion. Both are rejected by the people of language. The author also dismisses the reliance on the grammarian al-Radi (Radi al-Din al-Astarabadi) alone without proof, implying that a single authority's view cannot override the consensus. Finally, he suggests a possible alternative: if one were to count the derived forms from incomplete verbs (like 'kāna' and its sisters) as non-temporal copulas, that might be acceptable, but not 'huwa'. The passage reflects the tension between logical terminology and Arabic grammar, defending the primacy of the native grammatical tradition.

Non-Temporal vs Temporal Copula
Defines the non-temporal and temporal copula with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the non-temporal and temporal copula in Arabic logical propositions and recognize examples of each.

Core Idea
The copula either links timelessly (non-temporal) or indicates time (temporal); 'kāna' is the key temporal copula.

In the previous discussion of assents (tasdīqāt), the logicians clarified that the copula (rābiṭa) does not denote an event. This leads to a key distinction: a copula can be non-temporal, simply connecting subject and predicate without time, or temporal, explicitly indicating time. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly parsing propositions in Islamic logic and grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula in a given proposition
Look for the element that links the subject (mubtada') and predicate (khabar). In Arabic, it may be implicit (dammir) or explicit like 'kāna'.
2
Check if the copula indicates time
Ask: does the copula itself convey a time (past, present, future)? The non-temporal copula does not; it only asserts the connection. The temporal copula, like 'kāna', specifies past time.
3
Classify the copula type
If no time is indicated, it is non-temporal (rābiṭa ghayr zamāniyya). If time is indicated (e.g., 'kāna'), it is temporal (rābiṭa zamāniyya).
Non-temporal copulas include the implied copula in Arabic nominal sentences and explicit particles like Greek 'estin' or Persian 'ast'.
4
Understand the Arabic grammar connection
Arabic grammarians call 'kāna' a deficient verb (nāqiṣa) precisely because it does not carry an event (ḥadath) but only a temporal link. This aligns with the logical concept of the temporal copula.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The assents [tasdīqāt] but it is not far; for they explicitly state that it does not include the event [ḥadath] that is in derivatives, and it is called the non-temporal copula, and (estin) in Greek, and (ast) in Persian from it, i.e., from the non-temporal. Independence (and perhaps the copula is in the form of the word, like 'kāna'), and it is a copula absolutely; for the Arabic grammarians explicitly state that it does not contain an event, and upon it is based the designation [of the verb] as deficient, and it is called the temporal copula.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage defines two types of copula (rābiṭa) in propositions. It begins by referring to a previous discussion on assents (tasdīqāt) and states that the copula does not indicate an event (ḥadath). The first type is the non-temporal copula, which is a linking word without time reference, exemplified by 'estin' in Greek and 'ast' in Persian. In Arabic, this is the implied copula in nominal sentences. The second type is the temporal copula, which is embedded in the form of a word like 'kāna'. The Arabic grammarians assert that 'kāna' does not signify an event (ḥadath) but only temporal connection, which is why it is called a deficient verb (nāqiṣa). This distinction is crucial for understanding the structure of propositions in logic.
