Categorical and Conditional Propositions
Division of propositions into categorical and conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between categorical and conditional propositions and identify their parts (subject/predicate vs. antecedent/consequent) in any given proposition.

Core Idea
The basic division of propositions is between direct attribution and conditional linkage.

Propositions are the building blocks of logical reasoning. This lesson classifies them into two fundamental types: categorical (حملیة), which makes a direct judgment about two terms, and conditional (شرطية), which links two complete statements. Knowing this division and the terminology for their parts is essential for analyzing any argument in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the definition: if the judgment affirms or negates something directly of something else, it is categorical; otherwise, it is conditional.
Categorical = single subject-predicate structure; Conditional = two complete propositions connected.
2
Recognize the structure of a categorical proposition
Look for the subject (موضوع) – what is being spoken about – and the predicate (محمول) – what is said about the subject.
Example: 'Zakat is obligatory.' Subject = Zakat, Predicate = obligatory.
3
Recognize the structure of a conditional proposition
Look for the antecedent (مقدم) – the 'if' part – and the consequent (تالي) – the 'then' part.
Example: 'If one possesses the nisab, then zakat is due.' Antecedent = possessing nisab, Consequent = zakat due.
4
Apply the correct terminology
Given a proposition, state whether it is categorical or conditional, and identify its parts using the terms from this lesson.
Practice with examples from fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَبْحَث: تَقْسِيمُ الْقَضِيَّةِ إِلَى الْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ. وَالْقَضِيَّةُ إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِثُبُوتِ شَيْءٍ لِشَيْءٍ أَوْ نَفْسِهِ عَنْهُ فَحَمْلِيَّةٌ، مُوجِبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيَّةٌ. وَيُسَمَّى الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ مَوْضُوعًا فِي الْحَمْلِيَّةِ، وَمُقَدَّمًا فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ، وَالْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ مَحْمُولًا فِي الْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَتَالِيًا فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ.
Topic: The division of the proposition into categorical and conditional. The proposition: if judgment is made in it of the affirmation of something for something or its negation from it, then it is categorical, affirmative or negative; otherwise, it is conditional. The subject of judgment is called the subject in the categorical, and the antecedent in the conditional; and the predicate of judgment is called the predicate in the categorical and the consequent in the conditional.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a definitional heading (mabḥath) that introduces the fundamental division of propositions (qaḍiyya) into categorical (ḥamliyya) and conditional (sharṭiyya). It begins by defining the categorical proposition: one in which a judgment is made about the affirmation or negation of something for something else (subject and predicate). It can be either affirmative or negative. If the judgment does not fit this pattern (i.e., it involves two complete propositions linked), then it is conditional. The passage then specifies the terminology: in a categorical proposition, the subject of judgment is called the subject (mawḍūʿ) and the predicate is called the predicate (maḥmūl); in a conditional proposition, the first part is called the antecedent (muqaddam) and the second part is called the consequent (tālī). This classification is essential for understanding the structure of logical arguments.

Logicians vs. Grammarians on Conditional Judgments
Explaining the disagreement on conditional judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logicians' and grammarians' views on the locus of judgment in conditional propositions and explain why the dispute is considered verbal.

Core Idea
The dispute hinges on whether the judgment is the connection or lies in the result.

When you read a conditional like 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists,' where is the judgment? Logicians say it's the link between clauses; grammarians say it's the result, with the condition as a restriction. This debate affected how Islamic scholars in different fields treated conditional rulings. In this lesson, we unpack the two positions and their resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two doctrines
Locate the logicians' view: judgment is the connection (rabṭ) between antecedent and consequent. The grammarians' view: judgment is in the result (jazā'), with the condition as a restriction (qayd).
The antecedent and consequent themselves contain no judgment, according to logicians.
2
Analyze the example
Examine the example 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists.' Logicians see a connection; grammarians paraphrase it as 'daytime exists at the time of sunrise' – a categorical with a temporal restriction.
This shows how the same sentence can be interpreted differently.
3
Explore the defense and narrowing
The author defends logicians by arguing their view concerns intelligible meanings, and denying that is obstinacy. Then the dispute is said to be verbal: grammarians focus on performative consequents (inshā'), logicians on statement consequents (khabar).
This narrowing suggests the schools are not in direct contradiction.
4
Assess the resolution
A commentator adds that when the consequent is a performative, it is not a statement, so the substantive dispute only applies to conditionals with statement consequents.
Thus the disagreement is really about how to analyze conditional language, not about the facts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذْهَبَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الرَّبْطُ بَيْنَ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي، وَأَمَّا هُمَا فَلَا حُكْمَ فِيهِمَا أَصْلًا، وَمَذْهَبُ أَهْلِ الْعَرَبِيَّةِ أَنَّهُ الْحُكْمُ فِي الْجَزَاءِ، وَالشَّرْطُ قَيْدٌ لِلْمُسْنَدِ فِيهِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْحَالِ أَوِ الظَّرْفِ. فَمَعْنَى قَوْلِنَا إِنْ كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةً فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ: النَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ وَقْتَ طُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ. كَذَا فِي الْمِفْتَاحِ. وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ مَقْصُودَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ أَنَّ مِنَ الْمَعَانِي الْمَعْقُولَةِ الْمُحْتَمَلَةِ لِلصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ مَا يُحْكَمُ فِيهِ بَيْنَ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي بِالِاتِّصَالِ وَالِانْفِصَالِ، وَلَا يَلِيقُ لِلنُّحَاةِ إِنْكَارُ هَذَا؛ لِكَوْنِهِ مُكَابَرَةً، وَكَوْنِ نَظَرِهِمْ فِي الْأَلْفَاظِ فَقَطْ دُونَ الْمَعَانِي، فَالنِّزَاعُ إِنَّمَا يُعْقَلُ بِأَنَّ الْقَضَايَا الْمُصَدَّرَةَ بِـ إِنْ وَأَمْثَالِهَا الْمُسْتَعْمَلَةَ فِي مُحَاوَرَاتِ الْعَرَبِ هَلْ هِيَ شَرْطِيَّاتٌ أَوْ حَمْلِيَّاتٌ، بِأَنْ يَكُونَ الْحُكْمُ فِي الْجَزَاءِ، وَالشَّرْطُ قَيْدًا لَهُ فِي الْمُسْنَدِ. فَذَهَبَ أَهْلُ الْمَنْطِقِ إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ، وَأَهْلُ الْعَرَبِيَّةِ إِلَى الثَّانِي. ثُمَّ قِيلَ: النِّزَاعُ لَفْظِيٌّ؛ فَإِنَّ أَهْلَ الْعَرَبِيَّةِ إِنَّمَا يَدَّعُونَ الْحُكْمَ فِي التَّالِي فِيمَا تَالِيهِ إِنْشَاءٌ، وَأَهْلُ الْمَنْطِقِ إِنَّمَا يُنْكِرُونَهُ فِيمَا تَالِيهِ خَبَرٌ. وَقَالَ بَعْضُ الشُّرَّاحِ: إِنَّ الَّتِي تَالِيهَا إِنْشَاءٌ لَيْسَ خَبَرًا، فَالنِّزَاعُ إِنَّمَا يَكُونُ فِيمَا تَالِيهِ خَبَرٌ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ مَا تَالِيهِ إِنْشَاءٌ.
And know that the doctrine of the logicians is that the judgment in the conditional is only the connection between the antecedent and the consequent, and as for them (the antecedent and consequent), there is no judgment in them at all. And the doctrine of the grammarians is that it (the judgment) is in the result (jazā'), and the condition is a restriction on the subject of the predicate phrase, similar to the circumstance or adverbial phrase. So the meaning of our saying 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists' is: daytime exists at the time of sunrise. This is in al-Miftāḥ. And it is not hidden that the intention of the logicians is that among the intelligible meanings that admit truth and falsity, there is that in which judgment is made between the antecedent and consequent by connection or disjunction, and it is not fitting for the grammarians to deny this because it would be obstinacy, and because their consideration is only of words, not meanings. So the dispute is only intelligible concerning propositions beginning with 'in' and its like used in Arabic discourse: are they conditionals or categoricals, such that the judgment is in the result and the condition is a restriction for it in the predicate phrase? So the logicians went to the first, and the grammarians to the second. Then it was said: the dispute is verbal; for the grammarians only claim judgment in the consequent when its consequent is a performative, and the logicians only deny it when its consequent is a statement. And some commentators said: that whose consequent is a performative is not a statement, so the dispute only occurs when the consequent is a statement. And it is not hidden that what has a performative as consequent...
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage addresses a key dispute between logicians and grammarians regarding the nature of conditional propositions. The author first states each school's position: logicians consider the judgment in a conditional to be the connection (rabṭ) between antecedent and consequent, not any judgment on the parts themselves; grammarians view the judgment as being solely in the result (al-jazā'), with the condition acting as a restriction (qayd) on the subject. An example clarifies: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists' means for grammarians that daytime exists at sunrise—the condition modifies the predicate phrase's time. The author then defends the logicians' perspective, arguing that denying the existence of judgments of connection/ disjunction among intelligible meanings is unreasonable (mukābara). The real dispute is about whether Arabic conditional sentences are truly conditional propositions (shartiyyāt) or categoricals (ḥamliyyāt) with a restricted judgment. Ultimately, some say the dispute is verbal (lafẓī): grammarians restrict their claim to performative consequents (inshā'), while logicians deny judgment in the consequent only for statements (khabar). A commentator adds that performative consequents are not statements, so the substantive dispute only concerns statements. The passage shows a sophisticated awareness of how linguistic analysis and logical analysis intersect.

The Locus of Judgment in Conditionals: Logicians vs. Grammarians
Author resolves disagreement on conditional judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions in the dispute over the locus of judgment in conditional propositions and evaluate the logical proof that supports the logicians' view.

Core Idea
A conditional sentence can be true even when its parts are false – that is why judgment must be on the relation, not the parts.

This passage from the commentary on the Īsāghūjī addresses a fundamental disagreement: where exactly does the truth or falsity of a conditional proposition reside? Logicians and grammarians offer different answers. By carefully analyzing a counterfactual example – 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' – you will see why the logical tradition holds that the judgment belongs to the relationship between antecedent and consequent, not to the individual parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing doctrines
Find in the Arabic the view of the logicians (المنطقيين) and the view of the grammarians (أهل العربية). What is the key difference?
Logicians: judgment on the relation; grammarians: judgment only in the consequent.
2
Examine the counterfactual example
Look at the example 'إن كان زيد حمارًا كان ناهقًا'. Why is this conditional considered true? What are the truth values of the parts in reality?
Both antecedent and consequent are false, yet the conditional is true.
3
Understand the proof
The author says that if the judgment were on the consequent alone, the conditional would be false whenever the consequent is false. But we know the conditional is true. Therefore the judgment must be on the relation.
4
Apply the logical principle
The argument uses the rule that negation of the absolute entails negation of the qualified. Explain why allowing a conditional to be true with a false consequent would lead to a contradiction if judgment were on the consequent alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذْهَبَ الْمُنْطِقِيِّينَ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ بَيْنَ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي، وَأَمَّا هُمَا فَلَا حُكْمَ فِيهِمَا أَصْلًا، وَمَذْهَبُ أَهْلِ الْعَرَبِيَّةِ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْجَزَاءِ، وَالشَّرْطَ قَيْدٌ لِلْمُسْنَدِ فِيهِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْحَالِ أَوِ الظَّرْفِ. قَالَ السَّيِّدُ الْأَوَّلُ هُوَ الْحَقُّ، لِلْقَطْعِ بِصِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ مَعَ كَذِبِ التَّالِي فِي الْوَاقِعِ، كَقَوْلِنَا إِنْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ حِمَارًا كَانَ نَاهِقًا، وَلَوْ كَانَ الْخَبَرُ هُوَ التَّالِي لَمْ يُتَصَوَّرْ صِدْقُهَا مَعَ كَذِبِهِ؛ ضَرُورَةَ اسْتِلْزَامِ انْتِفَاءِ الْمُطْلَقِ انْتِفَاءَ الْمُقَيَّدِ؛ فَإِنَّهُ مُشْتَمِلٌ عَلَى الْمُطْلَقِ، وَتَجْوِيزُ مُنَافَاةِ الْقَيْدِ لِلْمُطْلَقِ قَوْلٌ بِالْجَمْعِ بَيْنَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ.
Know that the doctrine of the logicians is that the judgment in the conditional is between the antecedent and the consequent, and as for the two, there is no judgment in them at all. The doctrine of the grammarians is that the judgment is in the consequence, and the condition is a qualification for the predicate in it, like the state or circumstance. The sayyid said: The first is correct, due to the certainty of the truth of the conditional along with the falsehood of the consequent in actuality, as in our saying: 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray.' And if the predicate were the consequent, its truth would not be conceivable along with its falsehood; because of the necessity that the negation of the absolute entails the negation of the qualified; for it includes the absolute, and allowing that the qualification contradicts the absolute is to say the two contradictories together.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known disagreement between logicians and grammarians regarding the nature of conditional propositions. The logicians hold that in a conditional, such as 'If it is day, then it is light,' the judgment (the truth or falsity) attaches to the relation between the antecedent and consequent, not to either part individually. The grammarians, however, maintain that the judgment is only in the consequent, with the antecedent serving merely as a qualifier for the predicate, akin to a circumstantial expression. The author then cites the sayyid (al-Sharif al-Jurjani) who asserts that the logicians' view is correct. He proves this by a decisive argument: a conditional can be true even if the consequent is false in reality. For example, 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' is true (since it expresses a conditional fact) even though Zayd is not a donkey and does not bray. If the grammarians were correct and the judgment were only on the consequent, then the conditional would be false whenever the consequent is false, because a simple proposition (the consequent) would be false. But we know the conditional is true, so the judgment must be on the relation, not just the consequent. The argument is that the negation of the absolute (the conditional) entails the negation of the qualified (the consequent only), so to allow the truth of the conditional with a false consequent would be contradictory if the judgment were only on the consequent. This guards against the misunderstanding that conditionals are simply compound propositions with no relational force.

Key Terms
شَرْطِيَّة
حُكْم
مُقَدَّم
تَالِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In the antecedent only
B) In the consequent only
C) In the relation between antecedent and consequent
D) In both antecedent and consequent equally

Key Takeaway
The truth of a conditional proposition depends on the relation between its parts, not on the truth of the parts themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional statement: 'If the witness is reliable, then accept his testimony.' According to the logicians' view, does this statement claim that the witness is reliable? Does it claim that his testimony is accepted? Explain.
?
Construct a similar proof: take a conditional that is true but has a false antecedent and false consequent (e.g., 'If the dead spoke, they would testify'). Show why this supports the logicians' position.

Distinction between Absolute and Conditional Propositions and the Nonexistent Counterpart Sophism
Author distinguishes absolute from conditional propositions and resolves the ma'dum al-nazir sophism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish absolute from conditional propositions and apply that distinction to resolve the ma'dum al-nazir sophism.

Core Idea
The absolute reports reality; the conditional reports a supposition—they are not related as universal to particular.

Islamic logicians often encounter propositions that seem to predicate non-existence, like 'Zayd is a nonexistent counterpart.' This passage clarifies a fundamental distinction: an absolute proposition reports about actual reality, while a conditional proposition reports about existence on a certain supposition. The falsehood of the absolute does not entail the falsehood of the conditional, because the absolute is not the universal of the conditional. This distinction dissolves the classic 'nonexistent counterpart' sophism, though a subtle nuance arises when the nonexistent is understood relationally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions of absolute and conditional
Find in the passage the definition of an absolute proposition (mutlaq) as a report about real existence (al-thubut al-waqi'i) and a conditional proposition (muqayyad) as a report about existence on a supposition (al-thubut 'ala al-taqdir). Notice that they differ in what they assert about the world.
2
Understand the logical relationship
The passage states that the falsehood of the absolute does not necessitate the falsehood of the conditional. This is because the absolute is not an absolute (universal) of the conditional. In other words, the conditional is not a special case of the absolute in a simple universal-particular hierarchy. Understand this by considering an example: 'Everything is mortal' is universal; 'Socrates is mortal' is particular. Here, 'Zayd is nonexistent' (absolute) and 'Zayd is a nonexistent counterpart' (conditional) are not related that way.
This step is crucial for avoiding the fallacy of composition.
3
Apply the distinction to the sophism
The sophism 'ma'dum al-nazir' claims that since the absolute 'Zayd is nonexistent' is false (because Zayd exists), the conditional 'Zayd is a nonexistent counterpart' should also be false. But the conditional is true on its supposition. Resolve it by noting that the conditional's absolute (the 'nonexistent' in the sense of 'being a counterpart') is not the same as the absolute proposition's 'nonexistent' (the general non-existence). The falsehood of the general does not affect the specific.
The passage mentions that the conditional's absolute is 'the nonexistent in a non-generic sense'.
4
Recognize the nuance of relational nonexistent
If 'nonexistent' is interpreted as a relational nonexistent (al-'adam al-rabati), meaning 'Zayd has no counterpart,' then the simple distinction is insufficient. The shaykh's response is needed: the relational meaning is not an independent predicate and is essentially different from the independent nonexistent. This shows that the resolution depends on how the term is understood.
This step highlights the precision required in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : الفرق بين القضية المطلقة والمقيدة بأن الأولى حكاية عن الثبوت الواقعي، والثانية عن الثبوت على التقدير، ولا يلزم من كذب الأولى كذب الثانية ؛ لأن الأولى ليست مطلقا للثانية. واعترض عليه بعض الأساتذة بأن الفرق تحكُم، بل مدلول مطلق القضية واحد منطبق على سائر القضايا. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. (وَبِمِثْل ذَلِكَ يَنْحَلُّ شُبْهَةُ «مَعْدُومُ النَّظِيرِ»(۱))، قرر الشبهة بأن معدوم النظير الذي هو المقيد ثابت لزيد مثلا، أن مطلقه الذي هو المعدوم غير الأعم ثابت له ، فلا شك في انحلاله بمثل ذلك الجواب؛ فإن مطلقه المعدوم من كونه نظير زيد أو نفسه، وهو ثابت في ضمن ثبوته ، وإن جعل المعدوم من العدم الرابطي، ويكون معناه زيد ليس له نظير وسيق الشبهة لا ينحل بذلك، بل يحتاج إلى ما أجاب به الشيخ من أنه ليس مقيدا للمعدوم، وأن المعنى الرابطي غير مستقل مغاير بالذات للمعدوم المحمول المستقل، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال .
It was said: The difference between the absolute and the conditional proposition is that the first is a report about real existence, and the second is a report about existence on a supposition, and the falsehood of the first does not necessitate the falsehood of the second because the first is not an absolute for the second. And some teachers objected that the difference is arbitrary; rather, the meaning of the absolute proposition is one and applies to all propositions. This, and God knows best what is correct. (And by a similar [response] the sophism of 'the nonexistent counterpart' is resolved.) He presented the sophism: that the nonexistent counterpart, which is the conditional, is affirmed for Zayd, for example, and that its absolute, which is the nonexistent in a non-generic sense, is affirmed for him, so there is no doubt that it is resolved by such a response; for its absolute, the nonexistent, in terms of being a counterpart of Zayd or Zayd himself, is affirmed within its affirmation. But if the nonexistent is taken as a relational nonexistent, and its meaning is 'Zayd has no counterpart,' the sophism is not resolved by that; rather, it needs what the shaykh answered: that it is not conditional for the nonexistent, and that the relational meaning is not independent and is essentially different from the nonexistent as an independent predicate. And God knows best the reality of the situation.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage defines the difference between absolute (mutlaq) and conditional (muqayyad) propositions, then uses that distinction to resolve a logical sophism known as 'ma'dum al-nazir' (nonexistent counterpart). The author begins by stating the distinction: an absolute proposition reports about actual reality (al-thubut al-waqi'i), while a conditional proposition reports about existence on a certain supposition (al-thubut 'ala al-taqdir). Consequently, the falsehood of the absolute does not entail the falsehood of the conditional, because the absolute is not the universal of the conditional. Some teachers object that this distinction is arbitrary; they argue that the meaning of an absolute proposition is unitary and applies to all propositions equally. The author, however, sides with the original position and uses it to resolve the sophism of 'nonexistent counterpart'. The sophism: one can say 'Zayd is a nonexistent counterpart' – this is a conditional proposition (since 'nonexistent counterpart' only applies on the supposition that Zayd existed but had no counterpart). The absolute proposition 'Zayd is nonexistent' is false (since Zayd exists), but the conditional 'Zayd is a nonexistent counterpart' is true (because on the supposition that Zayd was a counterpart, he would be nonexistent). The objection may arise: if the absolute is false, how can the conditional be true? The resolution: the absolute 'Zayd is nonexistent' is not the universal of the conditional; the conditional's absolute is 'nonexistent' in a specific sense (being a counterpart), not the general nonexistent. Thus the falsehood of the general nonexistent does not harm the conditional. However, if the 'nonexistent' is taken as a relational nonexistent (al-'adam al-rabati), meaning 'Zayd has no counterpart,' then the sophism is not resolved by this simple distinction because the relational nonexistent is not the same as the independent predicate. The author mentions that the shaykh's response is needed: the relational meaning is not independent and is essentially different from the independent nonexistent. This shows the subtlety required in handling logical conditions.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): A proposition that reports about existence in actual reality without any condition or supposition. [Note: In this text, mutlaq contrasts with muqayyad; in earlier logic, mutlaq often refers to an absolute categorical proposition as opposed to modal.]
مقيد (muqayyad): A proposition that reports about existence on a certain supposition or condition, not necessarily in actual reality. [Note: Stable term across the tradition as the opposite of mutlaq.]
شبهة (shubha): A sophism or fallacious argument that needs to be resolved; here specifically the ma'dum al-nazir sophism. [Note: In jadal, shubha can also mean a valid doubt; here it is a logical fallacy.]
معدوم النظير (ma'dum al-nazir): A term referring to a predicate like 'nonexistent counterpart' which is conditional on a supposition. [Note: This specific sophism appears in later logical works; not found in earlier texts.]
عدم رابطي (adam rabti): A relational nonexistent, where the nonexistence is not an independent attribute but a relation, e.g., 'Zayd has no counterpart'. [Note: This distinction between relational and independent nonexistence is technical in later logic and kalam.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the falsehood of an absolute proposition not necessitate the falsehood of a conditional proposition?
A) Because the absolute proposition is false in all cases.
B) Because the absolute proposition is not the universal (mutlaq) of the conditional.
C) Because conditional propositions are never false.
D) Because the absolute proposition is about supposition, not reality.

Key Takeaway
The absolute and conditional propositions are logically independent; the absolute does not serve as the universal for the conditional, and this distinction resolves the ma'dum al-nazir sophism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an absolute and a conditional proposition about an Islamic ruling and explain why the falsehood of one does not entail the falsehood of the other. Use an example from fiqh, such as 'Zakat is obligatory' (absolute) and 'If one possesses the nisab, zakat is obligatory' (conditional).
?
The sophism 'ma'dum al-nazir' can be rephrased: 'The statement (a) "The world is temporally originated" is absolute; (b) "The world is temporally originated on the supposition of creation" is conditional. If (a) is true, does (b) follow? Use the text's reasoning to answer whether the falsehood of (a) would affect (b).

Absolute vs. Restricted Propositions and the Non-Existent Peer
Author explains the difference between absolute and restricted propositions and resolves the sophism of the non-existent peer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and restricted propositions and apply that distinction to resolve the sophism of the non-existent peer.

Core Idea
Truth of a restricted proposition does not imply truth of its absolute counterpart.

This passage tackles a subtle but crucial logical distinction: the difference between a proposition that reports on actual reality (absolute) and one that reports on a hypothetical condition (restricted). The author defends this distinction against an objection and then demonstrates its power by dissolving a famous sophism about 'the non-existent of the peer.' By understanding this distinction, you will be able to avoid conflation errors in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Find in the Arabic the definitions of absolute and restricted propositions. The absolute reports on actual existence (thubut waqi'i), the restricted on existence under a presupposition (thubut 'ala al-taqdir).
2
Understand the objection
Some teachers objected that the distinction is arbitrary (taḥakkum) because the meaning of the absolute proposition is one and applies to all propositions. Reflect on why this objection, if correct, would collapse the distinction.
3
Apply the distinction to the sophism
The sophism claims that 'Zayd is a non-existent of a peer' (restricted) is true because its absolute 'Zayd is non-existent' is true. But the absolute of the restricted is not the same as the absolute proposition itself. Trace how the author separates 'non-existent' as a relational predicate (al-'adam al-ribāṭī) from the independent predicate.
4
Recognise the resolution's structure
The shaykh's answer shows that 'non-existent' in 'non-existent of a peer' is not a restriction of the absolute non-existent; it is a different, relational meaning. The sophism conflates the two. By distinguishing them, the argument no longer holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : الفرق بين القضية المطلقة والمقيدة بأن الأولى حكاية عن الثبوت الواقعي، والثانية عن الثبوت على التقدير، ولا يلزم من كذب الأولى كذب الثانية ؛ لأن الأولى ليست مطلقا للثانية. واعترض عليه بعض الأساتذة بأن الفرق تحكُم، بل مدلول مطلق القضية واحد منطبق على سائر القضايا. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. (وَبِمِثْل ذَلِكَ يَنْحَلُّ شُبْهَةُ «مَعْدُومُ النَّظِيرِ»)، قرر الشبهة بأن معدوم النظير الذي هو المقيد ثابت لزيد مثلا، أن مطلقه الذي هو المعدوم غير الأعم ثابت له ، فلا شك في انحلاله بمثل ذلك الجواب؛ فإن مطلقه المعدوم من كونه نظير زيد أو نفسه، وهو ثابت في ضمن ثبوته ، وإن جعل المعدوم من العدم الرابطي، ويكون معناه زيد ليس له نظير وسيق الشبهة لا ينحل بذلك، بل يحتاج إلى ما أجاب به الشيخ من أنه ليس مقيدا للمعدوم، وأن المعنى الرابطي غير مستقل مغاير بالذات للمعدوم المحمول المستقل، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال .
It was said: The difference between the absolute and restricted proposition is that the first is a report about actual existence, and the second is about existence on a presupposition, and it does not follow from the falsehood of the first the falsehood of the second, because the first is not the absolute of the second. And some teachers objected that the difference is arbitrary; rather, the meaning of the absolute proposition is one and applies to all propositions. This, and God knows best what is correct. And by a similar [answer] the sophism of 'the non-existent of the peer' is resolved. He formulated the sophism that the restricted non-existent of the peer is affirmed of Zayd, for example, but its absolute, which is the non-existent in the broader sense, is affirmed of him. So there is no doubt about its resolution by such an answer. For its absolute, the non-existent in terms of being a peer of Zayd or himself, is affirmed within its affirmation. And if the non-existent is taken as relational non-existence, meaning 'Zayd has no peer,' the preceding sophism is not resolved by that, but rather needs what the shaykh answered: that it is not a restriction of the non-existent, and that the relational meaning is not independent and is essentially different from the independent non-existent predicate. And God knows best the reality of the matter.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage defends the distinction between absolute and restricted propositions against an objection. The absolute proposition reports on actual existence, while the restricted reports on existence under a hypothetical condition. The objection claims that the meaning of any absolute proposition is one and the same, making the distinction arbitrary. The author then shows how this distinction resolves the famous sophism of 'the non-existent of the peer.' The sophism argues that 'Zayd is a non-existent of a peer' (restricted) is true because its absolute 'Zayd is non-existent' (in some sense) is true. But the answer is that the absolute of the restricted proposition is not the absolute proposition itself; rather, 'non-existent' in 'non-existent of a peer' means relational non-existence (like 'Zayd has no peer'), which is not the same as the predicate 'non-existent.' The shaykh's answer clarifies that the relational meaning is not an independent predicate and is essentially different from the absolute non-existent. Thus the sophism falls.

Key Takeaway

Contradiction in Conditionals: The Condition Qualifies the Judgment
Author presents and refutes objections regarding condition in conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two conditional statements with the same condition but opposite consequents are contradictory by analyzing how the condition qualifies the judgment.

Core Idea
The condition qualifies the entire consequent judgment, so affirming and negating under the same condition is a genuine contradiction.

This passage defends a fundamental principle: when two conditional propositions share the same antecedent but have opposite consequents (affirmative vs. negative), they are genuinely contradictory. Some object that the condition makes the truth relative, or that the antecedent is impossible, thus avoiding contradiction. In this lesson, we will dissect the logical structure, see why the objections fail, and apply this tool to Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditionals
Look at the example: 'If nothing is established, Zaid is standing' and 'If nothing is established, Zaid is not standing.' Note they share the same antecedent (condition) but have opposite consequents (affirmation and negation of the same predicate).
2
Determine how the condition qualifies the consequent
According to the grammarians and logicians, in the affirmative consequent, the condition qualifies the affirmation itself. So 'Zaid is standing at the time of non-establishment' means the standing is restricted to that time. In the negative consequent, the condition may qualify either the negation (making it a restricted negation) or the affirmation (making it the negation of the qualified). In either case, the condition is part of the judgment.
3
Analyze the contradiction
The author argues that whether the condition qualifies the affirmation or the negation, the two statements are contradictory because they assert and deny the same predicate of the same subject under the exact same qualification. Intuitively, 'Zaid is standing at time T' and 'Zaid is not standing at time T' cannot both be true.
4
Examine the objection of 'truth under assumption'
The objector claims that in conditionals, the consequent is true only relative to the antecedent, so there is no absolute contradiction. The author replies that the logicians never claim absolute truth; the contradiction holds within the conditional context: under the same assumption, the two consequents are opposites and cannot both be true. This is precisely the logicians' doctrine.
5
Apply to a real Islamic science example
Consider two conditional rulings in fiqh: 'If the water is ritually impure, then it cannot be used for ablution' and 'If the water is ritually impure, then it can be used for ablution.' Under the same condition, these cannot both be valid rulings; one must be false. This shows the practical force of conditional contradiction.
The condition qualifies the ruling, not just the substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصديقات كقولنا: إذا لم يثبت شيء من الأشياء فزيد قائم، وإذا لم يثبت شيء منها فزيد ليس بقائم، فنقول : أهل العربية قد قالوا بأن الشرط في موجبة التالي قيد للثبوت، ففي سالبة التالي إن كان قيدا له أيضا فقد ورد السلب على المقيد، وثبوته وسلبه نقيضان ،قطعا، وإن كان قيدا للسلب فهو سلب مقيد، ولكونه أخص من سلب المقيد صدق سلب المقيد ،أيضا وهما نقيضان، وإلى هذا أشار بقوله: (فَإِنَّ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ فِي وَقْتِ عَدَمِ ثُبُوتِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ» يُنَاقِضُ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ ، وَهُوَ بَدِيهِيٌّ ))، وبتقريرنا اندفع ما قال بعض الشراح من أنهم أرادوا بكون الشرط قيدا في المسند أنه قيد للثبوت في موجبة التالي، وقيد للسلب في سالبة التالي، ونقيض المقيد سلبه، لا السلب المقيد، كما أن نقيض الاتصال رفعه ، لا اتصال الرفع. (وَأَمَّا إِذَا كَانَ الْحُكْمُ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ بِالاِتِّصَالِ بَيْنَ النِّسْبَتَيْنِ فَلَا يَلْزَمُ ذَلِكَ ؛ فَإِنَّ نَقِيض الاِتِّصَالِ رَفْعُهُ ، لَا وُجُودُ اتِّصَالِ آخَرَ أَيَّ اتِّصَالٍ كَانَ(۲)) ، تاليه مناف لتالي الأول ، أم لا . واعترض بأن اجتماع النقيضين إنما يلزم إذا كان المراد بالتالي الثبوت في نفس الأمر، وهو ممنوع ، وإنما المراد الثبوت على ذلك التقدير، فلا تناقض ، كما لا تناقض بين المتصلتين المذكورتين . وأجاب عنه بعض الأساتذة في مكاتباته أن فيه خلطا بين الشرطية والحملية ؛ فإنه إن أراد بالثبوت على التقدير ثبوت نسبة على تقدير أخرى، بأن يكون الحكم بين الثبوت والتقدير فمسلم أنه لا تناقض، لكن ذلك بعينه مذهب الحكماء وإن غيرت العبارة، وإن أراد ثبوت محمول التالي لموضوعه، بأن يكون مصححا للسكوت عليه لكن على تقدير فممنوع أنه لا تناقض، كيف وقد ورد السلب على ذلك الثبوت، والشيء وسلبه نقيضان ضرورة، وإنكاره مكابرة . هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. واعترض عليه بأن اجتماع النقيضين إنما يستحيل إذا لم يكن القيد محالا ، والقيد ههنا محال ألا ترى أنه في الشرطية الميزانية قد يجتمعان على . تقدير المقدم المحال، ولا يخفى سخافته؛ فإن اجتماع النقيضين بمعنى مطابقتهما لما حكيا عنه محال بالضرورة، وأما في الشرطية فليس الحكم في التالي : ، فلا حكاية ، فلا مطابقة لما حكيا عنه، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Assertions (tasdiqat) such as our statement: 'If nothing of things is established, then Zaid is standing, and if nothing of them is established, then Zaid is not standing.' We say: The Arabic grammarians have said that the condition in the affirmative consequent is a qualification of the affirmation, so in the negative consequent, if it is also a qualification of it, then negation has occurred upon the qualified, and its affirmation and its negation are two contradictories, absolutely. And if it is a qualification of the negation, then it is a restricted negation, and because it is more specific than the negation of the qualified, the negation of the qualified is also true, and they are two contradictories. To this he pointed with his statement: 'Indeed, our statement “Zaid is standing at the time of non-establishment of anything” contradicts our statement “Zaid is not standing at that time,” and it is self-evident.' And by our account, what some commentators said is refuted: that they intended by the condition being a qualification in the predicate that it is a qualification of the affirmation in the affirmative consequent, and a qualification of the negation in the negative consequent, and the contradictory of the qualified is its negation, not the restricted negation, just as the contradictory of conjunction is its removal, not the conjunction of removal. 'And as for when the judgment in the conditional is about the connection between the two relations, then that does not follow; for the contradictory of connection is its removal, not the existence of another connection, whatever connection it may be.' Its consequent contradicts the first consequent or not. And he objected that the conjunction of two contradictories only follows if what is meant by the consequent is the affirmation in reality, which is denied; rather what is meant is the affirmation upon that assumption, so there is no contradiction, just as there is no contradiction between the two mentioned conditionals. And some teachers answered him in his writings that there is confusion between the conditional and the categorical; for if by 'affirmation upon assumption' he means the affirmation of a relation upon another assumption, such that the judgment is between affirmation and assumption, then it is granted that there is no contradiction, but that is exactly the doctrine of the philosophers even if the expression is changed. And if he means the affirmation of the predicate of the consequent upon its subject, such that it is a corrector for silence about it, but upon an assumption, then it is denied that there is no contradiction; how can it be when negation has occurred upon that affirmation, and the thing and its negation are two contradictories necessarily, and its denial is stubbornness. This, and Allah knows best the truth. And he objected against him that the conjunction of two contradictories is only impossible if the qualification is not impossible, and the qualification here is impossible; do you not see that in the balanced conditional they may coexist upon the assumption of an impossible antecedent, and its absurdity is not hidden; for the conjunction of two contradictories in the sense of their correspondence to what they report is impossible necessarily. And as for the conditional, the judgment is not in the consequent, so there is no report, so no correspondence to what they report, and Allah knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining and defending a point about the condition (shart) in conditional propositions from objections. The author begins by giving an example of two conditional statements that appear contradictory: 'If nothing is established, Zaid is standing' and 'If nothing is established, Zaid is not standing.' The Arabic grammarians argued that in an affirmative consequent, the condition qualifies the affirmation; in a negative consequent, if the condition also qualifies the negation, then the negation applies to the qualified (affirmation restricted by condition), and affirmation and negation of the same thing are contradictories. If the condition qualifies the negation itself, then it is a restricted negation, which also implies the negation of the unrestricted (since it is more specific), again yielding contradiction. The author then cites the original matn asserting that 'Zaid is standing at the time of non-establishment' contradicts 'Zaid is not standing at that time', which is self-evident. The author refutes commentators who tried to avoid contradiction by distinguishing types of qualification. He then discusses the case where the conditional's judgment is about the connection between two relations, where the contradictory is the removal of connection, not another connection. An objector argues that contradiction only arises if the consequent is taken as absolute truth, not truth-under-condition; the author responds by clarifying that the logicians intend the latter, and that the objector confuses conditional and categorical propositions. A second objection claims that the condition is impossible (like an impossible antecedent in a balanced conditional), so contradiction is avoided. The author dismisses this as absurd, since the conjunction of contradictories is impossible per se, and in a conditional the consequent does not report a fact. Overall, the passage defends the logicians' interpretation that the condition qualifies the judgment, leading to genuine contradiction when both affirmative and negative consequents are asserted under the same condition.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): The condition (if-clause) in a conditional proposition that qualifies the judgment in the consequent.
التالي (al-tālī): The consequent clause in a conditional proposition, whose judgment is subject to the condition. [Note: In Arabic grammar, 'tālī' often means the second part of a conditional sentence, but here it is used in the logical sense.]
نقيض (naqīḍ): The contradictory of a proposition; two propositions that cannot both be true or both false.
المقيد (al-muqayyad): A proposition restricted by a condition; its negation is the negation of the restricted form.
السلب المقيد (al-salb al-muqayyad): A negation that is itself restricted by a condition (e.g., 'not standing at time T').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following explains why the two conditionals with the same antecedent and opposite consequents are contradictory?
A) Because the condition is impossible, so no genuine contradiction occurs.
B) Because the condition qualifies the judgment of the consequent, so affirming and negating under that condition yields a contradiction.
C) Because the consequent is considered absolute truth regardless of the condition.
D) Because the negation is restricted and thus compatible with affirmation.

Key Takeaway
In a conditional proposition, the condition qualifies the entire judgment of the consequent; therefore, affirming and negating that judgment under the same condition is a genuine contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the following two conditionals from fiqh: 'If the water is ritually impure, then it cannot be used for ablution' and 'If the water is ritually impure, then it can be used for ablution.' Explain whether these are contradictory and why, using the logical principle from this lesson.

Propositions

Classification of Propositions by Subject
Author classifies propositions into personal, natural, indefinite, and quantified based on subject type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition as personal, indefinite, natural, or quantified based on the nature of its subject and the presence or absence of quantifiers or mental conditions.

Core Idea
A proposition's subject determines its type: particular → personal; universal with no condition → indefinite; universal with mental unity → natural; universal with quantifier → quantified.

When you encounter a proposition in Islamic logic texts, the first step is to determine what kind of subject it has. This classification—personal, indefinite, natural, or quantified—affects how the proposition is treated in syllogisms and arguments. The passage from Bahr al-Ulum lays out this division clearly.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the subject is particular or universal
Look at the subject term. If it refers to a specific individual (e.g., 'Zayd', 'this'), it is particular. If it refers to a general category (e.g., 'man', 'animal'), it is universal.
2
If particular, classify as personal (shakhṣiyya)
A proposition with a particular subject is called personal. Example: 'Zayd is standing' or 'This is standing.' The author notes that the term 'particular' is used instead of 'proper name' to include demonstratives.
3
If universal, check for explicit quantification
If the proposition includes a quantifier like 'all' or 'some', it is quantified (maḥṣūra). Example: 'All men are animals' or 'Some men are writers.'
Quantified propositions are also called musawwara.
4
If no quantifier, distinguish between indefinite and natural
If the universal is predicated without any added condition of generality or particularity, it is indefinite (muhmala). If it is predicated with the condition of mental unity (i.e., considering the essence in itself), it is natural (ṭabī‘iyya).
The difference is subtle: in natural, the essence is considered with the condition of mental unity; in indefinite, no condition is considered.
5
Apply the distinction to examples
Practice: 'Man is an animal' with no quantifier could be indefinite if it means man in general, or natural if it means the essence of man considered as a single concept. The author explains that the indefinite considers the essence without any condition, while the natural considers it under the condition of mental unity.
This distinction affects how the proposition can be used in arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تقسيم القضية باعتبار الموضوع
الموضوع إن كان جزئيا، لم يقل علما ليدخل نحو «هذا قائم»، ولأنه حقيقة في اللفظ فيختص بالملفوظة ظاهرا، (فالقضية شخصية ومخصوصة، وإن كان كليا فإن حكم عليه بلا زيادة شرط من العموم والخصوص (فمهملة عند القدماء، وإن حكم عليه بشرط الوحدة الذهنية فطبيعية). الفرق بين موضوع الطبيعية والمهملة أن الماهية ربما تلاحظ من حيث الإطلاق، لا بأن يكون الإطلاق قيدا في الملحوظ فيه، بل قيدا له في اللحاظ فقط، فهي من هذه الحيثية يسري إليها أحكام الأفراد أصلا؛ لأنها أحكام بالنظر إلى الخصوصية. وربما تلاحظ من حيث هي، مع قطع النظر عن العموم والخصوص والإطلاق والتقييد، لا في اللحاظ ولا في الملحوظ، ويجري فيه أحكام العموم والخصوص والإطلاق والتقييد، فالماهية بالاعتبار الثاني موضوع المهملة، وبالاعتبار الأول موضوع الطبيعية، ويعبر عنه بالماهية من حيث الإطلاق وبشرط الوحدة الذهنية ومن حيث العموم، وهذه عبارات وعنوانات والمعنون واحد. (وإن حكم [فيها] على أفراده)، أي على الكلي من حيث السريان في الأفراد، (فإن بين [فيها] كمية أفراده كلاً أو بعضا (فمحصورة ومسورة).
Section on the division of the proposition with respect to the subject. The subject, if it is particular — he did not say 'proper name' to include expressions like 'this is standing', and because it is literally in the utterance, so it is apparently restricted to spoken propositions — then the proposition is personal and particular. And if it is universal, then if it is predicated without any additional condition of generality or particularity, then it is indefinite (muhmala) according to the ancients. And if it is predicated with the condition of mental unity, then it is natural (tabi'iyya). The difference between the subject of the natural proposition and the indefinite is that the essence is sometimes considered in terms of absoluteness, not such that absoluteness is a condition in the object considered, but rather a condition in the consideration only. So from this perspective, the judgments of individuals apply to it at all, because they are judgments with respect to particularity. And sometimes it is considered in terms of itself, disregarding generality, particularity, absoluteness, and restriction, neither in the consideration nor in the object considered, and the judgments of generality, particularity, absoluteness, and restriction apply to it. So the essence in the second respect is the subject of the indefinite proposition, and in the first respect is the subject of the natural proposition. And it is expressed as 'the essence from the perspective of absoluteness' and 'conditioned by mental unity' and 'from the perspective of generality'. These are expressions and titles, but the denoted thing is one. And if judgment is made on its individuals — i.e., on the universal in terms of its extension over individuals — then if the quantity of its individuals is specified, either all or some, then it is quantified (mahsura) and quantified (musawwara).
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of propositions according to their subject. The author breaks down the types systematically. First, if the subject is a particular (juz'i), the proposition is called 'personal' (shakhsiyya) or 'particular' (makhsusa). He notes that the term 'particular' is used rather than 'proper name' ('alam) to include demonstratives like 'this'. Second, if the subject is universal (kulli), there are three subcases: 1) If the universal is predicated without any added condition of quantity or specificity, it is 'indefinite' (muhmala) according to the early logicians. 2) If it is predicated with the condition that the subject is considered with 'mental unity' (wahda dhihniyya), i.e., the essence in itself without regard to individuals, it is 'natural' (tabi'iyya). The author explains the subtle distinction: in the natural proposition, the essence is considered under the condition of absoluteness (min haythu al-itlaq), meaning that the absoluteness is a condition in the consideration (fi al-lihaz) not in the object considered (fi al-malhuz). Thus, judgments about individuals do not apply to it. In contrast, the indefinite proposition considers the essence without any condition, neither in consideration nor in object, so it can receive judgments of generality or particularity. So both the natural and indefinite have the same referent (the essence) but are considered differently. 3) If the universal is predicated with explicit quantification of its individuals (all or some), the proposition is 'quantified' (mahsura) or 'quantified' (musawwara). This classification is fundamental to understanding categorical propositions in Aristotelian logic as adapted by Islamic logicians.

Key Terms
الشخصية
المهملة
الطبيعية
المحصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The classification of propositions by subject is fundamental for understanding how universals function in logical arguments; the type determines the scope and applicability of the judgment.

Propositions
intermediate

Distinguishing Proposition Types by Subject
Author classifies propositions by subject type: particular, universal conditional/unconditional, quantified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given proposition as shakhsiyya, muhmala, tabi'iyya, or mahsuura based on its subject type and quantifier.

Core Idea
The subject type (particular or universal) determines the proposition's class; universal subjects yield absolute, natural, or quantified forms.

In this passage, the author breaks down propositions by examining the nature of their subject. This is foundational for understanding how logical statements carry varying degrees of generality. By mastering this classification, you will be able to identify the logical form of any proposition you encounter in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify subject as particular or universal
Read the proposition and see if the subject refers to a specific individual or a general concept. For example, 'Zayd is standing' has a particular subject; 'Human is rational' has a universal subject.
If it's a proper name or demonstrative, it's particular.
2
Check for a quantifier on universal subjects
If the proposition says 'every human' or 'some human', it is quantified (mahsuura). If no quantifier, proceed to the next step.
The quantifier may be implicit in context.
3
Distinguish between natural and absolute
If the universal is considered under the condition of mental unity, it is natural (tabi'iyya); if without any condition, it is absolute (muhmala). For instance, 'Human is a species' is natural; 'Human is living' is absolute.
In natural, the predicate applies to the essence; in absolute, it may extend to individuals.
4
Identify deviant propositions
If the quantifier is placed on the predicate side, as in 'Animal is every human', the proposition is deviant (munharifa).
This form is rare but included for completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم القضية باعتبار الموضوع ) فضل ) ا في تَقْسِيمِ الْقَضِيَّةِ بِاعْتِبَارِ الْمَوْضُوعِ ) الْمَوْضُوعُ إِنْ كَانَ جُزْئِيَّا) ، لم يقل عَلَما ليدخل نحو «هذا قائم»، ولأنه حقيقة في اللفظ فيختص بالملفوظة ظاهرا، (فَالْقَضِيَّةُ شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَمَخْصُوصَةٌ، وَإِنْ كَانَ كُلَّيَّا فَإِنْ حُكِمَ عَلَيْهِ بِلَا زِيَادَةِ شَرْطٍ من العموم والخصوص (فَمُهْمَلَةٌ عِنْدَ الْقُدَمَاءِ ، وَإِنْ حُكِمَ عَلَيْهِ بِشَرْطِ الْوَحْدَةِ الذَّهْنِيَّةِ فَطَبِيعِيَّةُ). الفرق بين موضوع الطبيعية والمهملة أن الماهية ربما تلاحظ من حيث الإطلاق، لا بأن يكون الإطلاق قيدا في الملحوظ فيه، بل قيدا له في اللحاظ فقط ، فهي من هذه الحيثية يسري إليها أحكام الأفراد أصلا ؛ لأنها أحكام بالنظر إلى الخصوصية. وربما تلاحظ من حيث هي، مع قطع النظر عن العموم والخصوص والإطلاق والتقييد، لا في اللحاظ ولا في الملحوظ، ويجري فيه أحكام العموم والخصوص والإطلاق والتقييد، فالماهية بالاعتبار الثاني موضوع المهملة، وبالاعتبار الأول موضوع الطبيعية، ويعبر عنه بالماهية من حيث الإطلاق وبشرط الوحدة الذهنية ومن حيث العموم، وهذه عبارات وعنوانات والمعنون واحد . (وَإِنْ حُكِمَ [ فِيهَا] عَلَى أَفْرَادِهِ)، أي على الكلي من حيث السريان في الأفراد، (فَإِنْ بُيِّنَ [فِيهَا] كَمِّيَّةُ أَفْرَادِهِ) كُلاً أو بعضا (فَمَحْصُورَةٌ وَمُسَوَّرَةٌ ، وَمَا الْبَيَانُ)، أي بيان الكمية ([يُسَمَّى ] سُورًا، وَقَدْ يُذْكَرُ السُّورُ جَانِبِ الْمَحْمُولِ (۲)، نحو الحيوان كل إنسان، (فَتُسَمَّى الْقَضِيَّةُ مُنْحَرِفَةٌ)، وتفصيلها مع قلة الجدوى في المطولات (۳) . (وَإِنْ لَمْ تُبَيَّنْ) كمية الأفراد (فَمُهْمَلَةٌ عِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ). اعلم: أن القدماء لم يعتبروها؛ لأن اعتبار الجزئية يغني عن اعتبارها، لكن ما فعله المصنف حسن، (وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالُوا إِنَّهَا تُلَازِمُ الْجُزْئِيَّةَ . هذا بظاهره يدل على أن القول بالتلازم بينهما إنما وقع من المتأخرين، وليس الأمر كذلك؛ فإن الشيخ قد صرح في تصانيفه بالتلازم. نعم، يرد عليهم أنها لا تصلح لملازمة الجزئية؛ إذ تصدق في مادة الطبيعية أيضا، وما قال المحقق الدواني رحمة الله ) ، من أن الأفراد المعتبرة في الجزئية أعم من الاعتبارية والحقيقية، ففي الإنسان كلي» يصدق «بعض الإنسان كلي، فمع أنه خلاف مصرحاتهم لا يفي بالمقصود ؛ فإنه ربما يحكم فيها بأحكام لا يسري إلى الأفراد، حقيقية كانت أو اعتبارية، كقولنا «الإنسان موضوع المهملة ، فالحق أن القول بتلازم الجزئية والمهملة مردود أو مأول.
Division of the proposition according to the subject. (Fadl) On the division of the proposition according to the subject. The subject, if it is particular (juz'i)—he did not say 'individual' to include examples like 'this is standing' and because it is literal in the word so it is confined to the uttered apparently—then the proposition is individual (shakhsiyya) and particular (makhsusa). And if it is universal (kulli), then if judgment is made upon it without any additional condition of generality or particularity, then it is absolute (muhmala) according to the ancients. And if judgment is made upon it with the condition of mental unity, then it is natural (tabi'iyya). The difference between the subject of the natural and the absolute is that the quiddity (mahiyya) is sometimes considered from the aspect of absoluteness (itlaq), not such that absoluteness is a condition in what is considered, but rather a condition for it in the consideration only; thus from this aspect the rulings of individuals apply to it absolutely, because they are rulings with respect to particularity. And sometimes it is considered in itself, disregarding generality, particularity, absoluteness, and restriction—not in the consideration nor in the considered—and rulings of generality, particularity, absoluteness, and restriction apply to it. So the quiddity according to the second consideration is the subject of the absolute, and according to the first consideration is the subject of the natural. It is expressed as the quiddity from the aspect of absoluteness and with the condition of mental unity and from the aspect of generality. These are expressions and titles, and the titled is one. And if judgment is made in it upon its individuals—i.e., upon the universal from the aspect of its extension to individuals—then if the quantity of its individuals is clarified, all or some, then it is quantified (mahsuura) and provided with a quantifier (musawwara). And the clarification—i.e., the clarification of quantity—is called a quantifier (sur). And sometimes the quantifier is mentioned on the side of the predicate, as in 'animal is every human', then the proposition is called deviant (munharifa). Its details, despite little benefit, are in lengthy works. And if the quantity of individuals is not clarified, then it is absolute (muhmala) according to the later scholars. Know that the ancients did not consider it because considering particularity suffices from considering it. But what the author did is good. And hence they said that it entails particularity. This in its apparent meaning indicates that the statement of entailment between them only occurred from the later scholars, but the matter is not so; for the Master (al-Shaykh) has explicitly stated entailment in his writings. Yes, it may be objected to them that it is not fit for entailing particularity because it applies also to the matter of the natural. And what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani (may God have mercy on him) said—that the individuals considered in particularity are more general than conventional and real individuals—so in 'Man is a universal', 'Some man is a universal' applies—yet despite being contrary to their explicit statements, it does not fulfill the purpose; for sometimes judgments are made in it that do not extend to individuals, whether real or conventional, as in our saying 'Man is the subject of the absolute.' So the truth is that the statement of entailment of particularity and the absolute is rejected or interpreted.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of propositions (qadiyya) based on the nature of their subject (mawdu'). The author begins by identifying two main cases: the subject is either particular (juz'i) or universal (kulli). If the subject is particular, the proposition is called shakhsiyya (individual) or makhsusa (particular). The example 'this is standing' illustrates a proposition about a specific individual. If the subject is universal, then further distinctions are made depending on how the judgment is applied. The ancients (qudama') considered a proposition with a universal subject judged without any additional condition of generality or particularity as 'muhmala' (absolute/unquantified). However, if the universal subject is judged under the condition of mental unity (wahda dhihniyya), it is called 'tabi'iyya' (natural). The author clarifies the subtle difference between these two: in a natural proposition, the quiddity (mahiyya) is considered in itself without any condition in the considered object, though the condition of mental unity is in the consideration; in an absolute proposition, the quiddity is considered without any regard to generality or particularity even in the consideration. Thus, the natural proposition does not extend to individuals (its rulings do not automatically apply to particular instances), while the absolute proposition can have rulings that apply to individuals. Next, if the judgment is made on the universal subject explicitly with respect to its individuals (i.e., considering extension), and the quantity (all or some) is specified, the proposition is 'mahsuura' (quantified) and the specification is called the 'sur' (quantifier). If the quantifier is placed on the predicate side, as in 'animal is every human', the proposition is 'munharifa' (deviant). If no quantity is specified, the proposition is 'muhmala' according to the later scholars. The author then discusses a debate: the later scholars claim that muhmala entails particularity (juz'iyya), while the ancients did not consider it. The author argues that this entailment is not valid because muhmala can also apply to natural propositions where judgments do not extend to individuals. The objection by al-Dawwani that the individuals considered in particularity include conventional as well as real individuals does not resolve the issue, as there are judgments in muhmala that do not apply to individuals at all, e.g., 'Man is the subject of the absolute.' Thus, the claim of entailment is either rejected or requires interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Propositions with universal subjects are divided into absolute (muhmala), natural (tabi'iyya), and quantified (mahsuura), depending on how the universal is considered.

The Debate on Muhmala and Particularity
Author classifies propositions by subject type: particular, universal conditional/unconditional, quantified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the debate on whether an absolute proposition (muhmala) necessarily entails particularity (juz'iyya) and evaluate the arguments.

Core Idea
The later claim that muhmala entails particularity is disputed; the author shows it fails because muhmala can apply to natural propositions where no individual extension occurs.

After classifying propositions, the author enters a critical discussion about the relationship between muhmala and particularity. This debate is not just academic—it affects how we interpret unquantified statements in Islamic texts. Understanding the disagreement sharpens your ability to evaluate logical claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the later scholars' position
The later scholars claimed that muhmala entails particularity (juz'iyya). That is, an absolute proposition like 'Human is living' implies 'Some human is living'.
This is based on the idea that without a quantifier, the proposition is taken as referring to some individuals.
2
Find the author's objection
The author argues that muhmala also applies to natural propositions (tabi'iyya) where judgments do not extend to individuals. For example, 'Man is the subject of the absolute' is a muhmala proposition, but it does not entail 'Some man is the subject of the absolute' because this judgment is about the concept, not individuals.
This shows the entailment fails.
3
Examine al-Dawwani's attempted solution
Al-Dawwani argued that the individuals considered in particularity include conventional (i'tibari) individuals, so 'Some man is a universal' is true in a sense. However, the author says this does not solve the problem because some muhmala judgments simply do not apply to any individuals, real or conventional.
The example 'Man is the subject of the absolute' is a judgment about the concept itself, not about instances.
4
Conclude the author's verdict
The author states that the claim of entailment is either rejected (mardud) or requires interpretation (mu'awwal). So we cannot automatically assume an absolute proposition implies a particular one.
This is a nuanced position that requires careful logical analysis.

Quantifier Placement and Deviated Propositions
Definition of quantifier and deviated proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a deviated proposition by locating the quantifier on the predicate side and distinguish it from a standard proposition.

Core Idea
The quantifier can shift to the predicate, creating a deviated proposition—a rare but important twist.

In this lesson, we explore the quantifier (سور) and its placement. The author explains that sometimes the quantifier is attached to the predicate, producing a 'deviated proposition' (منحرفة). While such propositions are uncommon, they test your ability to read logical structure in Arabic. We will walk through the definition and examples so you can recognize them confidently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier
Look for the word that indicates quantity, such as 'كل' (every) or 'بعض' (some). In standard propositions, it precedes the subject.
2
Check the quantifier's position
If the quantifier appears next to the predicate rather than the subject, the proposition is deviated. Example: 'الحيوان كل إنسان' (Every human is an animal) — here 'كل' is attached to the predicate 'حيوان'.
In Arabic word order, the predicate often comes first in nominal sentences.
3
Recognize the deviated label
Once you confirm the quantifier is on the predicate side, classify the proposition as 'munḥarifah' (deviated).
Some later texts use the term for any non-standard quantifier placement.
4
Assess its occurrence
The author notes that deviated propositions are rarely used and have little benefit in lengthy discussions. Accept this as a marginal form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْبَيَانُ، أي بيان الكمية يُسَمَّى سُورًا، وَقَدْ يُذْكَرُ السُّورُ جَانِبِ الْمَحْمُولِ، نحو الحيوان كل إنسان، فَتُسَمَّى الْقَضِيَّةُ مُنْحَرِفَةٌ، وتفصيلها مع قلة الجدوى في المطولات. وَإِنْ لَمْ تُبَيَّنْ كمية الأفراد فَمُهْمَلَةٌ عِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ. اعلم: أن القدماء لم يعتبروها؛ لأن اعتبار الجزئية يغني عن اعتبارها، لكن ما فعله المصنف حسن، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالُوا إِنَّهَا تُلَازِمُ الْجُزْئِيَّةَ. هذا بظاهره يدل على أن القول بالتلازم بينهما إنما وقع من المتأخرين، وليس الأمر كذلك؛ فإن الشيخ قد صرح في تصانيفه بالتلازم. نعم، يرد عليهم أنها لا تصلح لملازمة الجزئية؛ إذ تصدق في مادة الطبيعية أيضا، وما قال المحقق الدواني رحمة الله، من أن الأفراد المعتبرة في الجزئية أعم من الاعتبارية والحقيقية، ففي الإنسان كلي يصدق بعض الإنسان كلي، فمع أنه خلاف مصرحاتهم لا يفي بالمقصود؛ فإنه ربما يحكم فيها بأحكام لا يسري إلى الأفراد، حقيقية كانت أو اعتبارية، كقولنا الإنسان موضوع المهملة، فالحق أن القول بتلازم الجزئية والمهملة مردود أو مأول.
Explanation, i.e. explanation of quantity, is called a quantifier. And sometimes the quantifier is mentioned on the side of the predicate, like 'every human is an animal', so the proposition is called deviated. Its detail, with little benefit, is in lengthy books. And if the quantity of individuals is not specified, then it is unquantified according to the later scholars. Know that the earlier scholars did not consider it because considering particularity suffices for considering it, but what the author did is good. And from that they said that it entails particularity. This apparently indicates that the claim of entailment between them only occurred from the later scholars. Yes, one may object to them that it is not suitable for entailment of particularity because it is also true in the matter of nature. Commentary
This passage defines two types of propositions based on quantifiers. First, the quantifier (سور) is sometimes placed on the predicate side, resulting in 'deviated' propositions (منحرفة) like 'every human is an animal' where the quantifier attaches to the predicate. Then it discusses unquantified propositions (مهملة) where the quantity is not specified. The commentator notes that earlier logicians did not treat them as separate because particularity was sufficient, but the author (صاحب السلم) rightly includes them. A debate arises: do unquantified propositions entail particularity? The commentator argues that this entailment was asserted by earlier masters like Ibn Sina, not only later ones. However, an objection is raised: unquantified propositions can hold in 'material of nature' (مادة الطبيعية), i.e., essential predications, so they do not necessarily imply particularity. Al-Dawani attempted to salvage the entailment by expanding the notion of individuals, but this is criticized because judgments in unquantified propositions may not apply to any individuals at all, e.g., 'human is a subject' does not refer to individual humans. Thus, the entailment claim is rejected or needs interpretation.

Unquantified Propositions and the Entailment Debate
Definition of unquantified proposition and entailment controversy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the debate on whether unquantified propositions entail particularity and evaluate the arguments from early and later logicians.

Core Idea
Unquantified propositions seem to imply some individuals, but they can speak of the nature itself—so entailment is contested.

This lesson dives into the second part of the passage: unquantified propositions (مهملة) and the dispute about whether they automatically entail particularity. Early logicians ignored them, later logicians treated them as equivalent to particular, but the commentator raises a powerful objection: they can be true in purely essential predications. We will also see al-Dawani's attempted defense and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Define unquantified proposition
An unquantified proposition (مهملة) is one where the quantity of the subject is not specified—it does not say 'every' or 'some'. Example: 'الإنسان حيوان' (Man is an animal) without a quantifier.
In Arabic, it can be just a subject-predicate sentence without a quantifier word.
2
Understand the historical difference
Early logicians (المتقدمون) did not treat it as a separate type, considering particularity sufficient. Later logicians (المتأخرون) included it and claimed it entails particularity (الجزئية).
The commentator notes that even earlier masters like Ibn Sina held the entailment view.
3
Examine the entailment claim
The claim: if 'الإنسان حيوان' (Man is an animal) is true, then 'بعض الإنسان حيوان' (Some man is an animal) is also true. The commentator objects: unquantified can be true in 'مادة الطبيعية' (essential predications) where the predicate holds of the nature, not individuals, e.g., 'الإنسان نوع' (Man is a species) does not entail that some man is a species (since individual man is not a species).
This refutes automatic entailment.
4
Critique al-Dawani's defense
Al-Dawani argued that 'individuals' in particularity include conceptual individuals (like the universal 'human' itself considered as an individual). But the commentator rejects this: even then, judgments in unquantified propositions may not apply to any individuals, e.g., 'الإنسان موضوع' (Man is a subject) in a logical context does not refer to real or conceptual individuals in a way that warrants entailment.
5
Conclude the debate
The commentator ends with: 'the truth is that the claim of entailment between the unquantified and particular is rejected or interpreted.' So you must be cautious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْبَيَانُ، أي بيان الكمية يُسَمَّى سُورًا، وَقَدْ يُذْكَرُ السُّورُ جَانِبِ الْمَحْمُولِ، نحو الحيوان كل إنسان، فَتُسَمَّى الْقَضِيَّةُ مُنْحَرِفَةٌ، وتفصيلها مع قلة الجدوى في المطولات. وَإِنْ لَمْ تُبَيَّنْ كمية الأفراد فَمُهْمَلَةٌ عِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ. اعلم: أن القدماء لم يعتبروها؛ لأن اعتبار الجزئية يغني عن اعتبارها، لكن ما فعله المصنف حسن، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالُوا إِنَّهَا تُلَازِمُ الْجُزْئِيَّةَ. هذا بظاهره يدل على أن القول بالتلازم بينهما إنما وقع من المتأخرين، وليس الأمر كذلك؛ فإن الشيخ قد صرح في تصانيفه بالتلازم. نعم، يرد عليهم أنها لا تصلح لملازمة الجزئية؛ إذ تصدق في مادة الطبيعية أيضا، وما قال المحقق الدواني رحمة الله، من أن الأفراد المعتبرة في الجزئية أعم من الاعتبارية والحقيقية، ففي الإنسان كلي يصدق بعض الإنسان كلي، فمع أنه خلاف مصرحاتهم لا يفي بالمقصود؛ فإنه ربما يحكم فيها بأحكام لا يسري إلى الأفراد، حقيقية كانت أو اعتبارية، كقولنا الإنسان موضوع المهملة، فالحق أن القول بتلازم الجزئية والمهملة مردود أو مأول.
Explanation, i.e. explanation of quantity, is called a quantifier. And sometimes the quantifier is mentioned on the side of the predicate, like 'every human is an animal', so the proposition is called deviated. Its detail, with little benefit, is in lengthy books. And if the quantity of individuals is not specified, then it is unquantified according to the later scholars. Know that the earlier scholars did not consider it because considering particularity suffices for considering it, but what the author did is good. And from that they said that it entails particularity. This apparently indicates that the claim of entailment between them only occurred from the later scholars. But that is not the case, for the master explicitly stated the entailment in his writings. Yes, one may object to them that it is not suitable for entailment of particularity because it is also true in the matter of nature. And what the investigator al-Dawani said, that the individuals considered in particularity are more general than the conceptual and real, so in 'human is a universal', 'some human is a universal' is true, but although contrary to their explicit statements, it does not fulfill the purpose; for sometimes judgments are made in it that do not extend to individuals, whether real or conceptual, like our statement 'human is a subject' in the unquantified. So the truth is that the claim of entailment between particularity and the unquantified is rejected or interpreted.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage defines two types of propositions based on quantifiers. First, the quantifier (سور) is sometimes placed on the predicate side, resulting in 'deviated' propositions (منحرفة) like 'every human is an animal' where the quantifier attaches to the predicate. Then it discusses unquantified propositions (مهملة) where the quantity is not specified. The commentator notes that earlier logicians did not treat them as separate because particularity was sufficient, but the author (صاحب السلم) rightly includes them. A debate arises: do unquantified propositions entail particularity? The commentator argues that this entailment was asserted by earlier masters like Ibn Sina, not only later ones. However, an objection is raised: unquantified propositions can hold in 'material of nature' (مادة الطبيعية), i.e., essential predications, so they do not necessarily imply particularity. Al-Dawani attempted to salvage the entailment by expanding the notion of individuals, but this is criticized because judgments in unquantified propositions may not apply to any individuals at all, e.g., 'human is a subject' does not refer to individual humans. Thus, the entailment claim is rejected or needs interpretation.
