Position of Ahl al-Tahqiq on Judgment in Quantified Propositions
Author states the position of ahl al-tahqiq on quantified propositions: judgment is on the essence accidentally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the ahl al-tahqiq position and the later scholars' position on the subject of judgment in quantified propositions, and explain the epistemological reasoning using bi-al-dhat and bi-al-'arad.

Core Idea
Judgment is on the essence directly; individuals are judged only accidentally.

The ahl al-tahqiq hold a nuanced view: in quantified propositions, judgment is upon the essence (haqiqa) itself, but conditioned by its application to individuals. This contrasts with later scholars who place judgment directly on individuals. The reasoning hinges on the directness of knowledge: the essence is known essentially (bi-al-dhat), while particulars are known accidentally (bi-al-'arad) through the essence. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Locate in the Arabic the statement of the ahl al-tahqiq position and the contrasting later scholars' position. The ahl al-tahqiq judge on the essence itself; the later scholars judge on individuals.
The phrase 'خلافا للمتأخرين' signals the contrast.
2
Understand key terms: haqiqa and afrad
Haqiqa is the essence as a mental concept; afrad are the particular instances falling under that concept. Note that haqiqa here is the mental form, not external reality.
3
Grasp the epistemological distinction: bi-al-dhat vs bi-al-'arad
Knowledge of the essence is direct (bi-al-dhat) because it is the form obtained in the mind. Knowledge of particulars is indirect (bi-al-'arad) because it is through knowing the essence. The passage says: 'العلم الحصول في الذهن'.
This is based on the principle that the aspect in knowledge is known essentially, the thing with the aspect accidentally.
4
Apply to the condition of judgment
Judgment requires conceptualizing the subject. Since the essence is directly conceptualized, it is the subject essentially; individuals are only conceptualized via the essence, so they are subjects accidentally.
The phrase 'فَلَيْسَتْ الأفراد مَحْكُوما عَلَيْهَا إِلَّا كَذَلِكَ' confirms individuals are judged only accidentally.
5
Reinterpret a quantified statement
For a statement like 'Every human is rational', the ahl al-tahqiq hold that the judgment is on the essence of humanity (from the aspect of its individuals), not on each individual directly. This affects how the universal proposition is understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذْهَبَ أَهْلِ التَّحْقِيقِ، كالمحقق الدواني رحمة الله وأتباعه أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْمَحْصُورَةِ عَلَى نَفْسِ الْحَقِيقَةِ، لكن من حيث الانطباق على الأشخاص، ونسب هذا إلى القدماء، خلافا للمتأخرين؛ إذ عندهم الحكم على الأفراد؛ لأنها الحقيقة هي الْحَاصِلَةُ في الذَّهْنِ حَقِيقَة، أي من غير واسطة في العروض، فهي معلومة بالذات؛ لأن العلم الحصول في الذهن، وَالْجُزْئِيَّاتُ مَعْلُومَةٌ بِالْعَرَضِ، أي بواسطة معلومية الحقيقة؛ بناء على أن الوجه في العلم بالوجه معلوم بالذات، وذا الوجه معلوم بالعرض، وإذا كان من شرط الحكم تصور المحكوم عليه فَلَيْسَتْ الأفراد مَحْكُوما عَلَيْهَا إِلَّا كَذَلِكَ، أي بالعرض، وكانت الحقيقة محكوما عليها بالذات.
Know that the doctrine of the people of verification, like al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani (may Allah have mercy on him) and his followers, is that in the quantified proposition the judgment is upon the essence itself, but from the aspect of its application to individuals. This is attributed to the ancients, contrary to the later scholars; for with them the judgment is upon the individuals, because the essence is the concept obtained in the mind truly, i.e., without a medium in predication, so it is known essentially; for knowledge is obtaining in the mind. The particulars are known accidentally, i.e., through the knowledge of the essence; based on the principle that the aspect in knowledge is known essentially, and the thing with the aspect is known accidentally. And since it is a condition of judgment to conceptualize the subject of judgment, the individuals are not judged upon except in that manner, i.e., accidentally, and the essence is judged upon essentially.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage explains the doctrine of the people of verification (ahl al-tahqiq) regarding quantified propositions (mahsurah). They hold that the judgment is on the essence (haqiqa) itself, but with the condition that it is considered from the aspect of its applicability to individuals. This is attributed to the ancients (qudama), while the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) hold that judgment is on the individuals directly. The author then explains why: the essence is known essentially (bi-al-dhat) because it is the form in the mind, while particulars are known accidentally (bi-al-'arad) through the essence. Thus, the individuals are judged upon accidentally, and the essence is judged upon essentially.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): Judgment, the assertion of a predicate to a subject. [Note: Stable term across logic.]
المحصورة (al-mahsurah): A proposition with a quantifier (all, some, none). [Note: Standard.]
الحقيقة (al-haqiqa): The essence or quiddity considered as a mental concept. [Note: In some texts, haqiqa is used for real essence, but here it is the mental concept.]
الأفراد (al-afrad): The particular instances that fall under a universal concept. [Note: Stable.]
بالذات (bi-al-dhat): Essentially, referring to direct knowledge. [Note: These terms are used in epistemology and logic; here they distinguish between direct and indirect knowledge of entities.]
بالعرض (bi-al-'arad): Accidentally, referring to indirect knowledge. [Note: Contrasted with bi-al-dhat.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The essence (haqiqa) of humanity
B) Each individual human
C) The concept 'human' in the mind
D) The external existing human

Key Takeaway
The ahl al-tahqiq teach that in quantified propositions, judgment is on the essence essentially and on individuals accidentally, based on the distinction between direct knowledge of the universal concept and indirect knowledge of particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting an Objection on Quantified Propositions
Author presents and refutes an objection to the ahl al-tahqiq view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the objection to the ahl al-tahqiq view on quantified propositions and evaluate the author's refutation.

Core Idea
The subject of a quantified proposition is the quiddity as applicable to individuals, not a third entity.

In this passage, the author responds to a commentator's objection to the view of ahl al-tahqiq regarding the subject of quantified propositions. The objector raises several difficulties, but the author shows each leads to absurdity, thereby defending the original position. This exchange demonstrates the rigorous dialectical method used in Islamic logic to refine doctrinal positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position being attacked
The ahl al-tahqiq hold that the subject of a quantified proposition is 'the nature from the aspect of application to individuals' (al-tabi'a min hayth al-intibaq 'ala al-afrad). Understand what this means: the quiddity considered as potentially applying to its individuals.
2
Outline the objector's arguments
The objector considers possible interpretations: if it is a restrictive compound, every subject becomes compound; if it is a mental quiddity, the proposition becomes mental; if it is external quiddity, it becomes indefinite; if it is individuals, it is not suitable. Note each consequence.
The objector systematically eliminates options.
3
Track the objector's final alternative
The objector asks if there is a third rank in external reality expressed by the restrictive compound. The author refutes this by showing such a rank would be either a universal (making the proposition indefinite) or different in each individual (reducing to individuals).
4
Assess the author's refutation
The author argues the objection is disordered: in external reality, the quiddity is suitable for application to individuals, and it is more specific than the subject of indefinite propositions. The so-called 'indefinite of later scholars' is irrelevant.
The author identifies a category error in the objection.
5
Identify the refined position
The ahl al-tahqiq's view is refined: the subject is the quiddity itself, but considered with respect to its applicability to individuals, not as a compound or separate entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا أبحاث، الأول: ما أورده بعض الشراح، وقال: لا أفقه ماذا يريدون، إن أرادوا بالطبيعة من حيث الانطباق على أفراد هذا المركب التقييدي فهو باطل، وإلا يلزم أن يكون كل موضوع مركبا. وإن أرادوا مرتبة من مراتب الماهية فهي إما عبارة عن الماهية من حيث وجودها في الذهن بوجود ينسب إلى الأفراد. وعلى هذا ينحصر القضية المحصورة في الذهنية. وأيضا في ثبوت المحمول لهذه المرتبة كلام؛ إذ الظاهر أن المحمول لا يثبت إلا لموجود خارجي. وإما عبارة عن موجود خارجي، فظاهر أن الموجود في الخارج إما نفس الماهية أو أفراده، وعلى الأول يصير القضية مهملة، وعلى الثاني لا يصلح للحكم على رأيهم.
فإن قلت: لعل في الخارج مرتبة سوى مرتبة الماهية من والأفراد يعبر عنها بهذا المركب التقييدي؟
قلت: فهو إما معنى كلي مشترك في جميع الأفراد، أو في كل فرد من أفراده معنى مغاير لما في الفرد الآخر، فعلى الأول يصير القضية مهملة، وعلى الثاني حاله كحال الأفراد، انتهى كلامه ملخصا.
ولا يخفى ما فيه من الاختلال؛ فإن في الخارج الماهية بحيث يصلح للانطباق على الأفراد، وهي اعتبار أخص من موضوع المهملة. نعم، ينعقد منه مهملة المتأخرين، لكن لا يضرنا.
Then here are some investigations. First: What some commentators brought up, saying: I do not understand what they mean. If they mean by 'nature from the aspect of application to individuals' this restrictive compound, then it is false, otherwise every subject would be compound. And if they mean a rank among the ranks of quiddity, then it is either an expression of the quiddity from the aspect of its existence in the mind with an existence that relates to individuals; based on this, the quantified proposition reduces to a mental proposition. Also, there is discussion about the predicate being affirmed for this rank, since apparently the predicate is only affirmed for an external existent. Or it is an expression of an external existent, so it is apparent that the external existent is either the quiddity itself or its individuals; on the first, the proposition becomes indefinite; on the second, it is not suitable for judgment according to their view. If you say: Perhaps there is in the external a rank other than the rank of the quiddity and the individuals, expressed by this restrictive compound? I say: Then it is either a universal meaning common to all individuals, or in each individual there is a meaning different from that in the other individual; on the first, the proposition becomes indefinite; on the second, its case is like that of individuals. This summarizes his statement. And it is not hidden that it contains disorder; for in the external, the quiddity is such that it is suitable for applying to individuals, and it is a consideration more specific than the subject of the indefinite. Yes, it constitutes the indefinite of the later scholars, but that does not harm us.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection raised by some commentators against the doctrine of ahl al-tahqiq regarding quantified propositions. The objector questions the notion of 'nature from the aspect of application to individuals' which he calls a 'restrictive compound' (murakkab taqyidi). He argues that if this is what they mean, then every subject would be compound, which is absurd. Alternatively, if they mean a specific rank of the quiddity, he analyzes various possibilities: if it is the quiddity as existing in the mind with a relation to individuals, then every quantified proposition becomes mental (dhihniyya), which is problematic because predicates usually apply to external things. If it is the external quiddity itself, then the proposition becomes indefinite (muhmala). If it is the individuals, then it does not suit their view. The objector then asks if there is a third rank, and the author responds by showing that such a rank would either be a universal common to all individuals (making the proposition indefinite) or a different meaning in each individual (reducing it to individuals). The author then critiques this objection, stating that in external reality, the quiddity is such that it is suitable for application to individuals, and that this consideration is more specific than the subject of the indefinite proposition. He acknowledges that it forms the 'indefinite of the later scholars' (muhmala al-muta'akhkhirin), but that does not harm their position.

Key Takeaway
The subject of a quantified proposition is the quiddity considered as applicable to individuals, not a compound or external entity.

The Three Considerations of the Universal Subject
Author classifies the subject into particular and universal, and presents three considerations of the universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given proposition by identifying the consideration of its universal subject and naming the proposition type (shakhsiyya, muhmala, tabi'iyya, mahsurah).

Core Idea
The same universal term can serve as subject in three logically distinct ways, each yielding a different type of proposition.

In Islamic logic, the subject of a proposition can be particular or universal. For universal subjects, logicians distinguish three 'considerations' (i'tibarat) that determine how the subject is taken: in itself, isolated in the mind, or united with individuals. This classification underpins the different types of propositions—shakhsiyya, muhmala, tabi'iyya, and mahsurah. Mastering these distinctions is essential for analyzing the logical force of statements in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify whether the subject is particular or universal
Look at the subject term of the proposition. If it refers to a single individual (e.g., 'Zayd'), it is particular. If it applies to multiple individuals (e.g., 'human'), it is universal.
This initial division determines next steps.
2
For a universal subject, determine which consideration applies
The passage gives three considerations: (1) the thing itself without condition, (2) the quiddity isolated in the mind (bi-shart al-wahda al-dhihniyya), (3) the quiddity united with individuals mentally or externally. Examine how the subject is being used: as a bare essence, as a mental concept, or as applying to actual instances.
Consideration (1) is the broadest; (2) and (3) are more specific.
3
Match the consideration to the proposition type
Use the mapping from the passage: Particular subject → shakhsiyya (individual proposition). Universal by (1) → muhmala (indefinite). Universal by (2) → tabi'iyya (natural). Universal by (3) → mahsurah (quantified) or muhmala of the later scholars.
The muhmala of the ancients is by (1); the muhmala of the later scholars is by (3).
4
Check for quantifiers to confirm
If the proposition has a quantifier (all, some, etc.), it is mahsurah (third consideration). If it lacks a quantifier but still applies to individuals, it may be the later muhmala (also third consideration). If it lacks quantifier and does not apply to individuals, it is either natural (second) or indefinite (first).
Always refer back to the three considerations for precise classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شئت زيادة تفصيل فاستمع إلى التقسيم من الرأس، فنقول: الموضوع جزئي أو كلي، سواء كان ذاتيا أو عرضيا، وله اعتبارات ثلاثة: الأول نفس الشيء من حيث هي، الثاني الماهية المنحازة في الذهن، بحيث لا يمكن سراية أحكام الأفراد إليها، ويعبر عنها بالماهية بشرط الوحدة الذهنية، الثالث الماهية المنحازة المتحدة مع الأفراد ذهنا أو خارجا، محققا أو مقدرا، وهذان الاعتباران أخصان من الأول، فالجزئي موضوع الشخصية، والكلي بالاعتبار الأول موضوع المهملة وبالاعتبار الثاني موضوع الطبيعية، وبالاعتبار الثالث موضوع المحصورة ومهملة المتأخرين، فتأمل، وكن على بصيرة وتوكل على الله؛ فإنه أعلم بالصواب.
And if you wish more detail, listen to the division from the beginning. We say: The subject is either particular or universal, whether essential or accidental. It has three considerations: First: the thing itself as it is. Second: the quiddity isolated in the mind, such that the judgments of individuals cannot extend to it; it is expressed as the quiddity under the condition of mental unity. Third: the quiddity isolated but united with individuals mentally or externally, actually or assumed. These two considerations are more specific than the first. So the particular is the subject of the individual proposition (shakhsiyya). The universal, by the first consideration, is the subject of the indefinite proposition (muhmala); by the second consideration, the subject of the natural proposition (tabi'iyya); and by the third consideration, the subject of the quantified proposition (mahsurah) and the indefinite of the later scholars (muhmala al-muta'akhkhirin). So reflect, and be upon insight, and rely on Allah, for He knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of the subject of propositions. The subject is either particular (juz'i) or universal (kulli). The universal has three considerations: (1) The thing itself (nafs al-shay'), without any condition, which is the broadest; (2) The quiddity isolated (munhaza) in the mind, such that the judgments of individuals cannot apply to it; this is called 'quiddity under the condition of mental unity' (al-mahiyya bi-shart al-wahda al-dhihniyya); (3) The quiddity isolated but united with individuals, either mentally or externally, actually or assumed. These correspond to different types of propositions: the particular is the subject of the individual proposition (shakhsiyya). The universal by the first consideration (without condition) is the subject of the indefinite proposition (muhmala). By the second consideration (isolated in mind) it is the subject of the natural proposition (tabi'iyya). By the third consideration (united with individuals) it is the subject of the quantified proposition (mahsurah) and also the indefinite as understood by later scholars (muhmala al-muta'akhkhirin). The author emphasizes that the latter two considerations are more specific than the first.

Key Terms
الموضوع

Subject of Judgment: Individuals vs. Essences
Author argues that opposite attributes cannot coexist, and judgment by essence is on individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why universal judgments ultimately refer to individual entities when opposite attributes are involved.

Core Idea
Opposite attributes prove that universal judgments refer to individuals, not essences.

This lesson unpacks two key points from the passage: (1) two opposite attributes cannot belong to the same essence simultaneously, and (2) judgment by essence necessarily applies to individuals. These insights are foundational for understanding how universal propositions relate to the real world in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
The passage contains two distinct points: first, that opposite attributes (like sleeper and awake) cannot coexist in the same subject; second, that we necessarily judge some propositions by essence, and that subject must be individuals.
2
Analyze the example of opposite attributes
The author gives the example 'Every sleeper is awake'. Sleeper and awake are mutually exclusive. If the subject of the judgment were the universal essence (e.g., 'human'), then the same essence would have contradictory attributes, which is impossible. Therefore, the subject must be different individuals at different times.
3
Understand why essences cannot be the subject
If the subject were the essence, contradictory predicates could not apply to it. But we do make judgments like 'Every sleeper is awake' meaning that any individual who is a sleeper at one time is awake at another. This forces us to treat the subject as the individuals themselves.
4
Recognize the necessity of judging by individuals
The fourth point asserts that some judgments are by essence (by themselves) and denying this is obstinacy. The subject of such judgments must be the individuals, because only individuals can bear changing or contradictory attributes while remaining the same entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن الوصفين قد يتنافيان، كقولنا كل نائم مستيقظ، فكيف يصح الحكم بثبوت المحمول المنافي لحقيقة الموضوع لها، والتزام ذلك ولو بالعرض - بعيد عن الإنصاف، كيف، وليس وصف الموضوع متحدا الأشخاص حين ثبوت وصف المحمول. مع الرابع: أنا نحكم ضرورة في بعض القضايا بالثبوت بالذات، وإنكار هذا مكابرة ، فلا بد من أن يكون المحكوم عليه بالذات الأشخاص.
Third: That the two attributes may be mutually exclusive, like our saying 'Every sleeper is awake'. So how can it be correct to judge the affirmation of a predicate that is contrary to the reality of the subject for it? And committing to that, even by accident, is far from fairness. How so, while the subject of the attribute is not identical in persons at the time of the affirmation of the predicate's attribute? And Fourth: That we judge necessarily in some propositions by essence, and denial of this is obstinacy. So necessarily the subject of judgment by itself is the individuals.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth points in the author's argument about the subject of judgment. The third point deals with opposite attributes (وصفين متنافيين). The author gives the example 'Every sleeper is awake' (كل نائم مستيقظ) — here 'sleeper' and 'awake' are mutually exclusive attributes. How can a judgment attribute a predicate contrary to the reality of the subject? The subject (e.g., the person) is not identical in its individuals at the moment of the predicate's affirmation. This suggests that the judgment cannot refer to the essence alone, because the essence does not change; it must refer to the individuals that bear contradictory attributes at different times. The fourth point is a direct assertion: we necessarily judge in some propositions by essence (بالثبات بالذات), and denying this is obstinacy. Therefore, the subject of judgment by itself (المحكوم عليه بالذات) must be the individuals (الأشخاص). This reinforces that in logic, universal judgments ultimately apply to individual entities.

Key Terms
وصفان متنافيان
ثبوت بالذات

The Subject of Judgment: Nature vs. Individuals
Author refutes Mirzajan's objection on the subject of judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the author's position on the subject of judgment and reconstruct his refutation of Mirzajan's objection.

Core Idea
The subject of judgment is individuals, not nature; nature is a way of knowing them.

In this dialectical passage, the author defends the view that the individuals (al-afrad) are the true subjects of judgment (mahkum 'alayhi bi al-dhat), against the objection of Mirzajan that the universal nature (al-tabi'a) should be the subject because individuals are 'absolutely unknown'. The debate centres on how we know the subject and whether judgment requires essential conception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's initial thesis
Find where the author states that obtaining by accident and attention by essence is sufficient for judgment. This sets up his position that the individuals, known by their face (wajh), can be subjects.
2
Extract Mirzajan's objection
Mirzajan argues that the face (wajh) is other than the possessor of the face (dhu al-wajh), so the individuals are absolutely unknown by essence. He claims judgment on the impossible, so nature must be the subject.
3
Analyse the author's refutation
The author distinguishes two meanings of 'absolutely unknown': (1) not obtained at all – he denies individuals are such; (2) not obtained by itself – he denies that judgment is impossible. He calls Mirzajan's argument a 'corrupt construction' (bina' fasid).
4
Summarise the dialectical outcome
The author concludes that the individuals, though known through the face (nature), are the true subjects of judgment. The nature serves as a means of knowledge but is not the primary subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم القضية باعتبار الموضوع
الحصول بالعرض والالتفات بالذات كاف للحكم وما قال ميرزاجان: إن معنى كون ذي الوجه ملتفتا إليه بالذات أن الوجه ملتفت إليه من حيث اتحاده مع ذي الوجه، فمع كونه بعيدا عن عباراتهم مصادم للضرورة، كما لا يخفى
والقول بأن المحكوم عليه بالذات يجب تصوره بالذات، ولا يكفي الالتفات بالذات غير تام ؛ فإن ذلك لم يُبَرْهَن عليه، بل الذي لا بد للحكم الاستقلال ، وذلك باللحاظ والالتفات حاصل.
وما قال ميرزاجان إن حصول الوجه غير حصول ذي الوجه، فهو مجهول مطلق بالذات؛ لعدم تعلق العلم به حقيقة، ومعلوم بالعرض، والمجهول المطلق يمتنع عليه الحكم، فالطبيعة محكوم عليها بالذات، دون الأفراد. وبناء على هذا قال: لا بد أن يحصل المعرف أولا بنفسه حين الكسب، ثم يطلب تصوره بوجه آخر ، حدا كان أو رسما غير سديد ؛ فإنه إن أراد بالمجهول المطلق ما لا يحصل بوجه ولا بنفسه فلا نسلم أن الأفراد مجهولة مطلقا بهذا المعنى، وإن أراد ما لا يحصل بنفسه، وإن حصل بوجهه فلا نسلم امتناع الحكم، كيف وذلك ليس ضروريا ولا مبرهنا عليه، بل هو أول النزاع، والتفريع بناء فاسد على فاسد، هذا، والله أعلم.
الثالث: أن الوصفين قد يتنافيان، كقولنا كل نائم مستيقظ، فكيف يصح الحكم بثبوت المحمول المنافي لحقيقة الموضوع لها، والتزام ذلك ولو بالعرض - بعيد عن الإنصاف، كيف، وليس وصف الموضوع متحدا الأشخاص حين ثبوت وصف المحمول.
مع
الرابع: أنا نحكم ضرورة في بعض القضايا بالثبوت بالذات، وإنكار هذا مكابرة ، فلا بد من أن يكون المحكوم عليه بالذات الأشخاص.
الخامس أن كل واحد من الأشخاص أخص من الطبيعة؛ لتحققها في البعض فقط ، بدون الكل، فيكون المحمول عرضا غريبا للموضوع، فلا تقع القضية حينئذ مسألة برهانية. الجواب : أن حقيقة الموضوع قد يكون معروضة للمحمول بالذات، إذا كانت نوعا للأشخاص، والأشخاص بواسطتها، كقولنا النار حار يابس ، أو بواسطة أمر مساو للنوع ، كقولنا «ما ليس فيه مبدأ مميل مستقيم، لا يقبل الخرق ، فحينئذ تقع مسألة برهانية. نعم، في بعض القضايا ليس الأمر كذلك ، فيلزم عدم وقوعها مسألة برهانية ، فتأمل.
السادس ما أشار إليه بقوله: (وَرُبَّمَا يَتَرَأَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ)، أي الحكم على الحقيقة (لَاقْتَضَى الْإِيجَابُ وُجُودَ الْحَقِيقَةِ حَقِيقَةٌ ; فَإِنَّ) الإيجاب يستدعي وجود المثبت له ، وأن الْمُثْبَتَ لَهُ هُوَ الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ حَقِيقَةٌ، مَعَ) أن التالي باطل؛ إذ (إِنَّهَا قَدْ تَكُونُ عَدَمِيَّةً)، كما في المعدولة الموضوع، (بَلْ سَلْبِيَّةٌ)، كما في سالبة الموضوع، فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْأَفْرَادَ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةً بِالوَجْهِ، لَكِنَّهَا مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهَا حَقِيقَةٌ ، أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى الْوَضْعِ الْعَام وَالْمَوْضُوع لَهُ الْخَاصَّ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَعْلُومَ بِالْوَجْهِ هُوَ الْمَوْضُوعُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ .
وَالْجَوَابُ ): أن اتحاد المثبت له والمحكوم عليه مطلقا ممنوع، كيف، و (إِنَّ مُفَادَ الْإِيجَابِ مُطْلَقًا هُوَ النُّبُوتُ مُطْلَقًا)، سواء كانت بالذات أو بالعرض، (وَكُلُّ حُكْمِ ثَابِتٍ لِلْأَفْرَادِ ثَابِتٌ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ)، أو بالعرض فيكون صالحة لكونها محكوما عليها بالذات، مع عدم صلوحها لكونها مثبتا لها بالذات .
(وَأَمَّا أَنَّهُ لِمَاذَا أَوَّلًا وَبِالذَّاتِ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ أَوْ لِلْفَرْدِ؟ فَمَنْهُومٌ زَائِدٌ عَلَى الْحَقِيقَةِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ).
Division of the proposition with respect to the subject. Obtaining by accident and attention by essence is sufficient for judgment. What Mirzajan said: that the meaning of 'the possessor of the face being attended to by essence' is that the face is attended to from the aspect of its union with the possessor of the face, so while it is far from their expressions, it contradicts necessity, as is not hidden. And the statement that what is judged upon by essence must be conceived by essence, and attention by essence is not sufficient, is incomplete; for that has not been demonstrated, rather what is necessary for judgment is independence, and that is attained by regard and attention. And what Mirzajan said: that the obtaining of the face is other than the obtaining of the possessor of the face, so it is absolutely unknown by essence, because knowledge does not attach to it in reality, and it is known by accident, and judgment on the absolutely unknown is impossible, so the nature is judged upon by essence, not the individuals. And based on this he said: it is necessary that the known be first obtained by itself at the time of acquisition, then its conception is sought by another face, whether a definition or a description, is not sound; for if he means by the absolutely unknown what is not obtained by any face nor by itself, then we do not concede that individuals are absolutely unknown in this sense; and if he means what is not obtained by itself, even if obtained by its face, then we do not concede the impossibility of judgment, how so, for that is neither necessary nor demonstrated, rather it is the very point of dispute, and the branching is a corrupt construction upon a corrupt one. This, and God knows best. Third: that the two descriptions may be contradictory, like our saying 'every sleeper is awake', so how can judgment be valid of the predicate's affirmation of the contrary of the reality of the subject for it, and adherence to that even by accident is far from fairness. How, and the description of the subject is not united in individuals at the time of the predicate's description. With the Fourth: that we judge necessarily in some propositions by essential affirmation, and denial of this is obstinacy, so the subject of judgment by essence must be the individuals. Fifth: that each individual is more specific than the nature, because it (the nature) is realized in some only, not all, so the predicate would be an accidental attribute for the subject, so the proposition would not then be a demonstrative issue. The answer: that the reality of the subject may be the subject of the predicate by essence, if it is a type for the individuals, and the individuals through it, like our saying 'fire is hot dry', or by means of something coextensive with the type, like our saying 'that which has no principle of straight inclination does not accept tearing', so then it becomes a demonstrative issue. Yes, in some propositions this is not the case, so it follows that they are not demonstrative issues, so reflect. Sixth: what he alluded to by his saying: 'Perhaps it appears that if it were so', i.e., the judgment on the reality, 'then the affirmative would necessitate the existence of the reality actually; for' affirmation demands the existence of that for which something is affirmed, and that that for which something is affirmed is the subject of judgment in reality, 'with' the consequent being false; since 'it may be non-existent', as in the subject modified by a privative, 'rather negative', as in the subject of a negative proposition. So the truth is that the individuals, even if known by the face, are truly judged upon. Do you not see the common term and the specific referent; for the known by the face is the true referent. And the answer: that the identity of the affirmed-for and the judged-upon absolutely is denied. How, and 'the import of affirmation absolutely is affirmation absolutely', whether essentially or accidentally, 'and every judgment established for individuals is established for the nature in general', or accidentally, so it is fit to be judged upon by essence, while not being fit to be the affirmed-for by essence. 'And as for why primarily and by essence it is for the nature or for the individual? That is a concept additional to reality, so reflect.'
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical commentary on a core issue in logic: whether the subject of a proposition (al-mahkum 'alayhi) is the nature (tabi'a) or the individuals (afrad). The author, in the style of a sharh, presents several objections and their answers. The passage begins by stating that obtaining by accident and attention by essence is sufficient for judgment, and critiques Mirzajan's view that the possessor of the face (dhu al-wajh) is attended to by essence. The author argues that Mirzajan's position contradicts necessity and lacks demonstration. There follows a series of objections (third, fourth, fifth, sixth) challenging the view that judgment is on the individuals. The third objection notes that contradictory predicates cannot hold for the same subject; the fourth insists that necessary judgments require the individuals as subjects; the fifth argues that individuals are more specific than the nature, making the predicate accidental; the sixth points out that affirmative judgments would require the existence of the nature if it were the subject. The author responds to each: the third and fourth are implied to be answered by the nature of judgment; the fifth is answered by showing that the nature can be the subject of a demonstrative issue when it is a type (naw' ) for individuals; the sixth is answered by distinguishing between the affirmed-for (muthbat lahu) and the judged-upon (mahkum 'alayhi), and by noting that universal affirmative judgments do not require the existence of the subject in extra-mental reality. All examples come from Islamic sciences: 'fire is hot dry' is a standard physical example; 'every sleeper is awake' illustrates contradiction; 'that which has no principle of straight inclination does not accept tearing' is a geometric example from Aristotelian physics. The commentary follows the order of the Arabic, explaining each objection and its refutation.

الحكم بالعرض
محكوم عليه بالذات
الطبيعة

ذي الوجه

Key Takeaway
The subject of judgment is the individuals, known through their face (attributes), not the universal nature; the debate turns on what it means to be 'absolutely unknown'.

Refuting the Objections: From Contradictory Predicates to Demonstrative Issues
Author continues refuting objections to the claim that individuals are the subjects of judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the author refutes each objection concerning contradictory predicates, necessary judgments, and the requirements for demonstrative propositions.

Core Idea
The author systematically refutes each objection by clarifying the relationship between nature and individuals in judgment.

After dealing with Mirzajan, the author presents a series of objections (third, fourth, fifth, sixth) that challenge the view that individuals are the subjects of judgment. The third objection points to contradictory predicates, the fourth insists that necessary judgments require individuals, the fifth argues that individuals are too specific, and the sixth raises the problem of non-existent subjects. The author answers each in turn.

Guided Path
1
List the third and fourth objections
The third objection: contradictory predicates cannot apply to the same subject, so the nature must be the subject. The fourth: necessary judgments (e.g., the whole is greater than the part) require individuals as subjects.
2
Summarise the author's response to the third and fourth
The author responds to the third by noting that the description of the subject is not united across individuals at the time of the predicate's description. The fourth is actually used to support his own view, as it implies individuals are the subjects.
3
Analyse the fifth objection and answer
The fifth objection: individuals are more specific than the nature, so predicates become accidental, preventing demonstrative propositions. The author answers by giving examples where the nature serves as the subject of a demonstrative proposition, either as a type (naw') or coextensive (musawi).
Example: 'Fire is hot dry' is demonstrative because the nature is the subject.
4
Examine the sixth objection and answer
The sixth objection: affirmative propositions require the existence of the subject, so if the nature is the subject, it must exist; but it may be non-existent. The author answers by distinguishing between the affirmed-for (muthbat lahu) and the judged-upon (mahkum 'alayhi), noting that affirmation does not require existence of the subject.
5
Reflect on the dialectical strategy
The author consistently defends his position that individuals are the real subjects, while the nature serves as a means. He does not reject the nature entirely but shows its logical role.

تقسيم القضية باعتبار الموضوع
الحصول بالعرض والالتفات بالذات كاف للحكم وما قال ميرزاجان: إن معنى كون ذي الوجه ملتفتا إليه بالذات أن الوجه ملتفت إليه من حيث اتحاده مع ذي الوجه، فمع كونه بعيدا عن عباراتهم مصادم للضرورة، كما لا يخفى
والقول بأن المحكوم عليه بالذات يجب تصوره بالذات، ولا يكفي الالتفات بالذات غير تام ؛ فإن ذلك لم يُبَرْهَن عليه، بل الذي لا بد للحكم الاستقلال ، وذلك باللحاظ والالتفات حاصل.
وما قال ميرزاجان إن حصول الوجه غير حصول ذي الوجه، فهو مجهول مطلق بالذات؛ لعدم تعلق العلم به حقيقة، ومعلوم بالعرض، والمجهول المطلق يمتنع عليه الحكم، فالطبيعة محكوم عليها بالذات، دون الأفراد. وبناء على هذا قال: لا بد أن يحصل المعرف أولا بنفسه حين الكسب، ثم يطلب تصوره بوجه آخر ، حدا كان أو رسما غير سديد ؛ فإنه إن أراد بالمجهول المطلق ما لا يحصل بوجه ولا بنفسه فلا نسلم أن الأفراد مجهولة مطلقا بهذا المعنى، وإن أراد ما لا يحصل بنفسه، وإن حصل بوجهه فلا نسلم امتناع الحكم، كيف وذلك ليس ضروريا ولا مبرهنا عليه، بل هو أول النزاع، والتفريع بناء فاسد على فاسد، هذا، والله أعلم.
الثالث: أن الوصفين قد يتنافيان، كقولنا كل نائم مستيقظ، فكيف يصح الحكم بثبوت المحمول المنافي لحقيقة الموضوع لها، والتزام ذلك ولو بالعرض - بعيد عن الإنصاف، كيف، وليس وصف الموضوع متحدا الأشخاص حين ثبوت وصف المحمول.
مع
الرابع: أنا نحكم ضرورة في بعض القضايا بالثبوت بالذات، وإنكار هذا مكابرة ، فلا بد من أن يكون المحكوم عليه بالذات الأشخاص.
الخامس أن كل واحد من الأشخاص أخص من الطبيعة؛ لتحققها في البعض فقط ، بدون الكل، فيكون المحمول عرضا غريبا للموضوع، فلا تقع القضية حينئذ مسألة برهانية. الجواب : أن حقيقة الموضوع قد يكون معروضة للمحمول بالذات، إذا كانت نوعا للأشخاص، والأشخاص بواسطتها، كقولنا النار حار يابس ، أو بواسطة أمر مساو للنوع ، كقولنا «ما ليس فيه مبدأ مميل مستقيم، لا يقبل الخرق ، فحينئذ تقع مسألة برهانية. نعم، في بعض القضايا ليس الأمر كذلك ، فيلزم عدم وقوعها مسألة برهانية ، فتأمل.
السادس ما أشار إليه بقوله: (وَرُبَّمَا يَتَرَأَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ)، أي الحكم على الحقيقة (لَاقْتَضَى الْإِيجَابُ وُجُودَ الْحَقِيقَةِ حَقِيقَةٌ ; فَإِنَّ) الإيجاب يستدعي وجود المثبت له ، وأن الْمُثْبَتَ لَهُ هُوَ الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ حَقِيقَةٌ، مَعَ) أن التالي باطل؛ إذ (إِنَّهَا قَدْ تَكُونُ عَدَمِيَّةً)، كما في المعدولة الموضوع، (بَلْ سَلْبِيَّةٌ)، كما في سالبة الموضوع، فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْأَفْرَادَ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةً بِالوَجْهِ، لَكِنَّهَا مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهَا حَقِيقَةٌ ، أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى الْوَضْعِ الْعَام وَالْمَوْضُوع لَهُ الْخَاصَّ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَعْلُومَ بِالْوَجْهِ هُوَ الْمَوْضُوعُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ .
وَالْجَوَابُ ): أن اتحاد المثبت له والمحكوم عليه مطلقا ممنوع، كيف، و (إِنَّ مُفَادَ الْإِيجَابِ مُطْلَقًا هُوَ النُّبُوتُ مُطْلَقًا)، سواء كانت بالذات أو بالعرض، (وَكُلُّ حُكْمِ ثَابِتٍ لِلْأَفْرَادِ ثَابِتٌ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ)، أو بالعرض فيكون صالحة لكونها محكوما عليها بالذات، مع عدم صلوحها لكونها مثبتا لها بالذات .
(وَأَمَّا أَنَّهُ لِمَاذَا أَوَّلًا وَبِالذَّاتِ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ أَوْ لِلْفَرْدِ؟ فَمَنْهُومٌ زَائِدٌ عَلَى الْحَقِيقَةِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ).
Division of the proposition with respect to the subject. Obtaining by accident and attention by essence is sufficient for judgment. What Mirzajan said: that the meaning of 'the possessor of the face being attended to by essence' is that the face is attended to from the aspect of its union with the possessor of the face, so while it is far from their expressions, it contradicts necessity, as is not hidden. And the statement that what is judged upon by essence must be conceived by essence, and attention by essence is not sufficient, is incomplete; for that has not been demonstrated, rather what is necessary for judgment is independence, and that is attained by regard and attention. And what Mirzajan said: that the obtaining of the face is other than the obtaining of the possessor of the face, so it is absolutely unknown by essence, because knowledge does not attach to it in reality, and it is known by accident, and judgment on the absolutely unknown is impossible, so the nature is judged upon by essence, not the individuals. And based on this he said: it is necessary that the known be first obtained by itself at the time of acquisition, then its conception is sought by another face, whether a definition or a description, is not sound; for if he means by the absolutely unknown what is not obtained by any face nor by itself, then we do not concede that individuals are absolutely unknown in this sense; and if he means what is not obtained by itself, even if obtained by its face, then we do not concede the impossibility of judgment, how so, for that is neither necessary nor demonstrated, rather it is the very point of dispute, and the branching is a corrupt construction upon a corrupt one. This, and God knows best. Third: that the two descriptions may be contradictory, like our saying 'every sleeper is awake', so how can judgment be valid of the predicate's affirmation of the contrary of the reality of the subject for it, and adherence to that even by accident is far from fairness. How, and the description of the subject is not united in individuals at the time of the predicate's description. With the Fourth: that we judge necessarily in some propositions by essential affirmation, and denial of this is obstinacy, so the subject of judgment by essence must be the individuals. Fifth: that each individual is more specific than the nature, because it (the nature) is realized in some only, not all, so the predicate would be an accidental attribute for the subject, so the proposition would not then be a demonstrative issue. The answer: that the reality of the subject may be the subject of the predicate by essence, if it is a type for the individuals, and the individuals through it, like our saying 'fire is hot dry', or by means of something coextensive with the type, like our saying 'that which has no principle of straight inclination does not accept tearing', so then it becomes a demonstrative issue. Yes, in some propositions this is not the case, so it follows that they are not demonstrative issues, so reflect. Sixth: what he alluded to by his saying: 'Perhaps it appears that if it were so', i.e., the judgment on the reality, 'then the affirmative would necessitate the existence of the reality actually; for' affirmation demands the existence of that for which something is affirmed, and that that for which something is affirmed is the subject of judgment in reality, 'with' the consequent being false; since 'it may be non-existent', as in the subject modified by a privative, 'rather negative', as in the subject of a negative proposition. So the truth is that the individuals, even if known by the face, are truly judged upon. Do you not see the common term and the specific referent; for the known by the face is the true referent. And the answer: that the identity of the affirmed-for and the judged-upon absolutely is denied. How, and 'the import of affirmation absolutely is affirmation absolutely', whether essentially or accidentally, 'and every judgment established for individuals is established for the nature in general', or accidentally, so it is fit to be judged upon by essence, while not being fit to be the affirmed-for by essence. 'And as for why primarily and by essence it is for the nature or for the individual? That is a concept additional to reality, so reflect.'
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical commentary on a core issue in logic: whether the subject of a proposition (al-mahkum 'alayhi) is the nature (tabi'a) or the individuals (afrad). The author, in the style of a sharh, presents several objections and their answers. The passage begins by stating that obtaining by accident and attention by essence is sufficient for judgment, and critiques Mirzajan's view that the possessor of the face (dhu al-wajh) is attended to by essence. The author argues that Mirzajan's position contradicts necessity and lacks demonstration. There follows a series of objections (third, fourth, fifth, sixth) challenging the view that judgment is on the individuals. The third objection notes that contradictory predicates cannot hold for the same subject; the fourth insists that necessary judgments require the individuals as subjects; the fifth argues that individuals are more specific than the nature, making the predicate accidental; the sixth points out that affirmative judgments would require the existence of the nature if it were the subject. The author responds to each: the third and fourth are implied to be answered by the nature of judgment; the fifth is answered by showing that the nature can be the subject of a demonstrative issue when it is a type (naw' ) for individuals; the sixth is answered by distinguishing between the affirmed-for (muthbat lahu) and the judged-upon (mahkum 'alayhi), and by noting that universal affirmative judgments do not require the existence of the subject in extra-mental reality. All examples come from Islamic sciences: 'fire is hot dry' is a standard physical example; 'every sleeper is awake' illustrates contradiction; 'that which has no principle of straight inclination does not accept tearing' is a geometric example from Aristotelian physics. The commentary follows the order of the Arabic, explaining each objection and its refutation.

عرض غريب
النوع
المثبت له
المحكوم عليه
الخاص والعام

A) To show that the subject of judgment must exist
B) To show that the affirmed-for does not need to exist for the judgment to be valid
C) To show that the nature is the affirmed-for
D) To show that the individuals are the affirmed-for

Key Takeaway
The author refutes each objection by clarifying the logical relationship between nature and individuals, showing that individuals are the real subjects while the nature serves as a means for necessary and demonstrative judgments.

The Four Quantified Propositions and Their Quantifiers
Enumerates the four quantified propositions and their quantifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four quantified propositions and match them with their correct quantifiers, and explain the conventional nature of the universal negative from the nakira rule.

Core Idea
The four quantified propositions are the universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative.

In this passage, the author introduces the four types of quantified categorical propositions (al-mahsūrāt) that are fundamental to logical analysis. Each proposition has a specific quantifier word (sūr) that determines its scope. Understanding these quantifiers is essential for constructing and evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, especially in usul al-fiqh where rulings are expressed with such quantifiers. The author also clarifies a subtle point about the indefinite noun (nakira) under negation, which is a convention rather than a logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the four categories
First, note that the author lists four types: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative. Memorize these categories as they are the foundation of quantified logic.
2
Identify the quantifiers for each type
For the universal affirmative, the quantifier is 'kull' (every) and the definite article for totality. For the particular affirmative, it is 'ba‘ḍ' (some) or 'wāḥid' (one). For the universal negative, it is 'lā shay'' (nothing) or 'lā wāḥid' (no one). For the particular negative, it is 'laysa kull' (not every) or 'laysa ba‘ḍ' (not some), and also 'ba‘ḍ laysa' (some is not).
Note that the author also mentions number words as potential quantifiers for the universal affirmative, but clarifies this is ambiguous.
3
Understand the nakira under negation
The author explains that when an indefinite noun (nakira) falls under negation, it yields universal negation by convention (waḍ‘an), not by intellect. This means that in Arabic logic, 'No man is immortal' is understood as universal, even though the indefinite could logically refer to some.
The author disagrees with the claim in al-Musallam that it gives universality intellectually.
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
Consider the ruling: 'No intoxicant (nakira) is permissible.' This is a universal negative proposition. By convention, the indefinite 'intoxicant' under negation yields the meaning 'All intoxicants are not permissible'. In fiqh, such quantifications are crucial for determining the scope of rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَحْصُورَاتُ أَرْبَعُ: الْمُوجَبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ، وَسُورُهَا كُلُّ وَلَامُ الاسْتِغْرَاقِ، وَقَدْ عُدَّ أَسْمَاءُ الْعَدَدِ أَيْضًا مِنْهُ، وَهَذَا إِنَّمَا يَتِمُّ لَوْ كَانَتْ أَفْرَادِيَّةً، وَهُوَ فِي حَيِّزِ الْخَفَاءِ عِنْدَ أَصْحَابِ فَنِّ اللُّغَةِ، وَالْمُوجَبَةُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ، وَسُورُهَا بَعْضُ وَوَاحِدُ، وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ، وَسُورُهَا لَا شَيْءَ وَلَا وَاحِدَ وَوُقُوعُ النَّكِرَةِ تَحْتَ النَّفْيِ؛ فَإِنَّهُ يُفِيدُ الاسْتِغْرَاقَ وَضْعًا لَا عَقْلًا، كَمَا زَعَمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ فِي «الْمُسَلَّمِ»؛ فَإِنَّ نَفْيَ النَّكِرَةِ صَحِيحٌ بِاعْتِبَارِ بَعْضِ الْأَفْرَادِ عَقْلًا، وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ، وَسُورُهَا لَيْسَ كُلَّ، إِذَا كَانَ الْمَقْصُودُ مِنْهُ رَفْعَ الْإِيجَابِ الْكُلِّيِّ، وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ، إِذَا لَمْ يُقْصَدْ بِهِ رَفْعُ الْإِيجَابِ الْجُزْئِيِّ، وَإِلَّا فَسُورُ السَّلْبِ الْكُلِّيِّ، وَبَعْضُ لَيْسَ، وَفِي كُلِّ لُغَةٍ سُورٌ يَخُصُّهَا، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ.
The quantified propositions are four: the universal affirmative, and its quantifier is 'every' and the definite article of totality; and the names of numbers have also been counted among it, but this is only complete if they are individual, and it is in obscurity according to the people of the art of language. And the particular affirmative, and its quantifier is 'some' and 'one'. And the universal negative, and its quantifier is 'nothing' and 'no one', and the occurrence of the indefinite noun under negation, for it benefits generality by convention, not by intellect, as the author claimed in 'al-Musallam'; for the negation of the indefinite is valid with respect to some individuals intellectually. And the particular negative, and its quantifier is 'not every' when the intent is to negate the universal affirmative, and 'not some' if it is not intended to negate the particular affirmative, otherwise it is the quantifier of universal negation, and 'some is not'. And in every language there is a quantifier specific to it, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage defines the four types of quantified categorical propositions (al-mahsūrāt) and their quantifiers. The author begins by stating that there are four: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative. For the universal affirmative, the quantifiers are 'kull' (every) and the definite article 'al-' when it signifies totality (istiqrāq). He then notes that number words (like 'three') are sometimes considered quantifiers, but only if they refer to individuals; this is ambiguous according to linguists. For the particular affirmative, the quantifiers are 'ba‘ḍ' (some) and 'wāḥid' (one). For the universal negative, the quantifiers are 'lā shay'' (nothing) and 'lā wāḥid' (no one). A key point: the indefinite noun (nakira) when placed under negation yields universal negation by convention (waḍ‘an), not by logical necessity; the author of 'al-Musallam' claimed it gives generality intellectually, but the correct view is that it is only by convention. For the particular negative, the quantifiers are 'laysa kull' (not every) when negating the universal affirmative, and 'laysa ba‘ḍ' (not some) when not negating the particular affirmative; if the latter is intended to negate the particular affirmative, then it becomes a universal quantifier. Additionally, 'ba‘ḍ laysa' (some is not) is also a quantifier for the particular negative. Finally, the author notes that every language has its own quantifiers. A practical application: in Islamic legal reasoning, we might say 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative) or 'No intoxicant is permissible' (universal negative), etc.

موجبة كلية
سالبة كلية
موجبة جزئية
سالبة جزئية
النكرة

Key Takeaway
The four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) have distinct quantifiers, and the universal negative from indefinite under negation is a matter of linguistic convention, not logical necessity.

Quantifiers of the Particular Negative Proposition
Author defines quantifiers for particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'laysa kull', 'laysa ba'd', and 'ba'd laysa' as quantifiers of the particular negative proposition and use each in its correct context.

Core Idea
The particular negative has three quantifiers, each tied to what is being negated.

In classical logic, the quantifier of a proposition indicates its quantity. For the particular negative (saliba juz'iyya), Arabic uses several expressions that can sometimes be confused. This passage clarifies the three main quantifiers and the conditions for using each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize that we are dealing with a particular negative (saliba juz'iyya), which denies the predicate of some individuals of the subject.
2
Understand the rule about negation of an indefinite noun
The passage begins with a general rule: placing an indefinite under negation gives it universal coverage by convention, not by reason. This is a key distinction.
This rule is preliminary to the discussion of quantifiers.
3
Distinguish 'laysa kull'
'Laysa kull' (not every) is the quantifier for the particular negative when the intention is to negate the universal affirmative. It means 'not all' are so.
Example: 'ليس كل عالم فقيه' (Not every scholar is a jurist).
4
Distinguish 'laysa ba'd'
'Laysa ba'd' (not some) serves as a particular negative quantifier only when the speaker does not intend to negate the particular affirmative. Otherwise, it functions as a universal negative.
If you intend to negate 'some are', then 'laysa ba'd' becomes universal negative, meaning 'none are'.
5
Distinguish 'ba'd laysa'
'Ba'd laysa' (some are not) is a straightforward quantifier for the particular negative, with no ambiguity.
Example: 'بعض الصلوات ليست بواجبة' (Some prayers are not obligatory).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحت النفي؛ فإنه يفيد الاستغراق وضعا لا عقلا، كما زعم المصنف في «المسلَّم»؛ فإن نفي النكرة صحيح باعتبار بعض الأفراد عقلا، والسالبة الجزئية، وسورها ليس كل، إذا كان المقصود منه رفع الإيجاب الكلي، وليس بعض، إذا لم يقصد به رفع الإيجاب الجزئي، وإلا فسور السلب الكلي، وبعض ليس، وفي كل لغة سور يخصها، وهو ظاهر. (المتن هكذا في حمد الله، وفي شرح بحر العلوم.. ووقوع النكرة في حيز النفي.)
Under negation: for it gives generality by convention, not by reason, as the author claimed in al-Muslim; for the negation of an indefinite noun is correct with respect to some individuals by reason. And the particular negative, its quantifier is 'not all', if what is intended is the removal of the universal affirmative; and 'not some', if the removal of the particular affirmative is not intended; otherwise it is the quantifier of universal negation; and 'some are not'. And in every language there is a quantifier specific to it, and that is clear. (The matn is thus in Hamdullah, and in Sharh Bahr al-Ulum... and the occurrence of the indefinite in the scope of negation.)
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage explains the quantifiers used for the particular negative proposition (al-saliba al-juz'iyya). It begins with a preliminary rule: placing an indefinite noun under negation gives it universal coverage by convention, not by reason, contrary to the claim in al-Muslim. The negation of an indefinite noun is valid even if only some individuals are negated rationally. Then it addresses the particular negative itself. Its quantifier is 'laysa kull' (not every) when the intention is to negate the universal affirmative (i.e., to deny 'all A are B'). If one does not intend to negate the particular affirmative, the quantifier is 'laysa ba'd' (not some); but if one does intend to negate the particular affirmative, then 'laysa ba'd' becomes the quantifier of the universal negative. Another quantifier is 'ba'd laysa' (some are not). The passage notes that each language has its own specific quantifiers, which is obvious.

D) Never

Key Takeaway
The particular negative quantifier must be chosen carefully: 'laysa kull' negates the universal, 'laysa ba'd' is conditional, and 'ba'd laysa' is direct.

Notation Convention: Subject and Predicate Letters
Author discusses the convention and pronunciation of logical notation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conventional letters used for subject and predicate in logic and explain the pronunciation debate with evidence from al-Lahori.

Core Idea
Logicians use ب and ج as placeholders; the pronunciation debate reveals deeper consistency in symbolic notation.

In Islamic logic (mantiq), propositions are often expressed in abstract form using letters. This lesson introduces the standard convention: the subject is represented by the letter (ب) and the predicate by (ج). A debate arises over how to pronounce these letters when reading them aloud: as compound names (like 'baa') or as simple letters (like 'b'). Understanding this notation is essential for following formal logical arguments in texts like al-Qutb's Sharh al-Matali'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the convention
Find in the passage the statement that logicians customarily express the subject with (ب) and the predicate with (ج). Note that this is a conventional notation, not a logical necessity.
The Arabic text says: 'وقد جرت عادتهم بأنهم يعبرون عن الموضوع ب(ب) وعن المحمول ب(ج)'.
2
Understand the pronunciation debate
Two views are presented: the more famous method is to pronounce them as compound names (like 'باء' and 'جيم'), but al-Lahori argues for simple pronunciation (single letters). Identify the evidence each side uses.
Al-Lahori's evidence: they use expressions like 'al-jim' and 'al-ba'.
3
Analyze al-Lahori's argument
Al-Lahori claims that simple pronunciation is 'more apparent' and cites that they speak of 'al-jim' and 'al-jimiyya'. This suggests that the letters are treated as indivisible units.
Consider why deviation from the written form (simple letters) is considered 'not sound' by the author.
4
Apply the notation
Practice reading a simple proposition using this notation. For example, 'Every human is mortal' becomes 'Every ب is ج'. This abstraction is used in syllogistic reasoning.
In actual texts, the letters are variables; the convention allows focusing on logical form.

An insight into the investigation of quantified propositions. The purpose of investigating quantified propositions is to clarify the intended meaning of their subjects and predicates and the relation [between them], and to clarify the reality of the truth of the proposition and the conditions of its truth, and other things that you will come across. Al-Qutb said in Sharh al-Matali': 'The most important matters in this chapter are the investigation of quantified propositions, because the knowledge of proofs, which is the highest demand of this art, is based upon it, and great confusion occurs from heedlessness of it.' And their custom has proceeded that they express the subject with (ب) and the predicate with (ج), and the more famous [method] is to pronounce both as compound names, like 'al-muqatta'at al-niqabiyya' [the letters of the alphabet?]. This contradicts the drawing of the line; for the principle is to pronounce as written, and deviation without necessity is not sound. Al-Fadil al-Lahori said: The more famous is to pronounce both as simple [letters], and it is more apparent. And evidence for that is that they express with 'jim' and 'jimiyya' and 'ba' and 'baniyya'. In short, when they intend to express the universal affirmative, for example, not...
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage introduces an important subtopic: the investigation of quantified propositions (taḥqīq al-maḥṣūrāt). It first states the purpose: to clarify the meaning of the subject, predicate, and the relation between them, and to clarify the truth conditions of propositions. A quote from al-Qutb in Sharh al-Matali' emphasizes that this investigation is crucial because knowledge of proofs (ḥujaj) depends on it, and negligence causes great confusion. Then it discusses a specific convention: logicians represent the subject with the letter (ب) and the predicate with the letter (ج). There is a debate about pronunciation. The common practice is to pronounce them as compound names (e.g., 'baa' and 'jeem'), but this contradicts the written form, which shows simple letters. Al-Lahori argues that pronouncing them as simple letters (b and j) is more famous and more apparent. Evidence: they use expressions like 'al-jim' and 'al-jimiyya', and 'al-ba' and 'al-baniyya', which treat them as simple letters. The passage ends with an incomplete statement about expressing the universal affirmative.

Convention of Expressing Subject and Predicate with Letters
Author explains logicians' convention of using ب and ج for subject and predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logicians' convention for using ب and ج in propositions and explain the correct pronunciation according to the preferred view.

Core Idea
Logicians use ب for predicate and ج for subject – pronounce them as simple letter names.

When you open a classical Islamic logic text, you will encounter symbols like 'كل ج ب' (Every J is B). Understanding that ج stands for the subject term and ب for the predicate term is essential to parse arguments correctly. This lesson clarifies the convention and a minor debate about how to pronounce these letters when reading aloud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the letters and their assigned terms
Note that logicians use the Arabic letter ب (bā') to represent the predicate term and ج (jīm) to represent the subject term in a categorical proposition.
This assignment is fixed across classical texts; do not confuse them.
2
Understand the pronunciation debate
The passage mentions two ways to pronounce these letters: as compound nouns (e.g., 'ba' and 'jim') or as simple letter names (e.g., 'bā' and 'jīm'). The author, citing al-Fadil al-Lahuri, favors the simple pronunciation as more common and consistent with written form.
The evidence cited is that logicians say 'جيم' and 'باء' when referring to the letters.
3
Apply to a sample proposition
Take the universal affirmative proposition 'كل ج ب' (Every J is B). Using the convention, this means 'Every subject is predicate'. The correct pronunciation when reading is 'kullu jīmin bā'un' (or similar) – not 'kullu jim ba''.
This helps avoid confusion when encountering such formulas in logical works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَدْ جَرَتْ عَادَتُهُم بِأَنَّهُمْ يعبرون عن الموضوع بـ (ب)، وَالْأَشْهَرُ التلفظ بهما اسما مركبا، هذا مخالف لرسم الخط؛ فإن الأصل أن يتلفظ كما كتب، والعدول بلا ضرورة غير سديد. قال الفاضل اللاهوري: الأشهر التلفظ بهما بسيطين، وهو الأظهر. ويدل على ذلك أنهم يعبرون بالجيم والجيمية والباء والبانية. وبالجملة إذا أرادوا التعبير عن الموجبة الكلية مثالا لا تحصل.
Their custom has proceeded that they express the subject by 'ب', and the more common [practice] is to pronounce them as a compound noun, but this contradicts the rule of writing; for the original is to pronounce as written, and deviation without necessity is not sound. Al-Fadil al-Lahuri said: The more common is to pronounce them as simple [letters], and it is more apparent. That is indicated by their expression 'جيم' and 'جيمية' and 'باء' and 'بائية'. In short, when they intend to express the universal affirmative for example, it is not obtained.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage defines the logicians' notational convention for expressing quantified propositions. The author notes that although the established practice is to use the letters 'ب' for the subject and 'ج' for the predicate, there is debate about how to pronounce them when reading them aloud. Some pronounce them as compound nouns (e.g., 'با' and 'جيم'), but the author considers this a deviation from the written form. He cites al-Fadil al-Lahuri, who argues that pronouncing them as simple letters (i.e., their alphabetical names) is more common and correct. The evidence for this is that logicians say 'جيم' and 'باء' when referring to these letters. Thus, when they want to express a universal affirmative proposition, they say 'كل ج ب' (every J is B) without the extra compound. This clarification helps the reader avoid confusion when encountering these symbols in logical works.

Three Meanings of 'Kull' in Logic
Author enumerates three meanings of 'kull' and specifies the distributive sense for syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of 'kull' (universal, collective, distributive) and identify the distributive meaning used in syllogisms.

Core Idea
The word 'kull' has three distinct meanings; only the distributive sense is used in logical syllogisms.

The word 'كل' (all/every) is a crucial quantifier in logic, but it can be ambiguous. The author clarifies three distinct meanings: the universal concept, the collective whole, and the distributive whole. Understanding these distinctions prevents misinterpretation of propositions in syllogisms. In this lesson, we will break down each meaning with examples and identify which one is operative in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings
List the three meanings as given: al-kullī (universal), al-kull al-majmū‘ī (collective), and al-kull al-afrādī (distributive).
These terms are standard in post-Avicennan logic.
2
Understand the universal meaning
Recognize that 'kull' here refers to the universal concept itself, not its individuals. Example: 'Every human is a species' means 'human' as a concept is a species.
This sense does not apply to individuals at all.
3
Distinguish collective vs distributive
The collective refers to all members taken together; the distributive refers to each member individually. Use the examples: 'All humans cannot fit in this house' (collective true, distributive false) vs 'A single loaf satisfies every person' (distributive true, collective false).
Test each example against both meanings.
4
Apply to syllogisms
Note that the distributive meaning is the one used in syllogisms. It forms universal propositions where the predicate applies to each individual of the subject.
This is why syllogistic validity depends on the distributive sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فههنا أربعة أمور، فلنحقّق أحكامها في مباحث. الأول: أن الكل يُطلق بمعنى الكلي تارة، مثل 'كل إنسان نوع'. وبمعنى الكل المجموعي تارة، نحو 'كل إنسان لا يسعه هذه الدار'. وبمعنى الكل الأفرادي تارة، أي كل واحد واحد، بدلا كان أو تعاقبا أو اجتماعا. والفرق بين المفاهيم الثلاثة ظاهر؛ إذ الأول لا يسري إليه أحكام الأفراد أصلا، بخلاف الأخيرين، ويصدق الثاني في المثال المذكور، دون الثالث، وفي قولنا 'كل إنسان يشبعه هذا الرغيف' يصدق الثالث دون الثاني. والمعتبر في القياسات والعلوم هو المعنى الثالث، والمشتمل عليه هي القضية الكلية.
Thus there are four matters here; let us investigate their rulings in sections. The first: that 'all' (الكُل) is used in the sense of the universal (الكُلِّي) sometimes, like 'Every human is a species'. And in the sense of the collective whole (الكُلّ المجموعِيّ) sometimes, like 'All humans cannot fit in this house'. And in the sense of the distributive whole (الكُلّ الأفرادِيّ) sometimes, i.e., each and every one, whether as substitution, succession, or together. And the difference between the three concepts is apparent; for the first does not extend to the rulings of individuals at all, unlike the latter two. And the second is true in the mentioned example, not the third; and in our statement 'A single loaf of bread satisfies every person', the third is true, not the second. And what is considered in syllogisms and sciences is the third meaning, and the proposition that contains it is the universal proposition.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four matters concerning the word 'كل' (all/every): its three distinct meanings. The author classifies them as follows: 
1. The universal meaning (المعنى الكلي): Here 'كل' refers to the universal concept itself, not its instances. Example: 'Every human is a species' – the subject 'human' is being taken as a universal, not individual humans. 
2. The collective whole (المعنى المجموعي): This refers to all individuals taken together as a single unit. Example: 'All humans cannot fit in this house' means the entire collection of humans exceeds the house's capacity, not that each individual cannot fit. 
3. The distributive whole (المعنى الأفرادي): This refers to each individual separately, whether temporally distributed (e.g., one by one) or simultaneously. Example: 'A single loaf satisfies every person' means each person individually can be satisfied by that loaf. 
The author highlights the difference: the universal meaning does not apply to individuals at all, while the other two do. The second (collective) is true for the first example (all humans collectively cannot fit), but the third (distributive) is false because each individual can fit. Conversely, for the second example, the third is true (each individual can be satisfied), but the second is false (the loaf is not enough for the entire collection if they eat together). 
Crucially, in logical syllogisms and scientific reasoning, only the third (distributive) meaning is used. This is the meaning that forms a universal proposition (القضية الكلية) where the quantifier 'كل' applies to each and every member of the subject class individually.

Key Terms
الكلي
الكل المجموعي
الكل الأفرادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 'كل إنسان يأكل رغيفاً' (Each person eats a loaf)

Key Takeaway
The distributive meaning of 'kull' (each individual) is the only one used in syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'All scholars are to be respected', explain which meaning of 'kull' is intended in a logical syllogism and why.

Three Technical Meanings of 'Kull' in Logic
Author distinguishes three meanings of 'kull'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three technical meanings of 'kull' (universal, collective whole, individual whole) and identify which is used in syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
The third meaning of 'kull' – the distributive individual whole – is the engine of syllogistic logic.

In logic, the term 'kull' ('all' or 'every') is not monolithic. Classical logicians distinguished three distinct meanings: the universal concept, the collective whole, and the individual whole. Understanding these distinctions is crucial because only one of them – the individual whole – is used in syllogistic reasoning and the demonstrative sciences. In this lesson, we will dissect each meaning with examples from Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings in the source Arabic
Locate where the author lists the three meanings: al-kulliyy, al-majmu'i, al-afradi. Note the Arabic terms and their definitions.
The first meaning is universal (kulliyy), the second is collective whole (majmu'i), the third is individual whole (afradi).
2
Contrast the universal with the other two
Observe that the first meaning (universal) does not apply to individuals at all, while the second and third do. For example, 'every human is a species' is a universal statement about the concept, not about individual humans.
3
Differentiate collective vs. individual with kalam examples
Consider the kalam example: 'All humans are responsible for knowing God.' If read collectively, the group as a whole is responsible; if distributively, each individual is responsible. The passage uses similar examples: 'all humans fit in this house' (collective, false) vs. 'every human is satisfied by this loaf' (individual, true).
The same phrase 'kull insan' can have different truth conditions depending on the intended meaning.
4
Verify which meaning is used in syllogisms
The text explicitly states: 'Wa al-mu'tabar fi al-qiyasat wa al-'ulum huwa al-ma'na al-thalith' – the third meaning (individual whole) is what is considered in syllogisms and sciences.
Only the distributive meaning supports the quantification needed for valid syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أَنَّ الْكُلَّ يُطْلَقُ بِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّيَّ تارة، مِثْلَ كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ نَوعٌ . وَبِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّ الْمَجْمُوعِيَّ تارة، نَحْوُ (كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ)، أي مجموع الإنسان لا يَسَعُهُ هَذِهِ الدَّارُ، وَبِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّ الْأَفْرَادِيِّ تارة، أي كل واحد واحد، بدلا كان أو تعاقبا أو اجتماعا، وَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الثَّلَاثَةِ ظَاهِرُ؛ إذ الأول لا يسري إليه أحكام الأفراد أصلا، بخلاف الأخيرين، ويصدق الثاني في المثال المذكور، دون الثالث، وفي قولنا كل إنسان يشبعه هذا الرغيف يصدق الثالث دون الثاني. وَالْمُعْتَبر في الْقِيَاسَاتِ وَالْعُلُومِ هُوَ الْمَعْنَى الثَّالِثُ، وَالْمُشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهِ هِيَ الْمَحْصُورَةُ ، وَأَمَا الْأُولى فَطبيعية ، والثانية شخصية، إن كان المضاف إليه للكل جزئيا، نحو كل زيد حسن ، أو مهملة ، إن كان كليا، كما في المثال المذكور؛ فإن مجموع الأشخاص يحتمل الزيادة والنقصان، لا كما ظن البعض أنها شخصية مطلقا، أو مهملة مطلقا، كذا في الحاشية. والقضية المشتملة على البعض المجموعي مهملة اتفاقا.
The first: that 'al-kull' is used sometimes in the meaning of the universal, like 'every human is a species'. And sometimes in the meaning of the collective whole, like 'all humans' meaning the totality of humans does not fit in this house. And sometimes in the meaning of the individual whole, meaning each single one, whether as a replacement, successively, or collectively. The difference between the three concepts is apparent: the first does not apply to the judgments of individuals at all, unlike the latter two. The second is true in the mentioned example, not the third, and in our saying 'every human is satisfied by this loaf', the third is true, not the second. What is considered in syllogisms and sciences is the third meaning. What contains it is the quantified proposition. As for the first, it is a natural proposition, and the second is personal if what is added to 'kull' is particular, like 'every Zayd is good', or indeterminate if it is universal, as in the mentioned example; indeed the totality of persons is subject to increase and decrease, not as some thought that it is absolutely personal or absolutely indeterminate, as in the gloss. The proposition that contains the collective part is indeterminate by agreement.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the term 'kull' into three distinct technical meanings. The author first presents the first meaning: 'kull' as the universal, such as 'every human is a species'. This refers to the universal concept itself. The second meaning is the collective whole, as in 'all humans' meaning the entire group, used in examples like 'all humans cannot fit in this house'. The third meaning is the individual whole, distributive, as in 'each individual human'. The author highlights the difference: the first meaning does not apply to individuals at all, while the second and third do. The second is true of the group but not distributively, as shown by the contrasting examples of the house and the loaf. Crucially, the third meaning is the one used in syllogistic logic and the sciences. Then the author classifies the types of propositions resulting from these meanings: propositions with the first meaning are 'natural' (tabi'iyya), with the second are 'personal' (shakhsiyya) if the added term is particular (e.g., 'every Zayd'), or 'indeterminate' (muhmala) if the added term is universal (since the group can vary). He corrects a misunderstanding that they are always personal or always indeterminate. Finally, he states that any proposition containing the collective part is indeterminate by consensus.

Propositional Forms from the Three Meanings of 'Kull'
Author classifies propositions based on the meaning of 'kull'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a proposition containing 'kull' as natural, personal, or indeterminate based on the meaning of 'kull' used.

Core Idea
The first meaning yields natural propositions, the second yields personal or indeterminate propositions, and only the third yields quantified propositions for syllogisms.

The three meanings of 'kull' give rise to different types of propositions. The author explains that when 'kull' is used in the first sense (universal), the proposition is 'natural' (tabi'iyya). In the second sense (collective), if the added term is particular, it becomes 'personal' (shakhsiyya); if universal, it remains 'indeterminate' (muhmala). The third sense produces the standard 'quantified' (mahdura) proposition used in syllogisms. This classification is essential for correctly analyzing logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify which meaning of 'kull' is used in the proposition
Determine if it's universal, collective, or individual based on the context and the author's definitions.
Refer back to the three meanings from the previous lesson.
2
Apply the author's rule for classification
If first meaning, the proposition is natural (tabi'iyya). If second meaning, check whether the term added to 'kull' is particular (juz'i) or universal (kulli): particular gives personal (shakhsiyya), universal gives indeterminate (muhmala). If third meaning, it is quantified (mahdura).
The passage explicitly gives these rules.
3
Test with examples from the text
For instance, 'every Zayd is good' uses collective 'kull' with a particular term (Zayd), so it is personal. 'All humans are species' uses universal 'kull', so it is natural. 'Every human is satisfied by this loaf' uses individual 'kull', so it is quantified.
4
Correct common misconceptions
The author corrects those who think the second meaning always yields personal or always indeterminate; it depends on the added term. Verify with additional examples from fiqh.
For example, 'all women must cover' – if 'women' is particular (referring to a specific group), it is personal; if universal, it is indeterminate.
