الأول : أَنَّ الْكُلَّ يُطْلَقُ بِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّيَّ تارة، مِثْلَ كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ نَوعٌ . وَبِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّ الْمَجْمُوعِيَّ تارة، نَحْوُ (كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ)، أي مجموع الإنسان لا يَسَعُهُ هَذِهِ الدَّارُ، وَبِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّ الْأَفْرَادِيِّ تارة، أي كل واحد واحد، بدلا كان أو تعاقبا أو اجتماعا، وَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الثَّلَاثَةِ ظَاهِرُ؛ إذ الأول لا يسري إليه أحكام الأفراد أصلا، بخلاف الأخيرين، ويصدق الثاني في المثال المذكور، دون الثالث، وفي قولنا كل إنسان يشبعه هذا الرغيف يصدق الثالث دون الثاني. وَالْمُعْتَبر في الْقِيَاسَاتِ وَالْعُلُومِ هُوَ الْمَعْنَى الثَّالِثُ، وَالْمُشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهِ هِيَ الْمَحْصُورَةُ ، وَأَمَا الْأُولى فَطبيعية ، والثانية شخصية، إن كان المضاف إليه للكل جزئيا، نحو كل زيد حسن ، أو مهملة ، إن كان كليا، كما في المثال المذكور؛ فإن مجموع الأشخاص يحتمل الزيادة والنقصان، لا كما ظن البعض أنها شخصية مطلقا، أو مهملة مطلقا، كذا في الحاشية. والقضية المشتملة على البعض المجموعي مهملة اتفاقا.
The first: that 'al-kull' is used sometimes in the meaning of the universal, like 'every human is a species'. And sometimes in the meaning of the collective whole, like 'all humans' meaning the totality of humans does not fit in this house. And sometimes in the meaning of the individual whole, meaning each single one, whether as a replacement, successively, or collectively. The difference between the three concepts is apparent: the first does not apply to the judgments of individuals at all, unlike the latter two. The second is true in the mentioned example, not the third, and in our saying 'every human is satisfied by this loaf', the third is true, not the second. What is considered in syllogisms and sciences is the third meaning. What contains it is the quantified proposition. As for the first, it is a natural proposition, and the second is personal if what is added to 'kull' is particular, like 'every Zayd is good', or indeterminate if it is universal, as in the mentioned example; indeed the totality of persons is subject to increase and decrease, not as some thought that it is absolutely personal or absolutely indeterminate, as in the gloss. The proposition that contains the collective part is indeterminate by agreement.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the term 'kull' into three distinct technical meanings. The author first presents the first meaning: 'kull' as the universal, such as 'every human is a species'. This refers to the universal concept itself. The second meaning is the collective whole, as in 'all humans' meaning the entire group, used in examples like 'all humans cannot fit in this house'. The third meaning is the individual whole, distributive, as in 'each individual human'. The author highlights the difference: the first meaning does not apply to individuals at all, while the second and third do. The second is true of the group but not distributively, as shown by the contrasting examples of the house and the loaf. Crucially, the third meaning is the one used in syllogistic logic and the sciences. Then the author classifies the types of propositions resulting from these meanings: propositions with the first meaning are 'natural' (tabi'iyya), with the second are 'personal' (shakhsiyya) if the added term is particular (e.g., 'every Zayd'), or 'indeterminate' (muhmala) if the added term is universal (since the group can vary). He corrects a misunderstanding that they are always personal or always indeterminate. Finally, he states that any proposition containing the collective part is indeterminate by consensus.

Types of Quantified Propositions: Natural, Personal, and Indefinite
Defines quantified propositions and distinguishes natural, personal, and indefinite types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a quantified proposition as natural, personal, or indefinite based on the nature of its subject term.

Core Idea
A quantified proposition's type depends on whether the subject is a natural kind, a particular individual, or a universal under the quantifier.

In Islamic logic, quantified propositions are not all alike. The source text introduces three types—natural, personal, and indefinite—based on the subject term's nature. This lesson guides you through the criteria to distinguish them and clarifies a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier and subject term
Look for quantifiers like 'كل' (every) or 'بعض' (some). The subject term is the noun or phrase immediately following the quantifier.
In the passage, the quantifier is 'كل'.
2
Determine if the subject is a natural kind
If the subject is taken as a nature or essence without considering individuals (e.g., 'الإنسان' meaning humanity), the proposition is natural (طبيعية).
Natural propositions treat the subject as a universal concept.
3
Check the complement of 'كل' for personal vs. indefinite
If the complement (المضاف إليه) is a particular term like 'زيد', it is personal (شخصية). If it is universal like 'إنسان', it is indefinite (مهملة).
This is the key distinction in the passage.
4
Verify with the collective part rule
The passage states that a proposition containing a collective part (البعض المجموعي) is always indefinite by agreement.
This reinforces the indefinite category.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The proposition that includes the collective part is indefinite by agreement.

Commentary
This passage defines the different types of quantified propositions. It clarifies that a quantified proposition may be natural (where the subject’s individuals are conceived as a natural kind), personal (where the subject refers to a proper name or particular), or indefinite (where the quantifier applies to a universal complement, leading to a proposition that is not strictly universal or particular). The author corrects a misconception that such propositions are always personal or always indefinite. The final sentence notes agreement that a proposition containing a collective part ('al-ba'd al-majmu'i') is considered indefinite.

The Two Contracts in a Categorical Proposition: Subject and Predicate
Author explains the two conceptual contracts underlying every categorical proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two implicit contracts (ʿaqd al-waḍʿ and ʿaqd al-ḥaml) in any categorical proposition and explain their roles in mental composition.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition contains a hidden mental contract that collects the subject individuals before predicating anything.

This passage from the commentary clarifies a crucial but often misunderstood distinction in analyzing categorical propositions: the contract of position (ʿaqd al-waḍʿ) and the contract of predication (ʿaqd al-ḥaml). The author notes that neither the original matn author nor the main commentator fully explained this, so he provides a concise clarification. Understanding these two contracts will sharpen your ability to dissect any proposition into its logical components, preventing confusion about how subject terms function.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate in a categorical proposition
Take a proposition like 'Aloeswood has a pleasant fragrance.' The subject term is 'aloeswood' and the predicate term is 'has a pleasant fragrance.' Recognize that the subject term is a concept that collects individuals.
2
Recognize the contract of position (ʿaqd al-waḍʿ)
Understand that before predicating anything, the mind takes the individuals of the subject under a unifying title (the subject concept). This mental act is the contract of position. It is a restrictive composition, not a proposition itself.
This contract is expressed in textbooks by the phrase 'كل ما لو وجد كان'—which is not a conditional but an interpretation indicating 'individuals described by such-and-such'.
3
Identify the contract of predication (ʿaqd al-ḥaml)
After the contract of position, the mind asserts the predicate of those collected individuals. This is the contract of predication, a predicative assertive composition that is explicitly expressed in the proposition.
4
Analyze a new proposition using both contracts
Take a legal example: 'Every intoxicant is haram.' First, the mind collects all individuals under the title 'intoxicant' (contract of position). Then, it predicates 'haram' of them (contract of predication). Verify that both contracts are present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إشارة إلى عقد من عقدين يشتمل عليهما الحمل، وهو عقد الوضع، لم يوضحهما المصنف ولا الشارح حق التوضيح، فأنا أقوم بتوضيحه باختصار. فاعلم أن أفراد الموضوع المحكوم عليها لا تؤخذ في القضية مبعثرة متفرقة، بل تؤخذ بعنوان يجمعها، هو مفهوم الموضوع. فإذا قلت: العود طيب الرائحة فالمحكوم عليه بطيب الرائحة هو أفراد الخشب المعروف بالعود، وقد أخذت تلك الأفراد بعنوان أنها «عود»، ثم حكم عليها بمفهوم المحمول. فأنت هنا في الحقيقة قد وصفت تلك الأفراد بوصفين، أحدهما وصف الموضوع، والآخر وصف المحمول. واتصاف الأفراد بوصف الموضوع – وبعبارة المصنف «صدق عنوان الموضوع على ذاته» – يسمى في اصطلاح هذا الفن «عقد الوضع»، وهو تركيب تقييد عقلي، وعنه يقع التعبير في كتبهم بـ«أن كل ما لو وجد كان»، وهذه ليست شرطية كما توهم القطب، بل هو تفسير للقضية الحملية على ما حققه السيد. فمعناه «في الأفراد الموصوفة بالفل». وأما اتصاف الأفراد بوصف المحمول فيسمى «عقد الحمل»، وهو تركيب خبري إسنادي. فصار المعنى: الأفراد الموصوفة بالفل طيب الرائحة. وكلمة «عقد» تعني تركيبا، وبالجملة فإن كل قضية محصورة أو مهملة تشتمل على عقدين: عقد الوضع وعقد الحمل، والقضية وإن دلت على تركيب واحد هو عقد الحمل، إلا أن عقد الوضع يدل عليه العقل كما أوضحنا.
This refers to one of the two contracts that predication comprises, namely the contract of position, which neither the author nor the commentator fully clarified, so I will clarify it briefly. Know that the individuals of the subject that are judged upon are not taken in the proposition scattered and separate, but are taken under a title that collects them, which is the concept of the subject. For example, if you say 'Aloeswood has a pleasant fragrance,' the object of the judgment of pleasant fragrance is the individuals of the wood known as aloeswood, and those individuals were taken under the title that they are 'aloeswood,' then you judged them by the concept of the predicate. So you have actually described those individuals with two descriptions: one is the description of the subject, and the other is the description of the predicate. The attachment of the individuals to the description of the subject—in the author's words, 'the truth of the title of the subject upon its essence'—is called in the terminology of this art the 'contract of position,' and it is a mental restrictive composition, and it is what is expressed in their books as 'everything that if it existed, would be such-and-such'—this is not a conditional as al-Qutb imagined, but an interpretation of the categorical proposition as al-Sayyid verified. So its meaning is 'in the individuals described by such-and-such.' As for the attachment of the individuals to the description of the predicate, it is called the 'contract of predication,' and it is a predicative assertive composition. So the meaning becomes: the individuals described by such-and-such have a pleasant fragrance. The word 'contract' means a composition. In summary, every quantified or indefinite proposition includes two contracts: the contract of position and the contract of predication. Although the proposition indicates only one composition, which is the contract of predication, the contract of position is indicated by the intellect, as we have clarified.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained clarification by the commentator (likely the author of Bahr Ulum) of the two implicit contracts (ʿaqd) underlying every categorical proposition. The commentator notes that neither the original author of the matn nor the main commentator fully explained this, so he steps in. He begins by stating that individuals of the subject are not taken as scattered but collected under a single title (the subject concept). Using the example 'Aloeswood has a pleasant fragrance,' he shows that we first take individuals of aloeswood under the title 'aloeswood' (contract of position), then we predicate a property of them (contract of predication). The first contract is a mental restrictive composition, expressed in textbooks as 'everything that if it existed, would be such-and-such'—a technical phrase that is not a conditional but an interpretation of the categorical as indicating individuals described by the subject. The second contract is a predicative assertive composition. Both contracts are present in every quantified proposition, though the proposition explicitly expresses only the second; the first is mentally understood. This distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing propositions and avoiding confusion about the role of the subject term.

Key Terms
عقد الوضع
عقد الحمل
صدق عنوان الموضوع على ذاته

Key Takeaway
Every categorical proposition involves two mental contracts: the contract of position (collecting individuals under the subject concept) and the contract of predication (asserting the predicate of those individuals).

The Two Knots of a Categorical Proposition and the Condition of Actual Predication
Author defines the condition of actual predication and refutes misinterpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the position-knot (عقد الوضع) and predicate-knot (عقد الحمل) in any categorical proposition and explain why actual predication (صدق العنوان بالفعل) is necessary for the truth of universal propositions.

Core Idea
A universal proposition is only true if its subject term actually describes each individual, not just mentally supposed.

In earlier lessons, you learned about universal and particular propositions. But what does it mean for a subject term to truly apply to its individuals? This lesson unpacks the internal structure of categorical propositions: the 'position-knot' that gathers individuals under the subject concept, and the 'predicate-knot' that judges them. The author then clarifies a crucial condition: the subject term must actually describe the individuals, not merely be imagined to. We'll see how later scholars misunderstood this and the objection that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two knots in a proposition
Take the example 'العود طيب الرائحة'. Break it into the position-knot (individuals taken under 'wood') and predicate-knot (judging them as 'sweet-smelling').
2
Understand the position-knot as a mental restriction
The position-knot is a mental composition (عقد الوضع) that restricts the individuals to those actually described by the subject title. It is not a conditional statement, as al-Qutb mistakenly thought.
3
Define actual predication (صدق العنوان بالفعل)
The subject title must actually describe the individuals, whether in external reality or in the mind's estimation with actual existence. The author quotes Ibn Sina to show that actuality includes mental description with existence.
4
Contrast with the later scholars' error
The later scholars thought that mere mental supposition (فرض العقل) suffices, even if the individuals are not actually described. This leads to a paradox: if we allow non-matching supposition, then a universal proposition could have individuals that are not actually subjects, making the universal false.
5
Apply to an example
Consider 'كل أبيض'. Only individuals actually described as 'white' are included. Bare essences or non-white things that are mentally supposed to be white are not included, because they are not actually described by the title.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن أفراد الموضوع المحكوم عليها لا تؤخذ في القضية مبعثرة متفرقة، بل تؤخذ بعنوان يجمعها، هو مفهوم الموضوع، فإذا قلت: العود طيب الرائحة فالمحكوم عليه بطيب الرائحة هو أفراد الخشب المعروف بالعود، وقد أخذت تلك الأفراد بعنوان أنها «عود»، ثم حكم عليها بمفهوم المحمول، فأنت هنا في الحقيقة قد وصفت تلك الأفراد بوصفين، أحدهما وصف الموضوع، والآخر وصف المحمول. واتصاف الأفراد بوصف الموضوع ـ وبعبارة المصنف «صدق عنوان الموضوع على ذاته» - يسمى في اصطلاح هذا الفن «عقد الوضع»، وهو تركيب تقييد عقلي، وعنه يقع التعبير في كتبهم بـ«أن كل ما لو وجد كان»، وهذه ليست شرطية كما توهم القطب، بل هو تفسير للقضية الحملية على ما حققه السيد (راجع حواشي الشمسية : ٤٥/٢٢، العطار على الخبيصي: ٢٤٧)، فمعناه في الأفراد الموصوفة بالفل». وأما اتصاف الأفراد بوصف المحمول يسمى «عقد الحمل»، وهو تركيب خبري إسنادي فصار المعنى الأفراد الموصوفة بالفل طيب الرائحة». وكلمة «عقد» تعني تركيبا، وبالجملة فإن كل قضية محصورة أو مهملة تشتمل على عقدين: عقد الوضع وعقد الحمل، والقضية وإن دلت على تركيب واحد هو عقد الحمل، إلا أن عقد الوضع يدل عليه العقل كما أوضحنا.

ثم قال: هذا الفعل ليس فعل الوجود في الأعيان فقط، فربما لم يكن ملتفتا إليه من حيث هو موجود في الأعيان، ولا بصفة هو على أن الموضوع يكون للشيء وهو موجود بل من حيث هو معقول بالفعل موصوفا بالصفة، على أن العقل يصفه بأن وجوده بالفعل يكون كذا سواء وجد أو لم يوجد، فيكون قولك كل أبيض» معناه كل واحد مما يوصف عند العقل بأن يجعل وجوده بالفعل أنه أبيض دائما، أو في وقت أي وقت كان، اهـ.

فحسب المتأخرون أن المعتبر فرض العقل اتصاف الأفراد بالعنوان، مطابقا كان أو غير مطابق، حتى دخل في «كل أبيض» الماديات والمجردات، وقالوا: هذه حقيقة الحقيقية، وأما الخارجية فلا بد فيها من صدق العنوان بالفعل، فورد عليهم أن لا يصدق كلية أصلا ؛ لأن من أفراد (ج) ما ليس (ب)، فلا يكون
Know that the individuals of the subject that are predicated are not taken in the proposition as scattered and separate, but are taken under a title that gathers them, which is the concept of the subject. So when you say: 'al-wood is sweet-smelling,' what is judged with sweet-smelling are the individuals of the wood known as al-wood, and those individuals have been taken under the title that they are 'wood', then they are judged by the concept of the predicate. So you here in reality have described those individuals with two descriptions: one is the description of the subject, and the other is the description of the predicate. The attribution of the individuals with the description of the subject – and in the author's expression 'the truth of the title to the essence' – is called in the terminology of this art 'position-knot' (عقد الوضع), and it is a mental restriction composition, and from it arises the expression in their books as 'that everything that if it existed would be', and this is not a conditional as al-Qutb supposed, but rather it is an interpretation of the categorical proposition according to what al-Sayyid verified (refer to Hawashi al-Shamsiyya: 45/22, al-Attar on al-Khubaysi: 247), so its meaning is 'in the individuals described with the such-and-such'. As for the attribution of the individuals with the description of the predicate, it is called 'predicate-knot' (عقد الحمل), and it is a predicative report composition, so the meaning becomes 'the individuals described with the such-and-such are sweet-smelling'. The word 'عقد' means a composition. In summary, every restricted or unrestricted proposition contains two knots: the position-knot and the predicate-knot. The proposition, even though it indicates one composition which is the predicate-knot, the position-knot is indicated by the mind as we have clarified.

Then he said: This actuality is not the act of existence in external entities only, for sometimes it is not attended to from the perspective of its being existent in external entities, nor with a description it has, but rather from the perspective of being actually intelligible, described by the attribute, such that the mind describes it as its existence actually being such and such, whether it exists or not. So your saying 'every white' means 'every one that is described in the mind as having its existence actually be white, always or at some time, any time it is' – end of his words.

So the later scholars thought that what is considered is the mind's supposition of the individuals being described by the title, whether it matches or does not match, such that into 'every white' enter material and immaterial things. They said: this is the true universal; as for the external universal, it requires the actual truth of the title. Then it was objected against them that no universality holds at all, because some individuals of (j) are not (b), so it would not be.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentator on the condition of 'actual predication' (صدق العنوان بالفعل) in universal propositions. The author begins by explaining the structure of a proposition: individuals are taken under a subject concept (عقد الوضع) and then judged with a predicate concept (عقد الحمل). He clarifies that the position-knot is a mental restriction composition, not a conditional statement as al-Qutb mistakenly thought. The commentator then quotes Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) to show that the 'actuality' in the subject term's application is not necessarily existence in external entities, but rather the mind's description of the individual as having actual existence with the attribute. The later scholars (متأخرون) misunderstood this, thinking that mere mental supposition (فرض العقل) suffices, even if the individuals are not actually described by the title. This leads to a paradox: if we allow non-matching supposition, then a universal proposition could have individuals that are not actually subjects of the predicate, making the universal false. For example, if we say 'every white is colored', and we include bare essences that are not actually white, the proposition fails. The commentator thus refutes the later scholars' position, insisting on actual predication (صدق العنوان بالفعل) as essential for true universal propositions.

Key Terms
عقد الوضع
عقد الحمل
صدق العنوان بالفعل

Checkpoint

A) The individual must exist externally and be white.
B) The individual must be mentally supposed to be white, regardless of actual description.
C) The individual must be actually described as white, either in external reality or in mental consideration with actual existence.
D) The individual must be a material object capable of being white.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a universal proposition depends on the subject term actually describing its individuals, not merely on mental supposition.

The Condition for Universal Affirmative Propositions: Actual Predication vs. Mere Possibility
Explains the true condition for universal propositions and refutes misunderstandings of Ibn Sina's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the views of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina on the truth condition of universal affirmative propositions and identify the correct interpretation as expounded by Bahr al-Ulum.

Core Idea
Universal propositions require actual predication after hypothetical existence, not mere mental supposition.

This passage from Bahr al-Ulum's commentary delves into a subtle but crucial debate in Islamic logic: what exactly makes a universal affirmative proposition like 'every A is B' true? The discussion centers on the views of two giants—al-Farabi and Ibn Sina—and a common misunderstanding that led later scholars astray. In this lesson, you will unpack their positions and see why Ibn Sina's condition of 'actual predication' (ittiṣāf bi-l-fiʿl) after supposing existence is more precise than al-Farabi's reliance on mere possibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage debates whether the individuals in 'every A is B' must actually exist externally or only be possible. This condition determines whether non-existent entities are included.
2
Understand al-Farabi's view
Al-Farabi held that if it is possible for an individual to be described by the predicate, then it falls under the universal, even if that individual never actually exists. This approach is simpler but potentially over-inclusive.
3
Grasp Ibn Sina's refinement
Ibn Sina requires that the intellect suppose the individual's existence and then see if it would actually be described by the predicate in reality. The supposition is of existence, not of the predication itself. This excludes individuals that are perpetually free from the predicate.
4
Recognize the common error and its correction
Later scholars mistakenly thought Ibn Sina accepted any mental supposition, leading to paradoxes. The commentator of al-Matali' and Bahr al-Ulum clarify that the predication must be possible in reality and then supposed as actual, not merely possible. The correction preserves the truth of genuine universals.
5
Appreciate the consequence for Islamic sciences
This condition ensures that universal propositions like 'every intoxicant is haram' cover all actual and hypothetical intoxicants, supporting sound legal analogy. Misunderstanding could invalidate many universal statements used in fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم
متصفا بالعنوان، (سَوَاءٌ وُجِدَ أَوْ لَمْ يُوجَدْ)، فالفرض إنما هو فرض الوجود ليعتبر الاتصاف بالفعل في نفس الأمر ، لا فرض الاتصاف.
(فَالذَّاتُ الْخَالِيَةُ عَن السَّوَادِ دَائِمًا)، وإن كانت يمكن لها الاتصاف بالسواد (لَا تَدْخُلُ في كُلُّ أَسْوَدَ عَلَى رَأي الشَّيْخ، وَمَنْ قَالَ بِدُخُولِهَا عَلَى رَأْيِهِ، كَشَارح «الْمَطَالِع» وَأَتْبَاعِهِ فَقَدْ غَلِطَ مِنْ قِلَّةِ تَدَبُرِهِ فِي بَعْضِ عِبَارَاتِهِ. نَعَمْ ، الذَّوَاتُ الْمَعْدُومَةُ الَّتِي هِيَ أَسْوَدُ بِالْفِعْلِ) في نفس الأمر (بَعْدَ) فرض (الْوُجُودِ دَاخِلَةٌ فِيهِ) .
اعلم أن الشيخ بعد ما حقق أن المراد صدق العنوان بالفعل على الأفراد قال : هذا الفعل ليس فعل الوجود في الأعيان فقط، فربما لم يكن ملتفتا إليه من حيث هو موجود في الأعيان، ولا بصفة هو على أن الموضوع يكون للشيء وهو موجود بل من حيث هو معقول بالفعل موصوفا بالصفة، على أن العقل يصفه بأن وجوده بالفعل يكون كذا سواء وجد أو لم يوجد، فيكون قولك كل أبيض» معناه كل واحد مما يوصف عند العقل بأن يجعل وجوده بالفعل أنه أبيض دائما ، أو في وقت أي وقت كان .
فحسب المتأخرون أن المعتبر فرض العقل اتصاف الأفراد بالعنوان، مطابقا كان أو غير مطابق، حتى دخل في «كل أبيض» الماديات والمجردات، وقالوا: هذه حقيقة الحقيقية، وأما الخارجية فلا بد فيها من صدق العنوان بالفعل، فورد عليهم أن لا يصدق كلية أصلا ؛ لأن من أفراد (ج) ما ليس (ب)، فلا يكون (ب)، ومنها ما هو (ب) ، فلا يكون ليس (ب) ، فتفطن البعض بذلك، وقيد الأفراد بالإمكان ، فورد عليهم خروج نحو كل ممتنع معدوم .
فلأجل دفع ذلك قال شارح «المطالع»: إن الشيخ إنما اعتبر فرض الاتصاف بعد كونه ممكنا في نفس الأمر، فلا يلزم عدم صدق جميع الكليات ؛ فإن (ج) الذي ليس (ب) مما يمتنع اتصافه بـ (ج) في نفس الأمر، إذا كان (ب) ذاتيا أو لازما له وقال لا فرق بين مذهب الفارابي والشيخ إلا في العبارة ؛ فإن الفارابي اكتفى بإمكان الصدق من حيث إنه ممكن، والشيخ اعتبر من حيث إنه ممكن مفروض بالفعل، لا من حيث إنه ممكن فقط .
والحق أن كل ذلك ناشئ من قلة التدبر في كلام الشيخ ؛ فإن مقصوده أن الأفراد التي اتصفت بالفعل في نفس الأمر بعد فرض الوجود بالبياض، سواء كانت موجودة في نفس الأمر أو معدومة داخلة في كل أبيض، وما هو غير موصوف به دائما سواء كان موجودا أو معدوما، لكن يمكن له البياض غير داخل ، وإن فرضه العقل متصفا به غیر مطابق، بخلاف مذهب الفارابي ، وليس المراد بالجعل جعل الاتصاف واختراعه بل جعل وجود الأفراد وفرضه؛ ليتمكن العقل من اعتبار اتصافه في الواقع بالعنوان بالفعل، على ما يقتضيه سوق كلامه ، فليرجع إليه. والله أعلم بمراد عباده.
وليعلم أيضا: إن كان مراد الفارابي بالإمكان الإمكان الحكمي فالفرق ، وإن كان المراد الإمكان المنطقي فلا فرق إلا في المفهوم، كما لا يخفى ، والله أعلم بالصواب .
Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: 'described by the predicate (whether it exists or does not exist), so the supposition is only the supposition of existence in order to consider actual predication in reality, not the supposition of the predication. (Thus the essence that is perpetually free from blackness), even if it can have blackness attached to it, (does not enter into 'every black' according to the view of the Shaykh, and whoever claims it enters on his view, like the commentator of al-Matali' and his followers, has erred due to lack of reflection on some of his statements. Yes, non-existent essences that are black in actuality) in reality (after) the supposition (of existence are included in it). Know that the Shaykh, after establishing that what is meant is the actual truth of the predicate to the individuals, said: This actuality is not the act of existence in external entities only; perhaps it is not attended to from the aspect of its being existent in external entities, nor from the aspect of its being a quality that the subject has while it exists, but rather from the aspect of its being intellectually conceived as actually described by the quality, such that the intellect describes it that its actual existence is such-and-such, whether it exists or does not exist. So your saying 'every white' means every one that is described by the intellect that making its existence actual is that it is white always or at any time. So the later scholars thought that what is considered is the intellect's supposition of the individuals being described by the predicate, whether it matches [reality] or not, so that material and immaterial [entities] enter into 'every white', and they said: This is the reality of [the universal] al-haqiqiyya, while [the universal] al-kharijiyya requires the actual truth of the predicate. So it was objected to them that no universal is true at all, because among the individuals of (C) there is what is not (B), so it is not (B), and among them there is what is (B), so it is not not (B). So some became aware of that and restricted the individuals to possibility, so it was objected to them that [statements] like 'every impossible is non-existent' are excluded. So to repel that, the commentator of al-Matali' said: The Shaykh only considered the supposition of predication after it being possible in reality, so the lack of truth of all universals does not follow; for (C) that is not (B) is such that its being described by (C) is impossible in reality, if (B) is essential or necessary for it. And he said there is no difference between the doctrine of al-Farabi and the Shaykh except in expression; for al-Farabi suffices with the possibility of truth in terms of its being possible, and the Shaykh considers it in terms of its being possible but supposed as actual, not just as possible. And the truth is that all that arises from lack of reflection on the words of the Shaykh; for his intent is that individuals that are actually described in reality after the supposition of existence with whiteness, whether they exist in reality or are non-existent, enter into 'every white', and what is not perpetually described by it, whether existent or non-existent, but can have whiteness, does not enter, even if the intellect supposes it described by it without correspondence, contrary to the doctrine of al-Farabi. And what is meant by 'making' is not making the predication and inventing it, but rather making the existence of the individuals and supposing it, so that the intellect can consider its predication in reality with the predicate actually, according to what the flow of his speech requires, so let one refer to it. And Allah knows best the intent of His servants. And let it also be known: If al-Farabi intended by 'possibility' the legal possibility, then there is a difference; if he intended the logical possibility, then there is no difference except in concept, as is not hidden. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Ulum is a commentary on a logic text concerning the definition of universal affirmative propositions, specifically the condition for their truth. The author addresses a debate between the views of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) on whether the individuals considered in a universal proposition like 'every A is B' must actually exist externally or can be merely possible. The author begins by stating that the supposition (farḍ) is of existence, not of the predication itself, because what matters is that the description truly applies in reality after existence is supposed. Thus, a subject that is perpetually free from blackness cannot enter into 'every black' even if it could have blackness, according to Ibn Sina; those who say it enters, like the commentator of al-Matali', are mistaken. However, non-existent essences that are actually black in reality after the supposition of existence do enter. The author then explains Ibn Sina's view more fully: the actuality of the predicate is not merely actual existence in the external world, but rather the intellect's conception of the subject as actually having the description, regardless of external existence. This means that 'every white' includes anything that the intellect would describe as 'white' if it were to exist. Later scholars misunderstood this and thought that the intellect's arbitrary supposition of predication is sufficient, leading to paradoxes where no universal could be true (since some A are not B and some are B). Some then restricted to possibility, but that excluded statements like 'every impossible is non-existent'. The commentator of al-Matali' attempted to reconcile al-Farabi and Ibn Sina by saying Ibn Sina requires the predication to be possible in reality, not just mentally supposed. The author concludes that the real difference is one of expression: al-Farabi is satisfied with mere possibility, while Ibn Sina requires that possibility be supposed as actual in the intellect's consideration, but this does not mean inventing predication; rather, the intellect supposes existence to see the actual predication. Finally, the author note that if al-Farabi's 'possibility' means legal (hikmi) possibility, there is a genuine difference; if logical possibility, the difference is only conceptual. The commentary thus clarifies the precise condition for universal propositions in Islamic logic, emphasizing that actual predication after hypothetical existence is key, not mental fabrication.

Key Terms
العنوان
الاتصاف بالفعل
الإمكان

Key Takeaway
The correct condition for universal affirmative propositions requires actual predication after hypothetical existence, not mere mental supposition or mere possibility.

Ibn Sina's Condition of Possibility for Universal Affirmatives
Author clarifies Ibn Sina's condition of possibility against an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Ibn Sina's condition of possibility avoids the counterexample of 'every impossible is non-existent'.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's requirement of actual assumption after possibility in reality saves universal propositions from false counterexamples.

In this passage, the author addresses a central issue in categorical logic: what ensures that a universal affirmative proposition like 'Every A is B' is true? An objection based on 'every impossible is non-existent' threatened earlier views. Ibn Sina's solution, as defended by the commentator of al-Mutali', distinguishes between mere possibility and assumed actuality. This lesson unpacks that solution and its implications for logical theory.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Problem
Some logicians required that individuals of the subject be possible for the predicate. But the proposition 'Every impossible is non-existent' would then be problematic because 'impossible' cannot be non-existent (since non-existent is a condition of impossibility). Identify the counterexample: 'every impossible is non-existent' seems false if possibility is required.
The Arabic variable (ب) stands for the predicate and (ج) for something else.
2
Ibn Sina's Response via the Commentator
According to the commentator of al-Mutali', Ibn Sina only considered the assumption of qualification after it is possible in reality. This means that for a universal like 'every C is B', we assume that C exists and is qualified by B, but only if such qualification is really possible. This avoids the counterexample because 'impossible' cannot be qualified by 'non-existent' in reality.
3
Distinguish Logical Possibility from Actual Assumption
The key is that Ibn Sina requires not just logical possibility (i.e., no contradiction) but actual assumption of the qualification in reality. This is different from al-Farabi who sufficed with the possibility of truth as such. Ibn Sina's condition ensures that the individuals included are those that can be truly described after assuming existence.
This distinction resolves the objection.
4
Apply to a Concrete Example
Take the proposition 'Every human is mortal'. According to Ibn Sina, we assume that if a human exists, he is mortal. Even non-existent but possible humans are included because if they existed, they would be mortal. But 'impossible' entities like a round square cannot even be assumed to exist without contradiction, so they are excluded.
This matches the author's explanation.

تبصرة في تحقيق المحصورات (ب)، ومنها ما هو (ب) ، فلا يكون ليس (ب) ، فتفطن البعض بذلك، وقيد الأفراد بالإمكان ، فورد عليهم خروج نحو كل ممتنع معدوم . فلأجل دفع ذلك قال شارح «المطالع»: إن الشيخ إنما اعتبر فرض الاتصاف بعد كونه ممكنا في نفس الأمر، فلا يلزم عدم صدق جميع الكليات ؛ فإن (ج) الذي ليس (ب) مما يمتنع اتصافه بـ (ج) في نفس الأمر، إذا كان (ب) ذاتيا أو لازما له وقال لا فرق بين مذهب الفارابي والشيخ إلا في العبارة ؛ فإن الفارابي اكتفى بإمكان الصدق من حيث إنه ممكن، والشيخ اعتبر من حيث إنه ممكن مفروض بالفعل، لا من حيث إنه ممكن فقط . والحق أن كل ذلك ناشئ من قلة التدبر في كلام الشيخ ؛ فإن مقصوده أن الأفراد التي اتصفت بالفعل في نفس الأمر بعد فرض الوجود بالبياض، سواء كانت موجودة في نفس الأمر أو معدومة داخلة في كل أبيض، وما هو غير موصوف به دائما سواء كان موجودا أو معدوما، لكن يمكن له البياض غير داخل ، وإن فرضه العقل متصفا به غیر مطابق، بخلاف مذهب الفارابي ، وليس المراد بالجعل جعل الاتصاف واختراعه بل جعل وجود الأفراد وفرضه؛ ليتمكن العقل من اعتبار اتصافه في الواقع بالعنوان بالفعل، على ما يقتضيه سوق كلامه ، فليرجع إليه. والله أعلم بمراد عباده. وليعلم أيضا: إن كان مراد الفارابي بالإمكان الإمكان الحكمي فالفرق ، وإن كان المراد الإمكان المنطقي فلا فرق إلا في المفهوم، كما لا يخفى ، والله أعلم بالصواب
A clarification on the investigation of categorical propositions: (b) and among them is (b), so it is not (b), so some understood that, and they restricted individuals with possibility, so there came against them the objection (of the case) like 'every impossible is non-existent'. To repel that, the commentator of al-Mutali' said: The master (Ibn Sina) only considered the assumption of qualification after it being possible in reality, so it is not necessary that all universal (propositions) be false; for (c) which is not (b) is such that its qualification by (c) is impossible in reality, if (b) is essential or necessary for it. And he said: There is no difference between the doctrine of al-Farabi and the master except in expression; for al-Farabi sufficed with the possibility of truth in terms of its being possible, while the master considered it in terms of its being actually assumed possible, not merely in terms of its being possible. The truth is that all of this arises from lack of reflection on the master's words; for his intention is that the individuals which are actually qualified (with whiteness) in reality after assuming existence, whether existing in reality or non-existent, are included in 'every white', and what is not qualified by it always, whether existent or non-existent, but is possible for it to have whiteness, is not included, and the intellect's assuming it as qualified is not corresponding (to reality), contrary to al-Farabi's doctrine. And by 'making' he does not mean making the qualification and inventing it, but rather making the existence of individuals and assuming it, so that the intellect can consider its qualification in reality by the title actually, according to what his discourse requires, so refer to it. And Allah knows best the intention of His servants. Also know: If al-Farabi's intention by possibility is juridical possibility, then there is a difference; if logical possibility, then there is no difference except in concept, as is not hidden. And Allah knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a clarification (tabṣira) regarding the investigation of categorical propositions (al-maḥṣūrāt). The author addresses a specific issue concerning the condition of possibility (imkān) in universal affirmative propositions. Some logicians restricted the individuals of the subject to those for which the predicate is possible, but an objection arose: 'every impossible is non-existent' would be false because 'impossible' cannot be non-existent. The commentator on al-Mutali' defended Ibn Sina (the 'master') by saying that Ibn Sina considered the assumption of qualification after possibility in reality, not mere logical possibility. This means that for a universal like 'every A is B', it is enough that if we assume an individual A exists and qualifies as B in reality, then it is included. This avoids the counterexample. The author then clarifies the difference between al-Farabi and Ibn Sina: al-Farabi sufficed with the possibility of truth as such, while Ibn Sina required actual assumption of possibility. However, the author argues that this is due to misunderstanding; Ibn Sina's aim was to distinguish individuals that are actually qualified (even if mentally assumed) from those that are not. The 'making' refers to assuming existence, not inventing the qualification. Finally, the author notes that if al-Farabi meant juridical possibility (imkān ḥukmī), there is a real difference; if logical possibility, the difference is only conceptual. The discussion uses logical variables (b, c) as placeholders.

Key Terms
الإمكان (al-imkān): The condition that the predicate can be true of the subject; here discussed as either logical possibility (imkān manṭiqī) or juridical possibility (imkān ḥukmī) and whether it requires actual assumption.
المحصورات (al-maḥṣūrāt): Categorical propositions quantified with 'all' or 'some', etc.
الشيخ (al-Shaykh): Ibn Sina, the master, whose doctrine on possibility is compared to al-Farabi's.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The predicate must be necessary for the subject.
B) The subject must exist in reality.
C) The assumption of qualification must be after possibility in reality.
D) The proposition must be about existing things.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's condition of possibility requires that the predicate's qualification be actually assumed after real possibility, not merely possible in thought.

?
Task: Consider the proposition 'Every possible world is created'. Using Ibn Sina's condition, explain whether a non-existent but possible world is included in the scope of 'every possible world'.
?
Task: Identify which of the following satisfies Ibn Sina's condition: a) an actually existing entity, b) a mentally assumed entity with no real possibility, c) a possible entity assumed to exist.

Al-Farabi vs Ibn Sina on the Imkān Condition: Semantic or Real?
Author compares al-Farabi and Ibn Sina on possibility condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-Farabi's and Ibn Sina's doctrines on possibility in categorical propositions and determine when the difference is substantial.

Core Idea
Al-Farabi and Ibn Sina differ only in expression if logical possibility is meant, but diverge if juridical possibility is intended.

The passage presents a comparison between al-Farabi and Ibn Sina on the condition of possibility for universal affirmative propositions. The author, after citing the commentator of al-Mutali', states that the two masters differ only verbally if logical possibility is understood. However, he later clarifies that the difference is real if juridical possibility is meant. This lesson explores the nuances of this distinction and its implications for logical theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Farabi's Doctrine
Al-Farabi sufficed with the possibility of truth in terms of its being possible. That is, for a universal proposition 'Every A is B', it is enough that it is not impossible for an A to be B. No requirement that we assume the actual qualification of any individual.
This is a more minimal condition.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's Doctrine
Ibn Sina considered the possibility from the perspective of actual assumption. He required that the individual be assumed to exist and be qualified by the predicate, provided that such qualification is possible in reality. This is a stronger condition.
This avoids counterexamples like 'every impossible is non-existent'.
3
Analyze the Author's Conclusion
The author notes that the apparent difference is due to lack of reflection. If al-Farabi meant logical possibility (imkān manṭiqī), then the difference is only in expression: both effectively require that the proposition be possibly true. But if al-Farabi meant juridical possibility (imkān ḥukmī), which considers permissibility in law, then there is a substantive difference because Ibn Sina's condition is about real possibility, not legal permissibility.
Juridical possibility involves things like permissibility of an action, which is different from logical possibility.
4
Apply to a Fiqh Example
Consider the universal 'Every contract is valid'. If al-Farabi meant juridical possibility, he might consider contracts that are legally possible but not yet existent as included. Ibn Sina would require assuming the contract exists and that it can be valid in reality. This could lead to different truth values.
This shows the real-world impact of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة في تحقيق المحصورات (ب)، ومنها ما هو (ب) ، فلا يكون ليس (ب) ، فتفطن البعض بذلك، وقيد الأفراد بالإمكان ، فورد عليهم خروج نحو كل ممتنع معدوم . فلأجل دفع ذلك قال شارح «المطالع»: إن الشيخ إنما اعتبر فرض الاتصاف بعد كونه ممكنا في نفس الأمر، فلا يلزم عدم صدق جميع الكليات ؛ فإن (ج) الذي ليس (ب) مما يمتنع اتصافه بـ (ج) في نفس الأمر، إذا كان (ب) ذاتيا أو لازما له وقال لا فرق بين مذهب الفارابي والشيخ إلا في العبارة ؛ فإن الفارابي اكتفى بإمكان الصدق من حيث إنه ممكن، والشيخ اعتبر من حيث إنه ممكن مفروض بالفعل، لا من حيث إنه ممكن فقط . والحق أن كل ذلك ناشئ من قلة التدبر في كلام الشيخ ؛ فإن مقصوده أن الأفراد التي اتصفت بالفعل في نفس الأمر بعد فرض الوجود بالبياض، سواء كانت موجودة في نفس الأمر أو معدومة داخلة في كل أبيض، وما هو غير موصوف به دائما سواء كان موجودا أو معدوما، لكن يمكن له البياض غير داخل ، وإن فرضه العقل متصفا به غیر مطابق، بخلاف مذهب الفارابي ، وليس المراد بالجعل جعل الاتصاف واختراعه بل جعل وجود الأفراد وفرضه؛ ليتمكن العقل من اعتبار اتصافه في الواقع بالعنوان بالفعل، على ما يقتضيه سوق كلامه ، فليرجع إليه. والله أعلم بمراد عباده. وليعلم أيضا: إن كان مراد الفارابي بالإمكان الإمكان الحكمي فالفرق ، وإن كان المراد الإمكان المنطقي فلا فرق إلا في المفهوم، كما لا يخفى ، والله أعلم بالصواب
A clarification on the investigation of categorical propositions: (b) and among them is (b), so it is not (b), so some understood that, and they restricted individuals with possibility, so there came against them the objection (of the case) like 'every impossible is non-existent'. To repel that, the commentator of al-Mutali' said: The master (Ibn Sina) only considered the assumption of qualification after it being possible in reality, so it is not necessary that all universal (propositions) be false; for (c) which is not (b) is such that its qualification by (c) is impossible in reality, if (b) is essential or necessary for it. And he said: There is no difference between the doctrine of al-Farabi and the master except in expression; for al-Farabi sufficed with the possibility of truth in terms of its being possible, while the master considered it in terms of its being actually assumed possible, not merely in terms of its being possible. The truth is that all of this arises from lack of reflection on the master's words; for his intention is that the individuals which are actually qualified (with whiteness) in reality after assuming existence, whether existing in reality or non-existent, are included in 'every white', and what is not qualified by it always, whether existent or non-existent, but is possible for it to have whiteness, is not included, and the intellect's assuming it as qualified is not corresponding (to reality), contrary to al-Farabi's doctrine. And by 'making' he does not mean making the qualification and inventing it, but rather making the existence of individuals and assuming it, so that the intellect can consider its qualification in reality by the title actually, according to what his discourse requires, so refer to it. And Allah knows best the intention of His servants. Also know: If al-Farabi's intention by possibility is juridical possibility, then there is a difference; if logical possibility, then there is no difference except in concept, as is not hidden. And Allah knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a clarification (tabṣira) regarding the investigation of categorical propositions (al-maḥṣūrāt). The author addresses a specific issue concerning the condition of possibility (imkān) in universal affirmative propositions. Some logicians restricted the individuals of the subject to those for which the predicate is possible, but an objection arose: 'every impossible is non-existent' would be false because 'impossible' cannot be non-existent. The commentator on al-Mutali' defended Ibn Sina (the 'master') by saying that Ibn Sina considered the assumption of qualification after possibility in reality, not mere logical possibility. This means that for a universal like 'every A is B', it is enough that if we assume an individual A exists and qualifies as B in reality, then it is included. This avoids the counterexample. The author then clarifies the difference between al-Farabi and Ibn Sina: al-Farabi sufficed with the possibility of truth as such, while Ibn Sina required actual assumption of possibility. However, the author argues that this is due to misunderstanding; Ibn Sina's aim was to distinguish individuals that are actually qualified (even if mentally assumed) from those that are not. The 'making' refers to assuming existence, not inventing the qualification. Finally, the author notes that if al-Farabi meant juridical possibility (imkān ḥukmī), there is a real difference; if logical possibility, the difference is only conceptual. The discussion uses logical variables (b, c) as placeholders.

Key Terms
الإمكان الحكمي (al-imkān al-ḥukmī): Juridical possibility, referring to what is legally permissible as opposed to logically possible.
الإمكان المنطقي (al-imkān al-manṭiqī): Logical possibility, referring to what is not self-contradictory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is there no real difference between al-Farabi and Ibn Sina?
A) When al-Farabi means logical possibility.
B) When al-Farabi means juridical possibility.
C) When Ibn Sina's condition is ignored.
D) When the proposition is negative.

Key Takeaway
The difference between al-Farabi and Ibn Sina on possibility is merely verbal if logical possibility is meant, but substantive if juridical possibility is intended.

Predication as Union of Two Different Things
Author defines predication and distinguishes essential from accidental union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of predication (essential and accidental) and apply the criteria to examples from Islamic logic and ontology.

Core Idea
Predication unites two distinct concepts in one existence, either directly or through an intermediary.

This lesson unpacks the definition of predication (al-ḥaml) given by the author. You will learn that predication is not about identity but about union of distinct things, and that this union can be essential or accidental. The passage also discusses the criteria for each type and the problems that arise when applying the criteria to genus and species. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions and arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definition
Find the term being defined (predication) and its definition: 'union of two different things in some mode of conception or existence.' The genus is 'union' and the differentia is 'of two different things'.
2
Distinguish essential vs accidental union
Note the two types: essential union has no intermediary in the attribution of existence; accidental union involves an intermediary (a third thing). Understand the criteria given: subsistence of the principle (mabda') directly or through a third.
3
Examine the claim of specification
Some logicians claim the first criterion (direct subsistence) is specific to essentials (dhātiyyāt) and the second to accidentals ('araḍiyyāt). Locate this claim in the passage.
4
Test the claim with genus and species
The author objects using the example of genus and species: the genus is a universal accident of the species, yet their union is essential. This counterexample challenges the claimed specification.
Consider why the attempted answer fails: existence belongs to the single thing that is both genus and species.
5
Evaluate the commentator's objection
Some commentators argue that the principle (e.g., 'whiteness') should be predicated rather than the derived term ('white'). The author dismisses this as absurd. Reflect on why the author rejects this objection.
The text cuts off, but the author's dismissal indicates a rejection of the objection based on the earlier definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمل اتحاد المتغايرين في نحو من التعقل بحسب نحو آخر بالذات أو من الوجود ، اتحادا بالذات. المشهور في تفسيره علاقة بين الشيئين، بحيث يكون نسبة وجود أحدهما بالذات إلى الآخر من غير واسطة في العروض، بأن يكونا موجودين بوجود واحد، أو اتحادا بالعرض. وهو علاقة بين الشيئين بحيث ينسب وجود أحدهما بالذات إلى الآخر بوساطته بالعرض، أو وجود ثالث بالذات إليهما بالعرض. ومعياره قيام مبدأ أحدهما بالآخر، أو انتزاعه عنه، بمقايسة إلى آخر أو لا بمقايسة ، أو قيام مبدئهما بثالث، أو انتزاعهما عن ثالث. وقالوا: الأول مختص بالذاتيات، والثاني بالعرضيات. ويرد عليه ورودا ظاهرا الانتقاض باتحاد الأجناس والفصول ؛ فإن الجنس عرض عام للفصل، كما أن الفصل خاصة له، مع كون الاتحاد بينهما بالذات . ولا ينفع الجواب بأن الوجود إذا نسب إلى النوع فهو وجود للجنس والفصل بالذات، وإذا نسب إلى الجنس فهو وجود للفصل والنوع بالعرض، وهكذا حال الفصل ؛ لأن الوجود إنما يعرض لهما من حيث إنهما واحد على مذهبهم، كما تقدم فالوجود واحد لشيء واحد، ذلك الواحد بعينه الجنس والفصل، فالوجود منسوب إليهما بالذات وأيضا لم يدل إلى الآن دليل على ذلك الاختصاص، والله أعلم بالصواب. واعترض بعض الشراح على الاتحاد بالعرض، بأن مدار الحمل لما كان قيام المبدأ فما شأن المشتق يكون محمولا دون المبدأ، بل المبدأ أولى بالحمل ؛ لكونه منتزعا منه بالذات، والمشتق بوساطته، والمبدأ المنضم أولى من المنتزع ؛ لكونه موجودا بالذات. ولا يخفى سخافته ؛ فإن الحمل بالعرض
Predication (al-ḥaml) is the union of two different things (al-mutaghāyirīn) in some mode of conception according to another mode, essentially, or in some mode of existence, essentially. The famous (interpretation) in its explanation is a relation between two things such that the existence of one is essentially attributed to the other without an intermediary in the predication, such that they exist with a single existence; or (union) accidentally. And it is a relation between two things such that the existence of one is essentially attributed to the other through an intermediary accidentally, or a third existence is essentially attributed to both accidentally. Its criterion is the subsistence of the principle (mabda') of one in the other, or its derivation from it, whether in comparison to another or not, or the subsistence of the principle of both in a third, or their derivation from a third. And they said: The first is specific to essentials (dhātiyyāt), and the second to accidentals ('araḍiyyāt). But there is an apparent counterexample against it: the union of genera and species; for the genus is a universal accident of the species, just as the species is a special property of it, while the union between them is essential. And the answer that 'if existence is attributed to the species, it is existence for the genus and species essentially, and if attributed to the genus, it is existence for the species and type accidentally, and similarly for the species' does not benefit, because existence only attaches to both in that they are one according to their doctrine, as previously, so existence is one for one thing, that one being exactly the genus and species, so existence is attributed to both essentially. Also, no proof has yet been given for that specification. And Allah knows best the truth. Some commentators objected to accidental union, saying: Since the basis of predication is the subsistence of the principle, what is the matter with the derived (term) being predicated instead of the principle? Rather, the principle is more deserving of predication, because it is derived from essentially, while the derived is through it, and the incorporated principle is more deserving than the derived, because it exists essentially. And its absurdity is not hidden, for accidental predication...
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage defines predication (al-ḥaml) in the logical sense as the union (ittiḥād) of two different things (al-mutaghāyirīn) in a mode of conception (ta‘aqqul) or existence (wujūd). The union can be essential (bi-al-dhāt) or accidental (bi-al-‘araḍ). Essential union is when the existence of one is directly attributed to the other without an intermediary, as when two things share one existence (e.g., in essential definitions). Accidental union is when there is an intermediary, like a third thing. The author gives criteria: the criterion for essential union is that the principle (mabda') of one attribute is in the other directly, or derivation without comparison; for accidental union, the principle is in a third or derives from a third. Some logicians claim the first criterion is specific to essentials (dhātiyyāt) and the second to accidentals, but the author objects with the example of genus and species: they are essentially united (both are part of the essence), yet the genus is a 'universal accident' of the species and vice versa according to them. The attempted answer that existence is essentially attributed to the species but accidentally to the genus fails because existence belongs to the one thing that is both genus and species. The author also dismisses an objection by some commentators that the predicate should be the principle (e.g., 'whiteness') rather than the derived (e.g., 'white'), calling that objection absurd, though the text cuts off before the full refutation. The discussion uses examples from ontology and logic.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-ḥaml): Predication; the logical relation between subject and predicate, defined here as union of two different things in existence or conception, either essentially or accidentally.
المتغايرين (al-mutaghāyirīn): The two different things that are united in predication; they are conceptually distinct.
بالذات (bi-al-dhāt): Essentially; said of predication where the existence of one is directly attributed to the other without intermediary.
بالعرض (bi-al-'araḍ): Accidentally; predication via an intermediary, or when a third thing's existence is attributed to both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The principle of the predicate subsists in the subject without an intermediary.
C) The subject and predicate must be identical in concept.
D) The existence of the predicate depends on a third thing.

Key Takeaway
Predication is the union of two distinct concepts, and the type of union (essential or accidental) depends on whether there is an intermediary in the attribution of existence.

The Third Condition for Predication: One Thing Under Two Descriptions
Author states the third condition for valid predication and its two subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third condition for valid predication and distinguish between theoretical and common conventional predication based on the two considerations.

Core Idea
Valid predication requires observing one entity through two distinct conceptual lenses.

This lesson explains the third condition for a proposition to be valid: the subject and predicate must be one thing considered under two different aspects. The passage from Bahr al-Ulum elaborates on this condition, distinguishing between theoretical predication (where the two descriptions are conceptually distinct) and common conventional predication (based on existential unity).

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Look for the statement that one thing must be observed with two considerations (bi-lahzayn). This is the core of the third condition.
The condition is stated at the beginning: 'الثالث: أن يلاحظ شيء واحد بلحاظين'.
2
Determine the two subtypes
The passage divides valid predication into two types: theoretical (نظري) and common conventional (شائع متعارف). Note the distinguishing features: theoretical uses conceptually distinct titles; common uses existential unity.
3
Apply the condition to an example
Take a statement like 'Existence is the Necessary.' Check if it satisfies the condition: is there one thing (existence) observed under two considerations (as existence and as Necessary)? Yes.
This is a theoretical predication because the two descriptions are conceptually distinct.
4
Distinguish the subtypes in practice
Given another predication, e.g., 'Zayd is a human,' determine if it is theoretical or common. Here, 'Zayd' and 'human' are existentially one (Zayd is an instance of human), so it is common conventional.
Common predication is used in the sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن يلاحظ شيء واحد بلحاظين، بأن يقيد بقيدين متغايرين، سواء كان ذلك القيد التفاتا أو غيره، وحينئذ يصح الحمل قطعا، وقد يكون غير مفيد، وقد يكون مفيدا . (وَقَدْ يَكُونُ نَظْريَّا أَيْضًا، كما إذا عنون شيء واحد بعنوانين متغايرين مفهوما، متباينين في بادئ الرأي ، كقول المحققين: الوجود هو الواجب، (أَوْ يُقْتَصَرُ فِيهِ)، أي الحمل عَلَى مُجَرَّدِ الاتِّحَادِ فِي الْوُجُودِ، فَيُسَمَّى الْحَمْلَ الشَّائِعَ الْمُتَعَارَفَ)، ومآله أن الموضوع نفسه أو فرده فرد المحمول، (وَهُوَ الْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْعُلُومِ) ، وقد مر تحقيقه في بحث الكليات ، فتذكر .
Third: that one thing be observed with two considerations, by being restricted by two distinct restrictions, whether that restriction is an attention or other. In that case predication is valid absolutely, and it may be non-informative or informative. And it may also be theoretical, such as when one thing is titled by two conceptually distinct titles, disparate at first sight, like the saying of the verifiers: 'Existence is the Necessary.' Or it is confined in it, i.e., predication based solely on unity in existence, then it is called common conventional predication. Its import is that the subject itself or its individual is an individual of the predicate. And it is what is considered in the sciences. Its verification has passed in the discussion of universals, so recall.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage explains the third condition for valid predication: taking one thing under two different descriptions (bi-lahzayn). The author first defines the condition: a single entity is conceptualized with two distinct restrictions, and then predication is valid. He distinguishes two subtypes: theoretical predication, where the two descriptions are conceptually distinct (e.g., 'Existence is the Necessary'), and common conventional predication (al-haml al-shā'i' al-mut'āraf), which is based on existential unity. The common predication is the one used in the sciences, and its full treatment is in the discussion of universals.

Key Terms
اللحاظين
الحمل النظري
الحمل الشائع المتعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Divisions of Common Predication: Essential, Accidental, Derivation, and Correlation
Author divides common predication into essential/accidental and by relation type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two divisions of common predication and classify examples as essential, accidental, derivation, or correlation.

Core Idea
Common predication splits by predicate nature and by relation mediation—and the two types are not unified.

In this passage, the author presents two independent ways to classify common predication. The first looks at whether the predicate is essential or accidental to the subject. The second looks at whether the predication is indirect (via a preposition) or direct. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for analyzing definitions and statements in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
The passage clearly presents two divisions: one based on the predicate's essential/accidental status, and another based on the mediation of the relation. Notice that the author uses different phrases for each.
The first division uses 'bi-l-dhat' and 'bi-l-'arad'; the second uses 'bi-l-ishtiqaq' and 'bi-l-muwata'a'.
2
Examine the first division: essential vs. accidental
Essential predication occurs when the predicate is part of the subject's definition (e.g., 'Man is animal'); accidental predication is when the predicate is not part of the essence (e.g., 'Man is white'). Think of Islamic examples like 'Wine is intoxicating' (essential) vs. 'Wine is red' (accidental).
3
Examine the second division: derivation vs. correlation
Derivation involves a preposition ('fi', 'dhu', 'lahu') that indicates a relation of inherence or possession, e.g., 'Zayd is in the house'. Correlation is direct, without preposition, indicating existential unity, e.g., 'Zayd is a man'. Trace how each type functions in Arabic sentences.
The author notes that derivation implies inherence (hulul), while correlation implies unity (ittihad).
4
Analyze the author's conclusion about homonymy
The author states that the term 'predication' is applied homonymously to derivation and correlation because the reality (haqiqa) of each differs: one involves inherence, the other existential unity. Reflect on why this distinction matters: it prevents us from treating all predication as uniform.
This shows that careful classification avoids logical errors when analyzing propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَنْقَسِمُ) الحمل الشائع بِحَسَبِ كَوْنِ الْمَحْمُولِ ذَاتِيَّا) للموضوع ، ، (أَوْ عَرَضِيًّا لَهُ إِلَى الْحَمْلِ بِالذَّاتِ أَوْ بِالْعَرَضِ، وَقَدْ يَنْقَسِمُ) الحمل بِأَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ إِلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ إِمَّا بِوَاسِطَةِ «في» أو «ذُو» أو «له»، فَهُوَ الْحَمْلُ بِالاشْتِقَاقِ ، أَوْ بِلا وَاسِطَةٍ ، وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ بِعَلَى، فَهُوَ الْحَمْلُ بِالْمُوَاطَاةِ، وَالْأَشْبَهُ أَنَّ إِطْلَاقِ الْحَمْلِ عَلَيْهِمَا بِالاِشْتِرَاكِ اللَّفْظِيَّ) ؛ لأن حقيقة الأول الحلول، والثاني الاتحاد في الوجود ، وليس بينهما أمر جامع، حتى يكون حملا .
And common predication divides, according to the predicate being essential to the subject or accidental, into essential predication or accidental predication. And it may divide, by the relation of the predicate to the subject either with a mediation of 'fi' or 'dhu' or 'lahu', so it is predication by derivation, or without mediation, and it is said 'bi-ala', so it is predication by correlation. And the more apparent is that applying the term 'predication' to both is by homonymy, because the reality of the first is inherence and the second is unity in existence, and there is no common thing between them such that it would be predication.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct divisions of common predication (al-haml al-sha'i'). The first division is based on the nature of the predicate relative to the subject: if the predicate is essential (dhati), it is essential predication (haml bi-l-dhat); if accidental (aradi), it is accidental predication (haml bi-l-'arad). The second division is based on the logical relation expressed: if the predicate relates via a preposition like 'fi' (in), 'dhu' (possessor of), or 'lahu' (to it), it is predication by derivation (haml bi-l-ishtiqaq); if without mediation, it is predication by correlation (haml bi-l-muwata'a). The author notes that these two types (derivation and correlation) are actually homonymous (ishtirak lafzi) because the first involves inherence (hulul) while the second involves existential unity (ittihad fi al-wujud), so the term 'predication' is applied to them ambiguously.

Key Terms
الحمل بالذات
الحمل بالعرض
الحمل بالاشتقاق
الحمل بالمواطاة
الاشتراك اللفظي

Checkpoint

Resolution of a Doubt about Predication with Unity and Difference
Author resolves a doubt about predication with unity and difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a sophistical doubt and a genuine one in predication, and apply the condition of unconditional predicate to resolve apparent contradictions in Islamic logical propositions.

Core Idea
Predication allows unity and difference in different respects; the predicate must be unconditional.

In this passage, the author addresses a doubt about how predication can hold when subject and predicate seem identical in one way but different in another. This is a common concern in logic, especially when analyzing definitions in usul al-fiqh. The author distinguishes between a sophist who misuses the doubt and a genuine objector, and provides a clear solution: the predicate must be considered without any additional condition, so that both aspects can coexist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of doubters
Determine whether the objection comes from a sophist (mughtalit) or a genuine arguer. The author says the answer differs depending on who raises it. A sophist's objection is not to be engaged with formal logic.
The doubter's intent matters for the appropriate response.
2
Understand the apparent paradox of unity and difference
The doubt is that if A is B (e.g., 'human' is 'rational animal'), then they are identical, so how can there be any difference? In predication, the subject and predicate are the same in essence (haqiqa) but differ in mental consideration (i'tibar).
This is similar to the distinction between essence and concept in usul.
3
Apply the solution: unconditional predicate
The predicate must be taken 'la bi shart shay'' (unconditionally), meaning we strip it of any additional qualification. Then we can conceive both unity (in reality) and difference (in aspect).
This condition ensures no contradiction arises.
4
Connect to Islamic science examples
In kalam, when we say 'the world is temporally originated,' the subject (world) and predicate (temporally originated) are united in that the world is indeed originated, but different in that the concept of 'world' is not identical to 'originated'. This is resolved by taking the predicate unconditionally.
Such distinctions are crucial in theological definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة في تحقيق المحصورات
يخفى أن الشاك لو كان مغالطا فهذا الجواب خارج عن قانون التوجيه، ولو كان مستدلا على نفي الحمل، كما يقتضيه عبارة السيد المحقق ـ قدس سره ـ بأن يكون ذلك مذهبه، فله وجه.
(وَحَلُهُ: أَنَّ) (ج) متحد مع (ب) باعتبار، ومغاير باعتبار، و(التَّغَائِرُ(۱) مِنْ وَجْهِ لاَ يُنَافِي الاِتِّحَادَ مِنْ وَجْهِ آخَرَ . نَعَمْ، يَجِبُ أَنْ يُؤْخَذَ الْمَحْمُولُ لا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ ، حَتَّى يُتَصَوَّرَ فِيهِ أَمْرَانِ): الاتحاد والمغايرة، وقد سبق تحقيقه،
فتذكر .
(Note on verifying the enumerated things). It is hidden that if the doubter were a sophist, then this answer is outside the law of guidance; and if he were arguing for the negation of predication, as is required by the expression of al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq — may his secret be sanctified — that that is his doctrine, then he has a point. And its solution: that (ج) is united with (ب) in one respect and different in another respect, and difference in one aspect does not negate unity in another aspect. Yes, it is necessary that the predicate be taken unconditionally, so that two matters — unity and difference — can be conceived in it. And its investigation has preceded, so remember.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific logical doubt (shakk) concerning predication when the subject and predicate are identical in one sense but different in another. The commentator clarifies that if the one raising the doubt is a sophist (mughtalit), then the earlier answer is not appropriate; but if he is genuinely arguing from a doctrinal position, then there is justification. The solution (hall) is that the two terms (ج and ب, used as variable terms) can be united in one respect and distinct in another, and this does not cause a contradiction. The key condition is that the predicate must be considered without any additional condition (la bi shart shay'), so that both unity and difference can be imagined in it. This is a standard logical move: predicates are considered as absolute concepts, allowing for multiple relations.

شاك

Negative Propositions and Subject Existence
Author explains that universal negative propositions have different subject existence requirements than affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a universal negative proposition does not require subject existence and identify the main points of debate about quantified negatives.

Core Idea
A negative can be true even if its subject class is empty, but quantification complicates the rule.

In Islamic logic, the existential import of propositions is a subtle issue. Affirmative propositions generally require the existence of their subjects, but negatives do not. This lesson unpacks the standard view and the debate around quantified negatives, as explained by the author and the martyred sheikh (Suhrawardi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard claim about negatives
Find in the Arabic the statement that negative propositions do not require subject existence. Note the phrase 'فَلَا تَسْتَدْعِي وُجُودَ الْمَوْضُوعِ'.
2
Compare with affirmative propositions
Understand that affirmatives do require existence. The author says the subject of a negative is more general than that of an affirmative, meaning negatives can apply to empty classes.
Example: 'No phoenix is a bird' is true even without phoenixes.
3
Examine Suhrawardi's objection
The martyred sheikh (Suhrawardi) argues that for quantified negatives, the 'contract of position' (عقد الوضع) forces the subject to have individuals. Locate his argument in the text.
He says quantified negatives require the existence of the subject of negation.
4
Analyze the response and counter-objection
Others respond that عقد الوضع is merely a restrictive composition and does not entail existence. But then a further objection arises that the attributive relation calls for existence. Understand the back-and-forth.
The passage leaves the issue open, showing it is a known point of contention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا السَّالِبَةُ فَلَا تَسْتَدْعِي وُجُودَ الْمَوْضُوعِ، بَلْ قَدْ يَصْدُقُ بِانْتِفَائِهِ)، هذا معنى ما تسمعهم يقولون: موضوع السالبة أعم من موضوع الموجبة. قال الشيخ المقتول: هذا الحكم صحيح في غير المحصورة، وأما فيها فلأن عَقْدَ الْوَضْعِ يستدعي وجود الأفراد، فيستدعي السالبة وجود المسلوب عنه، إلا أن في الموجبة اقتضاء مكررا ، بخلاف السالبة. أجابوا عنه بأن عقد الوضع تركيب تقييدي لا يقتضي الوجود أصلا، ويرد عليه أن النسبة التوصيفية تنادي على وجود الموضوع.
As for the negative (proposition), it does not require the existence of the subject; rather, it may be true by the non-existence of the subject. This is the meaning of what you hear them say: the subject of a negative is more general than the subject of an affirmative. The martyred sheikh said: This ruling is correct for non-quantified (propositions); but as for quantified ones, because the 'contract of position' (عقد الوضع) requires the existence of individuals, thus the negative requires the existence of that which is negated from, except that in the affirmative there is a repeated requirement, unlike the negative. They responded to him that the contract of position is a restrictive composition that does not require existence at all. It is objected to that the attributive relation calls for the existence of the subject.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage discusses an important logical subtlety: the conditions for the truth of negative propositions. The author states the standard view: a negative proposition (like 'No man is a stone') does not require the actual existence of its subject. For example, 'No phoenix is a bird' is true even though phoenixes do not exist, because the subject term does not need to refer to anything real. However, for quantified negative propositions (e.g., 'All S are not P'), there is a nuance. 'Sheikh al-Maqtul' (Suhrawardi) argues that in quantified statements, the 'contract of position' (عقد الوضع) — the act of positing a subject term with quantification — requires that there be individuals of the subject class. So even a negative like 'No man is a stone' would require men to exist. But the author notes that this is disputed: others respond that 'عقد الوضع' is merely a restrictive composition and does not entail existence. However, a counter-objection is that the attributive relation (the predicate attributed to the subject) does call for the subject's existence. This debate highlights the importance of existential import in logic, particularly for universal negatives. The author leaves the issue unresolved, indicating a known point of contention.

Key Terms
عقد الوضع
سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) 'عقد الوضع' always entails the existence of individuals.

Key Takeaway
A universal negative proposition does not require the existence of its subject, but this rule is complicated by quantification and the contract of position.
